# Anuttarovavai Sutra — Chapter 3 (Varga 3): The Final Decad

**Translation by:** Dishant Shah
**Source:** Shri Anuttarovavai Sutra, Gujarati edition (Guru Pran Prakashan)
**Section:** Third Varga — Ten Souls Who Reached Sarvarthasiddha
**Total Adhyayanas:** 10
**Overview:** This Varga contains the most extensive narrative of the entire Anuttarovavai. Its first chapter, Dhanya Anagar's story, is the climactic teaching of the scripture: a young merchant prince of Kakandi who, after one sermon from Mahavira, renounces unimaginable wealth and undertakes the most severe austerity vow recorded in the canon — the nine-fold abhigraha — until his body is reduced to bone and his radiance, paradoxically, surpasses the sun. He ascends to Sarvarthasiddha, the highest of the five Anuttara realms reachable from human birth, with one human life remaining before final liberation in Mahavideha. The chapter then briefly covers Sunakshatra (Adhyayana 2) and the eight remaining souls — Isidasa, Pellaka, Ramaputta, Chandima, Pedhalaputta, Potilla, Padma, and Vehallaputta — who follow the same arc by reference (Adhyayanas 3–10).

---

## Adhyayana 1 — Dhanya Kumar

**Translation by:** Dishant Shah
**Source:** Shri Anuttarovavai Sutra, Gujarati edition (Guru Pran Prakashan)
**Section:** Third Varga — Ten Householders of Kakandi (Dhanya, Sunakshatra, Rishidasa, Pellaka, Ramaputra, Chandrima/Chandrika, Prashtika, Pedhalaputra, Poshtila, Vehalla)
**This file:** Part A — Dhanya Kumar's worldly life of abundance, leading up to his first hearing of Mahavira's sermon and the rising of dispassion.

**Overview:** The Third Varga of the Anuttaropapātika Daśā tells the lives of ten extraordinary householders of the city of Kakandi who, having heard Mahavira's teaching, renounced vast worldly wealth and entered the path of severe austerity. The first of these — and the most fully described — is Dhanya Kumar, a young man of staggering opulence whose mother Bhadra was a celebrated *sārthavāhī* (caravan-leader and merchant of immense fortune). Dhanya's life of plenty is described in unusual detail: thirty-two palaces, thirty-two wives, thirty-two of every conceivable luxury — all so the renunciation that follows can be felt with full weight. This narrative is the canonical model of "from the highest abundance to the deepest austerity."

This part covers the opening question, the description of Kakandi and Bhadra, Dhanya's birth and education, his marriage to thirty-two princesses, the arrival of Mahavira in the city, and the rising of dispassion in Dhanya's heart upon hearing the Lord's discourse.

---

### Dhanya's Worldly Life of Abundance

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S1 — Utkshepa (The Opening Question)

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

भंते ! समणेणं भगवया महावीरेणं जाव संपत्तेणं अणुत्तरोववाइयदसाणं दोच्चस्स वग्गस्स अयमट्ठे पण्णत्ते, तच्चस्स णं भंते ! वग्गस्स अणुत्तरोववाइयदसाणं समणेणं भगवया महावीरेणं जाव संपत्तेणं के अट्ठे पण्णत्ते ?

एवं खलु जंबू ! समणेणं भगवया महावीरेणं जाव संपत्तेणं अणुत्तरोववाइयदसाणं तच्चस्स वग्गस्स दस अज्झयणा पण्णत्ता । तं जहा—

धण्णे य सुणक्खत्ते य, इसिदासे अ आहिए ।
पेल्लए रामपुत्ते य, चंदिमा पिट्ठिमा इ य ।।

पेढालपुत्ते अणगारे, णवमे पोट्टिले वि य ।
वेहल्ले दसमे वुत्ते, इमे य दस आहिया ।।

जइ णं भंते ! समणेणं भगवया महावीरेणं जाव संपत्तेणं अणुत्तरोववाइयदसाणं तच्चस्स वग्गस्स दस अज्झयणा पण्णत्ता, पढमस्स णं भंते ! अज्झयणस्स समणेणं भगवया महावीरेणं जाव संपत्तेणं के अट्ठे पण्णत्ते ? ॥3.A1.S1॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

bhante ! samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ jāva saṃpatteṇaṃ aṇuttarovavāiyadasāṇaṃ doccassa vaggassa ayamaṭṭhe paṇṇatte, taccassa ṇaṃ bhante ! vaggassa aṇuttarovavāiyadasāṇaṃ samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ jāva saṃpatteṇaṃ ke aṭṭhe paṇṇatte ?

evaṃ khalu jambū ! samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ jāva saṃpatteṇaṃ aṇuttarovavāiyadasāṇaṃ taccassa vaggassa dasa ajjhayaṇā paṇṇattā | taṃ jahā—

dhaṇṇe ya suṇakkhatte ya, isidāse a āhie |
pellae rāmaputte ya, caṃdimā piṭṭhimā i ya ||

peḍhālaputte aṇagāre, ṇavame poṭṭile vi ya |
vehalle dasame vutte, ime ya dasa āhiyā ||

jai ṇaṃ bhante ! samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ jāva saṃpatteṇaṃ aṇuttarovavāiyadasāṇaṃ taccassa vaggassa dasa ajjhayaṇā paṇṇattā, paḍhamassa ṇaṃ bhante ! ajjhayaṇassa samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ jāva saṃpatteṇaṃ ke aṭṭhe paṇṇatte ? || 3.A1.S1 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

"O Lord! If the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, the attainer of liberation, has expounded the meaning of the second section of the Anuttaropapātika Daśā in this manner, then, O Lord, what meaning has the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, the attainer of liberation, expounded for the third section of the Anuttaropapātika Daśā?" "Truly so, O Jambu! The Ascetic Lord Mahavira, the attainer of liberation, has expounded ten chapters in the third section of the Anuttaropapātika Daśā. They are as follows: Dhanya and Sunakshatra, Rishidasa and Pellaka; Ramaputra and Chandrima, and Prashtika; the monk Pedhalaputra ninth, and Poshtila; Vehalla is told as the tenth — these ten are spoken of." Then Jambu asked: "If, O Lord, the third section has ten chapters, then, O Lord, what meaning has the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, the attainer of liberation, expounded for the first chapter?"

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*bhante* = O venerable Lord | *samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ* = by the Ascetic Lord Mahavira | *jāva saṃpatteṇaṃ* = the attainer of liberation (canonical epithet) | *aṇuttarovavāiyadasāṇaṃ* = of the Anuttaropapātika Daśā | *doccassa vaggassa* = of the second section | *ayamaṭṭhe paṇṇatte* = this meaning has been expounded | *taccassa vaggassa* = of the third section | *ke aṭṭhe paṇṇatte* = what meaning has been expounded? | *evaṃ khalu jambū* = "Truly so, O Jambu" | *dasa ajjhayaṇā paṇṇattā* = ten chapters have been declared | *taṃ jahā* = namely, as follows | *dhaṇṇe* = Dhanya ("the blessed, fortunate one") | *suṇakkhatte* = Sunakshatra ("of auspicious star") | *isidāse* = Rishidasa ("servant of the seers") | *pellae* = Pellaka | *rāmaputte* = Ramaputra ("son of Rama") | *caṃdimā* = Chandrima (also read as Chandrika in the Gujarati gloss) | *piṭṭhimā* = Prashtika | *peḍhālaputte aṇagāre* = the homeless monk Pedhalaputra | *ṇavame* = ninth | *poṭṭile* = Poshtila | *vehalle dasame vutte* = Vehalla is told as the tenth | *ime ya dasa āhiyā* = these ten are spoken of | *paḍhamassa ajjhayaṇassa* = of the first chapter | *ke aṭṭhe paṇṇatte* = what meaning has been expounded?

**5. COMMENTARY**

This opening sutra is the formal hinge between the second and third sections of the scripture. The disciple Jambu has just received the meaning of the second section from his teacher Sudharma; with the natural rhythm of a serious student, he now asks the next question — what does the third section teach? The dialogue form is more than a literary frame here; it is the Jain conviction made visible that sacred knowledge is always handed down person to person, question to answer, never poured out as a monologue.

Sudharma's reply names the ten chapters in a small mnemonic verse — two short rhythmic couplets that make the names easy to memorize. This is one of the rare moments the canon breaks into verse, and the compilers preserved it precisely because it allowed students to hold all ten names at a single hearing. The names themselves carry meaning: *Dhanya* means "the blessed" or "the fortunate one" — and the irony of the name will become clear, because Dhanya is fortunate not in the worldly wealth he begins with but in the spiritual fortune that lets him let it all go. *Sunakshatra* means "the one born under an auspicious star." *Rishidasa* means "servant of the sages." *Ramaputra* means "son of Rama." Each name is a small portrait.

A note on the architecture of the entire scripture: the Anuttaropapātika Daśā is divided into three sections (*vargas*) for a total of thirty-three souls. The first section gave the ten princes of Rajagriha — sons of King Shrenika. The second section gave the next thirteen souls. This third section gives the final ten — these are not princes of Rajagriha but householders of Kakandi, a different city, a different social class, a different setting. Yet the inner story is the same: extraordinary worldly station, decisive renunciation upon hearing Mahavira, severe austerity, and rebirth in one of the highest heavens with liberation now within one human birth's reach.

The repeated phrase *jāva saṃpatteṇaṃ* — "the attainer of liberation" — is, as in earlier chapters, theological precision rather than ornament. It seals every answer with the authority of a fully liberated being's words. What follows is not Sudharma's interpretation; it is Mahavira's own teaching, transmitted intact.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Jambu asks his teacher Sudharma: "You just told me what the second part of this scripture teaches — now please tell me what the third part teaches." Sudharma names the ten people whose stories make up the third part — beginning with Dhanya — and Jambu then asks, "What does the first chapter, the story of Dhanya, actually teach?"

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When you receive a body of teaching, do you let yourself be content with one section, or does something in you keep reaching for what comes next?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S2 — The City of Kakandi and the Mother Bhadra

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

एवं खलु जंबू ! तेणं कालेणं तेणं समएणं काकंदी णामं णयरी होत्था, रिद्धित्थिमियसमिद्धा । सहस्संबवणे उज्जाणे सव्वोउय पुष्फ-फल-समिद्धे । जियसत्तू राया ।

तत्थ णं काकंदीए णयरीए भद्दा णामं सत्थवाही परिवसइ । अड्ढा दित्ता वित्ता वित्थिण्ण-विउल-भवण-सयणासण-जाणवाहणा बहुधण जायरूव-रयया आओग-पओग-संपउत्ता विच्छड्डिय-पउर-भत्तपाणा बहुदासी-दास-गो-महिस-गवेलग प्पभूया बहुजणस्स अपरिभूया । ॥3.A1.S2॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

evaṃ khalu jambū ! teṇaṃ kāleṇaṃ teṇaṃ samaeṇaṃ kākaṃdī ṇāmaṃ ṇayarī hotthā, riddhitthimiyasamiddhā | sahassaṃbavaṇe ujjāṇe savvouya pussa-phala-samiddhe | jiyasattū rāyā |

tattha ṇaṃ kākaṃdīe ṇayarīe bhaddā ṇāmaṃ satthavāhī parivasai | aḍḍhā dittā vittā vitthiṇṇa-viula-bhavaṇa-sayaṇāsaṇa-jāṇavāhaṇā bahudhaṇa jāyarūva-rayayā āoga-paoga-saṃpautta vicchaḍḍiya-paura-bhattapāṇā bahudāsī-dāsa-go-mahisa-gavelaga ppabhūyā bahujaṇassa aparibhūyā | || 3.A1.S2 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

"Truly so, O Jambu! In that age, at that time, there was a city named Kakandi — prosperous, stable, and abundantly endowed. Outside it lay the garden called Sahasramravana ("Grove of a Thousand Mangoes"), rich in the fruits and flowers of every season. The king was named Jitashatru. In that city of Kakandi there dwelt a caravan-leading merchantess named Bhadra. She was wealthy and resplendent, possessed of vast and spacious mansions, beds, seats, conveyances, and vehicles; abundant in wealth, gold, and silver; engaged in lending and trading; one in whose home, even after meals had been served and shared, much food and drink still remained left over; rich in maidservants, manservants, cattle, buffaloes, and flocks; and not to be slighted by anyone — held in high regard by the multitude."

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*evaṃ khalu jambū* = "Truly so, O Jambu" | *teṇaṃ kāleṇaṃ teṇaṃ samaeṇaṃ* = in that age, at that time (the canonical opening formula) | *kākaṃdī ṇāmaṃ ṇayarī hotthā* = there was a city named Kakandi | *riddhitthimiyasamiddhā* = prosperous, stable, abundantly endowed (a triple formula: rich in resources, secure in governance, abundant in well-being) | *sahassaṃbavaṇe ujjāṇe* = the garden called Sahasramravana ("Grove of a Thousand Mangoes") | *savvouya pussa-phala-samiddhe* = rich in flowers and fruits of every season | *jiyasattū rāyā* = the king was Jitashatru ("Conqueror of Foes") | *bhaddā ṇāmaṃ satthavāhī* = a caravan-leader/merchantess named Bhadra ("the auspicious one") | *parivasai* = dwelled, resided | *aḍḍhā* = wealthy | *dittā* = resplendent, brilliant | *vittā* = of great fortune | *vitthiṇṇa-viula-bhavaṇa* = vast and spacious mansions | *sayaṇāsaṇa* = beds and seats | *jāṇavāhaṇā* = conveyances and vehicles | *bahudhaṇa* = of abundant wealth | *jāyarūva-rayayā* = (possessed) of gold and silver | *āoga-paoga-saṃpautta* = engaged in lending and trading (i.e., active in financial enterprise) | *vicchaḍḍiya-paura-bhattapāṇā* = abundant food and drink remaining even after distribution | *bahudāsī-dāsa* = many maidservants and manservants | *go-mahisa-gavelaga* = cattle, buffaloes, and flocks of sheep/goats | *ppabhūyā* = abundantly possessed of | *bahujaṇassa aparibhūyā* = not to be slighted by the multitude — held in respect by all

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra opens the story of Dhanya by first painting the world he is born into. The city of Kakandi was a prosperous urban centre — described, like every great Jain city, with the threefold formula "prosperous, stable, abundantly endowed." Three precise things are being claimed: the city had abundant material resources, it had stable governance, and it had general well-being among its people. Outside its walls lay a garden famous in the canon — the Sahasramravana, "the Grove of a Thousand Mangoes," a name that suggests both physical abundance and the kind of place where seekers and sages would gather. The king is Jitashatru, a name that means "the one who has conquered his enemies" — a stock name for kings in Jain narrative.

What follows is more striking. The scripture introduces not Dhanya's father but his mother, Bhadra, and devotes the entire description of the household to her. She is not described as the wife of a wealthy merchant; she is herself a *sārthavāhī* — a caravan-leader, a merchant in her own right. The Gujarati commentary draws careful attention to this point: in this period, women in business were not exceptional. Bhadra ran a money-lending and trading enterprise; she owned vast mansions, lent capital, and managed extensive cattle and a large household of attendants. She was, in short, an independent businesswoman of immense fortune.

The detail that "even after meals had been served, abundant food and drink remained" is a canonical signal of true wealth — wealth that is never strained, that always has more than enough to give. The closing phrase "not to be slighted by anyone" carries social weight: in a society that often pressured single mothers or female heads of household, Bhadra commanded full respect. She was a woman whose station no one dared diminish.

A subtle but important silence runs through this sutra: Dhanya's father is not named, and is not described anywhere in the chapter. The Gujarati commentary observes this silence directly and interprets it: it is likely that Dhanya's father died when Dhanya was very young, and that Bhadra herself bore the entire weight of raising him, arranging his education, his marriage, and eventually walking with him through his renunciation. The scripture's choice to give every description of the household in Bhadra's name reflects this reality. The entire arc of Dhanya's life — birth, abundance, renunciation, sallekhana — unfolds in the orbit of his mother.

This silence also gives the chapter a quiet feminine power. The mother who has built everything will, in a few sutras' time, be the one whose grief and final blessing release her son into the path of liberation. The whole drama of attachment and release runs through her.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

In a great prosperous city called Kakandi, ruled by a king named Jitashatru, there lived a wealthy businesswoman named Bhadra — Dhanya's mother — who was a caravan-trader and money-lender so rich that even after every meal there was always food left over, and so respected that no one in the city dared slight her.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

What inheritance — material, moral, or spiritual — has shaped you most? And what part of who you are today began in someone else's strength?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S3 — The Birth and Education of Dhanya Kumar

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तीसे णं भद्दाए सत्थवाहीए पुत्ते धण्णे णामं दारए होत्था । अहीणपंचिंदियसरीरे लक्खण-वंजण-गुणोववए एमाणुम्माणपमाणपडिपुण्ण सुजायसव्वंग सुंदरंगे ससिसोमाकारे कंते पियदंसणे सुरूवे पंचधाईपरिगहिए, जहा महब्बलो तहा धण्णं कुमारं अम्मापियरो सातिरेगट्ठवासजायं सोहणंसि तिहिकरणणक्खत्त मुहुत्तंसि कलायरियस्स उवणेंति । तए णं से कलायरिए धण्णं कुमारं लेहाइयाओ गणियप्पहाणाओ सउणरुतपज्जवसाणाओ बावत्तरिं कलाओ सुत्तओ य अत्थओ य करणओ य सेहावेइ, सिक्खावेइ । तं जहा—

(1) लेहं (2) गणियं (3) रूवं (4) णट्टं (5) गीयं (6) वाइयं (7) सरगयं (8) पोक्खरगयं (9) समताल (10) जूयं (11) जणवायं (12) पासयं (13) अट्ठावयं (14) पोरेकच्चं (15) दगमट्टियं (16) अण्णविहिं (17) पाणविहिं (18) वत्थविहिं (19) विलेवणविहिं (20) सयणविहिं (21) अज्ज (22) पहेलियं (23) मागहियं (24) गाहं (25) गीयं (26) सिलोयं (27) हिरण्णजुत्तिं (28) सुवण्णजुत्तिं (29) चुण्णजुत्तिं (30) आभरणविहिं (31) तरुणीपडिकम्मं (32) इत्थिलक्खणं (33) पुरिस लक्खणं (34) हयलक्खणं (35) गयलक्खणं (36) गोणलक्खणं (37) कुक्कुडलक्खणं (38) छत्तलक्खणं (39) दंडलक्खणं (40) असिलक्खणं (41) मणिलक्खणं (42) कागणिलक्खणं (43) वत्थुविज्जं (44) खंधारमाणं (45) णगरमाणं (46) वूहं (47) पडिवूहं (48) चारं (49) पडिचारं (50) चक्कवूहं (51) गरुलवूहं (52) सगडवूहं (53) जुद्धं (54) णिजुद्धं (55) जुद्धातिजुद्धं (56) अट्ठिजुद्धं (57) मुट्ठिजुद्धं (58) बाहुजुद्धं (59) लयाजुद्धं (60) ईसत्थं (61) छरुप्पवायं (62) धणुव्वेयं (63) हिरण्णपागं (64) सुवण्णपागं (65) सुत्तखेडं (66) वट्टखेडं (67) णालियाखेडं (68) पत्तच्छेज्जं (69) कडगच्छेज्जं (70) सजीवं (71) णिज्जीवं (72) सउणरुतं । ॥3.A1.S3॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tīse ṇaṃ bhaddāe satthavāhīe putte dhaṇṇe ṇāmaṃ dārae hotthā | ahīṇapaṃciṃdiyasarīre lakkhaṇa-vaṃjaṇa-guṇovavae emāṇummāṇapamāṇapaḍipuṇṇa sujāyasavvaṃga suṃdaraṃge sasisomākāre kaṃte piyadaṃsaṇe surūve paṃcadhāīparigahie, jahā mahabbalo tahā dhaṇṇaṃ kumāraṃ ammāpiyaro sātiregaṭṭhavāsajāyaṃ sohaṇaṃsi tihikaraṇaṇakkhatta muhuttaṃsi kalāyariyassa uvaṇeṃti | tae ṇaṃ se kalāyarie dhaṇṇaṃ kumāraṃ lehāiyāo gaṇiyappahāṇāo sauṇarutapajjavasāṇāo bāvattariṃ kalāo suttao ya atthao ya karaṇao ya sehāvei, sikkhāvei | taṃ jahā—

(1) lehaṃ (2) gaṇiyaṃ (3) rūvaṃ (4) ṇaṭṭaṃ (5) gīyaṃ (6) vāiyaṃ (7) saragayaṃ (8) pokkharagayaṃ (9) samatāla (10) jūyaṃ (11) jaṇavāyaṃ (12) pāsayaṃ (13) aṭṭhāvayaṃ (14) porekaccaṃ (15) dagamaṭṭiyaṃ (16) aṇṇavihiṃ (17) pāṇavihiṃ (18) vatthavihiṃ (19) vilevaṇavihiṃ (20) sayaṇavihiṃ (21) ajja (22) pheliyaṃ (23) māgahiyaṃ (24) gāhaṃ (25) gīyaṃ (26) siloyaṃ (27) hiraṇṇajuttiṃ (28) suvaṇṇajuttiṃ (29) cuṇṇajuttiṃ (30) ābharaṇavihiṃ (31) taruṇīpaḍikammaṃ (32) itthilakkhaṇaṃ (33) purisa lakkhaṇaṃ (34) hayalakkhaṇaṃ (35) gayalakkhaṇaṃ (36) goṇalakkhaṇaṃ (37) kukkuḍalakkhaṇaṃ (38) chattalakkhaṇaṃ (39) daṃḍalakkhaṇaṃ (40) asilakkhaṇaṃ (41) maṇilakkhaṇaṃ (42) kāgaṇilakkhaṇaṃ (43) vatthuvijjaṃ (44) khaṃdhāramāṇaṃ (45) ṇagaramāṇaṃ (46) vūhaṃ (47) paḍivūhaṃ (48) cāraṃ (49) paḍicāraṃ (50) cakkavūhaṃ (51) garulavūhaṃ (52) sagaḍavūhaṃ (53) juddhaṃ (54) ṇijuddhaṃ (55) juddhātijuddhaṃ (56) aṭṭhijuddhaṃ (57) muṭṭhijuddhaṃ (58) bāhujuddhaṃ (59) layājuddhaṃ (60) īsatthaṃ (61) charuppavāyaṃ (62) dhaṇuvveyaṃ (63) hiraṇṇapāgaṃ (64) suvaṇṇapāgaṃ (65) suttakheḍaṃ (66) vaṭṭakheḍaṃ (67) ṇāliyākheḍaṃ (68) pattacchejjaṃ (69) kaḍagacchejjaṃ (70) sajīvaṃ (71) ṇijjīvaṃ (72) sauṇarutaṃ | || 3.A1.S3 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

To that caravan-leading merchantess Bhadra was born a son named Dhanya. He was complete in all five senses and entire in body, endowed with auspicious marks, beauty-spots, and inner virtues; perfect in measure, weight, and proportion; finely formed in every limb, beautifully shaped, with the gentle radiance of the moon; lovely, of pleasing appearance, of fine form. He was raised by five wet-nurses. Just as in the case of Mahabala, so also Dhanya — when he had passed eight years of age — was, on an auspicious day chosen by tithi, karana, nakshatra, and muhurta, taken by his mother to a master of the arts. Then that master taught and trained Dhanya Kumar in the seventy-two arts — beginning with writing, with arithmetic as the chief, and ending with the language of bird-cries — instructing him in their texts, their meanings, and their practical application. Namely: (1) writing, (2) arithmetic, (3) shape-changing, (4) drama, (5) singing, (6) playing instruments, (7) recognizing musical notes, (8) playing percussion, (9) keeping equal rhythm, (10) gambling, (11) public dialogue, (12) dice, (13) chess (or "ashtapada"), (14) chivalrous conduct, (15) shaping objects from water-and-clay (pottery), (16) the art of foods, (17) the art of drinks, (18) the art of clothing, (19) the art of unguents, (20) the art of beds, (21) Aryan metres, (22) riddle-making, (23) Magadhi-language poetry, (24) gathas (verses), (25) songs, (26) shlokas, (27) the working of silver, (28) the working of gold, (29) the working of fragrant powders, (30) the art of ornaments, (31) the adornment of women, (32) reading the marks of women, (33) reading the marks of men, (34) reading the marks of horses, (35) reading the marks of elephants, (36) reading the marks of cattle, (37) reading the marks of cocks, (38) reading the marks of parasols, (39) reading the marks of staves, (40) reading the marks of swords, (41) reading the marks of jewels, (42) reading the marks of small coins, (43) the science of architecture, (44) the measure of military encampments, (45) the measure of cities, (46) battle-array (vyuha), (47) counter-array, (48) reconnaissance, (49) counter-reconnaissance, (50) the wheel-array, (51) the eagle-array, (52) the wagon-array, (53) ordinary warfare, (54) special warfare, (55) extra-special warfare, (56) staff-fighting, (57) fist-fighting, (58) wrestling, (59) creeper-fighting (a grappling style), (60) archery, (61) sword-craft, (62) the science of the bow, (63) the cooking/tempering of silver, (64) the cooking/tempering of gold, (65) thread-play, (66) ball-play, (67) tube-play (a kind of game), (68) leaf-cutting, (69) bracelet-cutting, (70) reviving the dead-seeming, (71) rendering the living dead-seeming, and (72) understanding the cries of birds.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tīse ṇaṃ bhaddāe satthavāhīe* = of that merchantess Bhadra | *putte dhaṇṇe ṇāmaṃ dārae hotthā* = there was a son named Dhanya, a young child | *ahīṇa-paṃciṃdiya-sarīre* = with all five senses unimpaired and a sound body | *lakkhaṇa-vaṃjaṇa-guṇovavae* = endowed with auspicious bodily marks, beauty-marks (moles, etc.), and inner virtues | *emāṇummāṇa-pamāṇa-paḍipuṇṇa* = perfect in measure, weight, and proportion | *sujāya-savvaṃga* = beautifully formed in every limb | *suṃdaraṃge* = of beautiful body | *sasisomākāre* = of moon-like gentle radiance | *kaṃte* = lovely, dear-to-look-upon | *piyadaṃsaṇe* = of pleasing appearance | *surūve* = of fine form | *paṃcadhāī-parigahie* = raised by five wet-nurses (a canonical formula: nurses for feeding, bathing, dressing, playing, and lap-holding) | *jahā mahabbalo* = "just as Mahabala" — a canonical reference-marker pointing to the parallel story of Mahabala in the Bhagavati Sutra (and also Megha Kumar in the Jnata Dharma Katha), whose education-passage applies here | *sātirega-aṭṭha-vāsa-jāyaṃ* = having passed eight years of age (literally: "born more than eight years") | *sohaṇaṃsi tihi-karaṇa-ṇakkhatta-muhuttaṃsi* = on an auspicious day, in an auspicious *tithi* (lunar day), *karaṇa* (half-day division), *nakshatra* (lunar mansion), and *muhurta* (period of time) | *kalāyariyassa uvaṇeṃti* = (the parents) take him to a master of the arts | *bāvattariṃ kalāo* = the seventy-two arts | *suttao ya atthao ya karaṇao ya* = in their texts, their meanings, and their practical application | *sehāvei, sikkhāvei* = he trained him, he taught him | *lehaṃ* = writing | *gaṇiyaṃ* = arithmetic | *rūvaṃ* = shape-changing, sculpting | *ṇaṭṭaṃ* = drama, dance | *gīyaṃ* = singing | *vāiyaṃ* = playing instruments | *saragayaṃ* = recognizing musical notes | *pokkharagayaṃ* = playing percussion (drum) | *samatāla* = keeping rhythm | *jūyaṃ* = gambling | *jaṇavāyaṃ* = public dialogue, debate | *pāsayaṃ* = dice-play | *aṭṭhāvayaṃ* = the eight-square board game (chess-like *ashtapada*) | *porekaccaṃ* = chivalrous social etiquette | *dagamaṭṭiyaṃ* = water-and-clay craft (pottery) | *aṇṇavihiṃ* = the art of foods | *pāṇavihiṃ* = the art of drinks | *vatthavihiṃ* = the art of clothing | *vilevaṇavihiṃ* = the art of unguents/perfumes | *sayaṇavihiṃ* = the art of beds and bedding | *ajja* = Aryan metres ⚠️ (the Gujarati gloss reads this as Aryan-metre composition) | *pheliyaṃ* = riddles | *māgahiyaṃ* = poetry in the Magadhi language | *gāhaṃ* = the *gatha* metre, Prakrit verse | *gīyaṃ* = song | *siloyaṃ* = the *shloka* metre | *hiraṇṇa/suvaṇṇa/cuṇṇa-juttiṃ* = the working of silver, gold, and aromatic powders | *ābharaṇavihiṃ* = the art of ornaments | *taruṇīpaḍikammaṃ* = the adornment of young women | *itthi/purisa/haya/gaya/goṇa/kukkuḍa lakkhaṇaṃ* = reading the bodily marks of women, men, horses, elephants, cattle, and roosters | *chatta/daṃḍa/asi/maṇi/kāgaṇi lakkhaṇaṃ* = reading the marks of parasols, staves, swords, jewels, and small coins | *vatthuvijjaṃ* = the science of architecture and dwelling-construction | *khaṃdhāramāṇaṃ* = the measure (planning) of military camps | *ṇagaramāṇaṃ* = the planning of cities | *vūhaṃ / paḍivūhaṃ* = battle-formation and counter-formation | *cāraṃ / paḍicāraṃ* = reconnaissance and counter-reconnaissance | *cakkavūhaṃ / garulavūhaṃ / sagaḍavūhaṃ* = the wheel-array, eagle-array, and wagon-array (named battle formations) | *juddhaṃ / ṇijuddhaṃ / juddhātijuddhaṃ* = ordinary, special, and extreme warfare | *aṭṭhi/muṭṭhi/bāhu/layā juddhaṃ* = staff-fighting, fist-fighting, wrestling, and creeper-fighting (named martial styles) | *īsatthaṃ* = archery | *charuppavāyaṃ* = sword-craft | *dhaṇuvveyaṃ* = the science of the bow | *hiraṇṇa/suvaṇṇa-pāgaṃ* = the tempering/refining of silver and gold | *sutta/vaṭṭa/ṇāliyā kheḍaṃ* = thread-play, ball-play, tube-play (named children's and skill games) | *pattacchejjaṃ* = leaf-cutting (precise cutting designs) | *kaḍagacchejjaṃ* = bracelet-cutting | *sajīvaṃ* = making the dead seem alive | *ṇijjīvaṃ* = making the living seem dead | *sauṇarutaṃ* = understanding the cries of birds

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra is, on its surface, a description of a wealthy boy's education. Beneath the surface it is a careful theological setup. The scripture is showing that Dhanya was not given up to spiritual life out of any lack — he was raised in fullness, blessed in body, in fortune, in skill. Every advantage that worldly life can offer was given to him. What he renounces, then, is not a life of nothing; it is a life of everything.

The opening physical description follows a canonical formula. Each phrase has a precise meaning. He was *complete in all five senses* — none impaired, none weak. He carried *auspicious marks* — the *lakshana* are the major bodily signs (the mark of a wheel on the foot, balanced shoulders, even teeth) which Indian tradition reads as evidence of noble destiny, while the *vyanjana* are smaller beauty-marks like moles. He was *perfect in measure, weight, and proportion* — three technical aesthetic categories: his height and width were balanced, his physical mass was right for his frame, and his proportions matched the canonical ideal. He had the gentle radiance of the moon. The whole portrait is the canonical image of a noble being — every external sign in alignment with inner worth.

He was raised by *five wet-nurses*. This is a stock phrase in the canon. The five nurses each had a specialized role: one for feeding, one for bathing, one for dressing, one for playing with the child, and one for holding him in her lap. This was the standard royal-household upbringing, applied here to a merchantess's son — confirming that Bhadra's wealth was on the scale of royalty.

The phrase "just as Mahabala" is a canonical reference-marker. Mahabala's story is told in detail in the Bhagavati Sutra; this scripture instructs the reader to apply Mahabala's full childhood and education narrative to Dhanya rather than repeat it line by line. It is the same literary device used in earlier chapters with reference to Megha Kumar.

When Dhanya passed eight years of age — the traditional age for beginning formal study — Bhadra waited for an auspicious moment and took him to a master of the arts. The astrological precision is notable: the moment was chosen by *tithi*, *karana*, *nakshatra*, and *muhurta* — four overlapping units of time used in traditional astrology to identify the most favorable instant. This is not superstition in the canon's framing; it is the principle that important beginnings deserve aligned beginnings.

The list of seventy-two arts is one of the most famous educational catalogues in Indian literature. It defines a complete classical education. The arts include literacy (writing, arithmetic), the fine arts (drama, music, singing, instrument-playing, sculpture), poetry in multiple metres and languages, crafts (pottery, gold-work, silver-work, clothing, perfumery), domestic arts (foods, drinks, bedding), the arts of social refinement (etiquette, ornament, female adornment), the sciences of reading bodily signs (of women, men, animals, weapons, jewels), architecture and city-planning, the entire range of military arts (battle-formations, reconnaissance, formations named after wheel and eagle and wagon, named martial styles from staff-fighting to wrestling), arts of metalwork and refinement, several skill-games, fine-precision crafts (leaf-cutting, bracelet-cutting), and at the very edge — strange and esoteric arts: making the dead seem alive, making the living seem dead, and understanding the cries of birds. The last one is a culmination — the *sauṇaruta* art of decoding bird-language was considered an apex skill, often associated with sages and adepts.

Dhanya was taught all seventy-two — and not superficially. The phrase "in their texts, their meanings, and their practical application" makes the depth explicit. He learned each art three times over: as a body of written knowledge, as understanding of meaning, and as practical capability. By the end of his training he was a fully accomplished young man — literate, refined, physically skilled, militarily capable, artistically cultured.

The Gujarati commentary adds a beautiful synthesis: this education was meant not only to make Dhanya capable in the world but to make his eventual renunciation more meaningful. A man who renounces ignorance has renounced little; a man who renounces complete worldly mastery has renounced everything. Dhanya's seventy-two arts are not boast — they are weight. When he later walks away from all of it, the weight of what he leaves behind is the weight of this list.

⚠️ The Prakrit list of seventy-two arts contains some spellings (e.g. *pheliyaṃ* for *prahelika*, *ajja* possibly for *ārya* metre) where the Gujarati commentary's expansion has been used to interpret the term. Some entries (e.g. *sajīvaṃ* / *ṇijjīvaṃ*) have been read in the canonical sense given by the commentary ("making the dead seem alive / making the living seem dead"), which describes a particular skill of stage-craft or illusion. The numbering and order follow the PDF exactly.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Bhadra had a son named Dhanya — beautiful, healthy, perfectly formed, full of every good quality. She raised him with five nurses, and at the age of eight, on an auspicious day, she took him to a master who taught him all seventy-two arts a young noble was expected to know — from writing and arithmetic, to music and dance, to military skills and architecture, to crafts so fine and rare that the last one on the list was understanding what birds are saying.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

Look honestly at all the skills, capacities, and accomplishments you have gathered. If one day a deeper call came, would the weight of what you have built make the leaving harder — or would it become the very thing whose release sets you free?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S4 — Dhanya's Maturity and Mastery

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं धण्णे कुमारे बावत्तरिकलापंडिए णवंगसुत्तपडिबोहिए अट्ठारसविह-देसिप्पगारभासाविसारए गीयरई गंधव्व णट्टकुसले हयजोही गयजोही रहजोही बाहुजोही बाहुप्पमद्दी अलं भोगसमत्थे जाए यावि होत्था । ॥3.A1.S4॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ dhaṇṇe kumāre bāvattarikalāpaṃḍie ṇavaṃgasuttapaḍibohie aṭṭhārasaviha-desippagārabhāsāvisārae gīyaraī gaṃdhavva ṇaṭṭakusale hayajohī gayajohī rahajohī bāhujohī bāhuppamaddī alaṃ bhogasamatthe jāe yāvi hotthā | || 3.A1.S4 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then Dhanya Kumar became a master of the seventy-two arts, awakened in the nine bodily organs (which had lain dormant in childhood), expert in the eighteen kinds of regional dialects, devoted to song and rhythm, skilled in heavenly music and drama, a warrior on horseback, a warrior on elephant, a warrior in chariot, a warrior with his arms; one who could crush opponents by the might of his arms; fully fit for the enjoyments of life — such he became, having entered into the prime of youth.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ* = thereupon, then | *dhaṇṇe kumāre* = Dhanya Kumar | *bāvattari-kalā-paṃḍie* = a master of the seventy-two arts | *ṇavaṃga-sutta-paḍibohie* = (with) the nine bodily organs awakened from their childhood dormancy — the canonical formula refers to two ears, two eyes, two nostrils, the tongue, the skin, and the mind, all of which only fully mature in adolescence | *aṭṭhārasaviha-desippagāra-bhāsā-visārae* = expert in the eighteen kinds of regional/desi dialects | *gīyaraī* = devoted to song, fond of music | *gaṃdhavva* = (skilled in) gandharva music — the heavenly art of song | *ṇaṭṭa-kusale* = skilled in dance and drama | *haya-johī* = a warrior on horseback | *gaya-johī* = a warrior on elephant | *raha-johī* = a warrior in a chariot | *bāhu-johī* = a warrior with his arms (hand-to-hand combat) | *bāhuppamaddī* = able to crush an opponent with the strength of his arms | *alaṃ bhoga-samatthe* = fully fit for the enjoyments of life | *jāe yāvi hotthā* = (such) he became — i.e., he entered the prime of youth

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra completes the portrait of Dhanya as a young man at the peak of his powers. Where the previous sutra described his education, this one describes the man that education produced. Every line is a fresh credential.

The phrase about the *nine bodily organs awakening* is canonical shorthand for the maturation of adolescence. In Jain anatomy the nine organs are: two ears, two eyes, two nostrils, the tongue, the skin, and the mind. In childhood, these are present but their full capacities lie dormant; in youth, they awaken into their mature operation. The scripture is using a precise formula to say simply: he passed through puberty and into full adulthood.

He became expert in the *eighteen kinds of regional dialects*. This is a remarkable detail. It means Dhanya could communicate fluently across the entire linguistic range of the Indian subcontinent — the languages of merchants, of diplomats, of distant cities. For the son of a caravan-leading merchantess this skill carries trade-significance: he could deal with people from anywhere. But the canonical formula is also showing his refinement and worldly competence at the highest level. He was at home everywhere.

He was *devoted to song and rhythm* and *skilled in gandharva music and drama*. The *gandharvas* in Indian mythology are the celestial musicians; to be "skilled in gandharva music" is to play and sing at a level that approaches the divine. Dhanya was not merely a connoisseur of the arts; he was a practitioner of refined and elevated cultural forms.

He was a fourfold warrior — *on horseback, on elephant, in chariot, with his arms*. These are the four classical battle-modes of ancient India. To master one was honorable; to master all four was extraordinary. And he was *bahuppamardi* — strong enough in his arms alone to crush an opponent. He was, in short, physically formidable.

The closing phrase — *alaṃ bhoga-samatthe* — translates literally as "fully capable for enjoyment." It is the canonical way of saying that he had reached the age and condition of life in which the full pleasures of the world were now within his reach. He was ready for marriage, for household life, for the entire arc of worldly existence.

The Gujarati commentary, after rehearsing all these accomplishments, gives a quiet observation: Dhanya was born in a wealthy household, his body was complete and beautiful, he was raised by five nurses like a prince, and his parents had ensured his education in all seventy-two arts. The point of so dwelling on this perfection is to make the renunciation that follows feel like the giving up of the highest point of worldly achievement. Spiritual life is sometimes pictured as a path for those who lack worldly success. The canon refuses this picture. The greatest renunciants in Jain tradition are precisely those who walked away at the peak of their power, with everything in hand. Dhanya stands here at that peak.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Dhanya grew up to be a master of all seventy-two arts, fluent in eighteen regional languages, an accomplished singer and dancer, a fearsome warrior on horseback, on elephant, in chariot, and in hand-to-hand combat — strong enough to crush an opponent with his bare arms — and now in the prime of youth, fully ready for the enjoyments of adult life.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When you stand at the peak of any worldly capacity — physical, intellectual, social — does that height feel like the destination, or does it begin to feel like a question you have not yet answered?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S5 — The Building of the Thirty-Two Palaces

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं सा भद्दा सत्थवाही धण्णं दारयं उम्मुक्कबालभावं विण्णाय परिणयमित्तं जोव्वणगमणुपत्तं बावत्तरिकलापंडियं णवंगसुत्तपडिबोहयं अट्ठारसविहदेसिप्पगार भासाविसारयं गीयरई गंधव्व-णट्ट-कुसलं सिंगारागार चारवेसं संगयगय-हसिय- भणिय-चिट्ठिय-विलास-णिउण्णजुत्तोवयारकुसलं हयजोहिं गयजोहिं रहजोहिं बाहुजोहिं बाहुप्पमद्दिं अलं भोगसमत्थं यावि जाणित्ता, बत्तीसं पासाय वडिंसए कारेइ, अब्भुगयमूसिए पहसिए विव मणिकणगरयणभत्तिचित्ते वाउद्धूयविजय वेजयंतिपडागाछत्ताइच्छत्तकलिए, तुंगे, गगणतलमभिलंघमाणसिहरे, जालंतर रयण पंजरुम्मिल्लियव्व मणिकणगथूभियाए, वियसिय सयपत्तपुंडरीए तिलय रयणद्ध- चंदच्चिए णाणामणिमयदामालंकिए, अंतो बहिं च सण्हे तवणिज्ज रुइल वालुयापत्थडे, सुहफासे ससिरीयरूवे पासादीए जाव पडिरूवे । ॥3.A1.S5॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ sā bhaddā satthavāhī dhaṇṇaṃ dārayaṃ ummukkabālabhāvaṃ viṇṇāya pariṇayamittaṃ jovvaṇagamaṇupattaṃ bāvattarikalāpaṃḍiyaṃ ṇavaṃgasuttapaḍibohayaṃ aṭṭhārasaviha-desippagāra bhāsāvisārayaṃ gīyaraī gaṃdhavva-ṇaṭṭa-kusalaṃ siṃgārāgāra cāravesaṃ saṃgayagaya-hasiya-bhaṇiya-ciṭṭhiya-vilāsa-ṇiuṇṇa-juttovayāra-kusalaṃ hayajohiṃ gayajohiṃ rahajohiṃ bāhujohiṃ bāhuppamaddiṃ alaṃ bhogasamatthaṃ yāvi jāṇittā, battīsaṃ pāsāya vaḍiṃsae kārei, abbhugayamūsie pahasie viva maṇikaṇagarayaṇabhatticitte vāuddhūyavijaya vejayaṃtipaḍāgācchattāicchattakalie, tuṃge, gagaṇatalamabhilaṃghamāṇasihare, jālaṃtara rayaṇa paṃjarummilliyavva maṇikaṇagathūbhiyāe, viyasiya sayapattapuṃḍarīe tilaya rayaṇaddha-caṃdaccie ṇāṇāmaṇimayadāmālaṃkie, aṃto bahiṃ ca saṇhe tavaṇijja ruila vāluyāpatthaḍe, suhaphāse sasirīyarūve pāsādīe jāva paḍirūve | || 3.A1.S5 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then the merchantess Bhadra, knowing that her son Dhanya had passed beyond childhood and just attained mature youth — that he was a master of the seventy-two arts, awakened in the nine bodily organs, expert in the eighteen kinds of regional dialects, devoted to song and rhythm, skilled in heavenly music and drama, of charming dress and the bearing of the abode of beauty itself, accomplished in graceful gait, smile, speech, posture, and gesture, refined in every appropriate courtesy, a warrior on horseback, on elephant, in chariot, and with his arms, capable of crushing opponents by his strength, and fully fit for enjoyments — caused thirty-two excellent palaces to be built for him. The palaces were lofty and tall; bright as if smiling; ornamented with paintings of jewels, gold, and gems; topped with the victory-banner Vaijayanti and royal pennants and parasol-upon-parasol fluttering in the wind; soaring high; their spires reaching up to touch the sky; their windows like jeweled lattice-cages opening into views; crowned with finials of jewel and gold; adorned with images of fully blossomed white lotuses and hundred-petalled flowers; decorated with the sacred *tilaka* mark, jewels, and the half-moon design; garlanded with strings of varied jewels; their interiors and exteriors smooth, with floors paved in shining bright sand of pure gold; soft to the touch, lustrous in form — so beautiful that they were beyond the comparable, surpassing in every way.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ sā bhaddā satthavāhī* = then the merchantess Bhadra | *dhaṇṇaṃ dārayaṃ* = (regarding) the boy Dhanya | *ummukkabālabhāvaṃ viṇṇāya* = recognizing that he had emerged from childhood | *pariṇayamittaṃ* = newly matured | *jovvaṇagamaṇupattaṃ* = having attained youth | *bāvattarikalāpaṃḍiyaṃ* = (and become) a master of the seventy-two arts | *ṇavaṃgasuttapaḍibohayaṃ* = his nine bodily organs awakened | *aṭṭhārasaviha-desippagāra-bhāsā-visārayaṃ* = expert in the eighteen kinds of regional dialects | *gīyaraī* = fond of song | *gaṃdhavva-ṇaṭṭa-kusalaṃ* = skilled in celestial music and drama | *siṃgārāgāra cāravesaṃ* = of dress that was the very abode of beauty | *saṃgayagaya-hasiya-bhaṇiya-ciṭṭhiya-vilāsa-ṇiuṇṇa-juttovayāra-kusalaṃ* = accomplished in graceful gait, smile, speech, posture, charm, and refined courtesy | *hayajohiṃ … bāhuppamaddiṃ alaṃ bhogasamatthaṃ* = (the four-fold warrior list and the "fit-for-enjoyment" formula, as in the prior sutra) | *jāṇittā* = having recognized this | *battīsaṃ pāsāya vaḍiṃsae kārei* = caused thirty-two excellent palaces to be built | *abbhugayamūsie* = lofty and tall | *pahasie viva* = as though smiling | *maṇikaṇagarayaṇabhatticitte* = ornamented with designs in jewels, gold, and gems | *vāuddhūya-vijaya-vejayaṃti-paḍāgā-cchattāicchatta-kalie* = endowed with the wind-blown Vijaya/Vaijayanti banners, pennants, and parasol-upon-parasol | *tuṃge* = soaring high | *gagaṇatalam-abhilaṃghamāṇa-sihare* = with peaks (apparently) crossing the surface of the sky | *jālaṃtara rayaṇa paṃjarummilliyavva* = like jeweled cage-windows opening through the lattices | *maṇikaṇaga-thūbhiyāe* = crowned with finials of jewel and gold | *viyasiya-sayapatta-puṃḍarīe* = (adorned with) blossomed white lotuses and hundred-petalled flowers | *tilaya-rayaṇaddha-caṃdaccie* = marked with *tilaka*-design, jewels, and the half-moon ornament | *ṇāṇāmaṇimayadāmālaṃkie* = decorated with garlands of varied jewels | *aṃto bahiṃ ca saṇhe* = smooth within and without | *tavaṇijja ruila vāluyāpatthaḍe* = floors paved with shining lustrous golden sand | *suhaphāse* = soft and pleasant to the touch | *sasirīyarūve* = of lustrous and graceful form | *pāsādīe jāva paḍirūve* = beautiful, and so on, beyond compare (a canonical compression covering several stock adjectives)

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra is the first of two long, ornate descriptions of the dwellings Bhadra built for her son. The motif is canonical: just as Megha Kumar's father had palaces built for him, just as Mahabala's parents had palaces built for him, so Bhadra — though a widowed merchantess — provides exactly this princely treatment for Dhanya. The fact that a woman of business is doing for her son what kings did for their sons is the scripture's quiet way of underscoring her wealth and her devotion.

The number thirty-two is significant. In the next sutras, the entire household will be organized in units of thirty-two: thirty-two palaces, thirty-two wives, thirty-two of every category of luxury and gift. The number is symbolic in the canon — thirty-two is the number of completeness in many enumerations: the thirty-two laws of moral conduct, the thirty-two auspicious bodily marks, the thirty-two fine attendants, the thirty-two ideal bodily proportions. To say "thirty-two" is to say "the full canonical set." Dhanya's life is built up in the language of completeness.

The description of the palaces is one of the most lyrical descriptive passages in the scripture. Each phrase is a precise architectural detail, but together they form a single image of impossibly luminous habitation. The palaces are tall enough that their spires seem to touch the sky. They are decorated outside and inside — with paintings depicting jewels, gold, and precious stones; with pennants and banners that flutter in the wind, including the special victory-banner called Vaijayanti; with parasols stacked one above another (a mark of royal honor); with lattice-windows that look like jeweled cages opening to the world outside; with golden finials; with images of blossoming lotuses and hundred-petalled flowers; with the sacred *tilaka* design; with the half-moon ornament; with garlands of every kind of jewel. Inside and out the surfaces are perfectly smooth. The floors are paved with shining sand of pure gold — a detail that, even in a culture accustomed to luxury, was the marker of unimaginable opulence.

The phrase "as though smiling" applied to the buildings is a beautiful poetic touch. The palaces gleamed with such inner radiance that they seemed not just to shine but to be alive with cheerful welcome. Architecture here is not just construction; it is presence.

A note on the Gujarati gloss for this sutra: the commentary explicitly says that this entire descriptive cluster — and the one that follows about the central palace — is to be understood as identical in form to the descriptions in the Jnata Dharma Katha. The compilers expected the reader to draw on the parallel passages elsewhere. The point of including such elaborate description is theological: when Dhanya later renounces all this, the reader must feel the weight of what is being given up. A renunciation that costs nothing teaches nothing. A renunciation that walks away from this much teaches everything.

There is also an unspoken layer in this sutra worth noticing. Bhadra builds these palaces because she sees that her son has matured and will soon enter householder life. This is the act of a loving mother securing her son's future. She is not preparing him to renounce; she is preparing him to live a full worldly life. The tragedy and beauty of what comes later — when she will weep and beg him not to leave — is rooted right here, in this sutra, where she is so carefully building everything that he will eventually let go of.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

When Bhadra saw that her son Dhanya had grown into a brilliant, accomplished young man ready for adult life, she had thirty-two magnificent palaces built for him — tall, gleaming with gold and jewels, topped with banners and parasols, their spires almost touching the sky, their floors paved with shining gold dust, so beautiful they almost seemed to be smiling.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

How much of the world that surrounds you was built by someone who loved you — and what is the most loving way to honor that gift, even if some day your path leads beyond it?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S6 — The Central Palace Among the Thirty-Two

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तेसिं मज्झे एगं भवणं अणेगखंभसयसंणिविट्ठं लीलट्ठियसालभंजियागे अब्भुगयसुकयवइरवेइयातोरणवररइयसालभंजियासुसिलिट्ठविसिट्ठलट्ठसंठियपसत्थ वेरुलियखंभणाणामणिकणगरयणखचियउज्जलं बहुसमसुविभत्तणिचियरमणिज्ज भूमिभागं ईहामिग जाव भत्तिचित्तं खंभुग्गयवइर वेइयापरिगयाभिरामं विज्जाहरजमल जुयलजुत्तं अच्चीसहस्स मालिणीयं विवं रूवगसहस्सकलियं भिसमाणं भिब्भिसमाणं चक्खुल्लोयणलेसं सुहफासं ससिरीयरूवं कंचणमणिरयणथूभियागं णाणाविह पंचवण्ण घंटापडागपरिमंडियगसिहरं धवलमरीचिकवयं विणिम्मुयंत लाउल्लोइयमहियं जाव गंधवट्टिभूयं पासादीयं दरिसणिज्जं अभिरूवं पडिरूवं । ॥3.A1.S6॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tesiṃ majjhe egaṃ bhavaṇaṃ aṇegakhaṃbhasayasaṃṇiviṭṭhaṃ līlaṭṭhiyasālabhaṃjiyāge abbhugayasukayavairaveiyātoraṇavararaiyasālabhaṃjiyāsusiliṭṭhavisiṭṭhalaṭṭhasaṃṭhiyapasattha veruliyakhaṃbhaṇāṇāmaṇikaṇagarayaṇakhaciyaujjalaṃ bahusamasuvibhattaṇiciyaramaṇijja bhūmibhāgaṃ īhāmiga jāva bhatticittaṃ khaṃbhuggayavaira veiyāparigayābhirāmaṃ vijjāhara-jamala juyalajuttaṃ accīsahassa māliṇīyaṃ vivaṃ rūvagasahassakaliyaṃ bhisamāṇaṃ bhibbhisamāṇaṃ cakkhulloyaṇalesaṃ suhaphāsaṃ sasirīyarūvaṃ kaṃcaṇamaṇirayaṇathūbhiyāgaṃ ṇāṇāviha paṃcavaṇṇa ghaṃṭāpaḍāgāparimaṃḍiyagasiharaṃ dhavalamarīcikavayaṃ viṇimmuyaṃta lāullouyamahiyaṃ jāva gaṃdhavaṭṭibhūyaṃ pāsādīyaṃ darisaṇijjaṃ abhirūvaṃ paḍirūvaṃ | || 3.A1.S6 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Among those thirty-two palaces, one central mansion was built that stood upon many hundreds of pillars; in it were set sculpted figurines posed in playful gestures, framed by raised, finely-wrought, diamond-studded balustrades and ornamental archways; the figurines were beautifully smoothed, well-shaped, finely posed, of auspicious form; the supporting pillars were of choice cat's-eye gem inlaid with various jewels, gold, and precious stones, all blazing with light. Its floor was perfectly level, well-divided, dense, and lovely; ornamented with pictures of wolf-deer and other figures; the spaces between the upraised diamond-balustrades were charming with twin pairs of celestial vidyādhara figures; it was as if garlanded with a thousand flames of light; ornamented with a thousand decorative figures; shining brightly, dazzlingly, with a brilliance that drew and held the eye; soft to the touch; lustrous in form; with finials of gold, jewels, and precious stones; its roof-peak adorned with bells and pennants in many shapes and five colors; emitting white rays of light all around; well-plastered and well-whitewashed; fragrant as a mass of pure scent; beautiful, worthy of beholding, surpassing in form, beyond compare.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tesiṃ majjhe* = among those (thirty-two palaces) | *egaṃ bhavaṇaṃ* = one central mansion | *aṇega-khaṃbhasaya-saṃṇiviṭṭhaṃ* = resting upon many hundreds of pillars | *līlaṭṭhiya-sālabhaṃjiyāge* = with sculpted female figurines posed in playful gestures (the *shalabhanjika* — a tree-spirit motif of a woman holding a branch) | *abbhugaya-sukaya-vaira-veiyā-toraṇa* = with raised, well-made, diamond-studded balustrades and archways | *vara-raiya-sālabhaṃjiyā* = excellent, finely-made figurines | *susiliṭṭha-visiṭṭha-laṭṭha-saṃṭhiya-pasattha* = beautifully smoothed, distinguished, of fine and auspicious form | *veruliya-khaṃbha* = pillars of *vaiḍūrya* (cat's-eye gem) | *ṇāṇā-maṇi-kaṇaga-rayaṇa-khaciya-ujjalaṃ* = inlaid with various jewels, gold, and gems, all blazing | *bahusama-suvibhatta-ṇiciya-ramaṇijja-bhūmibhāgaṃ* = its floor perfectly level, well-divided, dense, and beautiful | *īhāmiga* = (figures of) wolf-deer (the *ihamriga* — a mythical wolf-deer image often used in palace ornament) | *jāva bhatticittaṃ* = "and so on, painted with figures" — canonical compression | *khaṃbhuggaya-vaira-veiyā-parigayā-abhirāmaṃ* = the spaces between the diamond-balustrades pleasing | *vijjāhara-jamala-juyala-juttaṃ* = furnished with twin pairs of *vidyādhara* (celestial knowledge-bearer) figures | *accī-sahassa-māliṇīyaṃ* = garlanded as if by a thousand flames | *vivaṃ rūvaga-sahassa-kaliyaṃ* = ornamented with a thousand decorative figures | *bhisamāṇaṃ bhibbhisamāṇaṃ* = blazing, dazzling | *cakkhu-lloyaṇa-lesaṃ* = of a brilliance that absorbs the eye's gaze | *suha-phāsaṃ* = soft to the touch | *sasirīya-rūvaṃ* = of lustrous form | *kaṃcaṇa-maṇi-rayaṇa-thūbhiyāgaṃ* = with finials of gold, jewels, and gems | *ṇāṇāviha paṃcavaṇṇa ghaṃṭā-paḍāgā-parimaṃḍiya-gasiharaṃ* = its peak adorned with bells and pennants of many kinds and five colors | *dhavala-marīci-kavayaṃ* = emitting white rays of light all around | *viṇimmuyaṃta lāullouya-mahiyaṃ* = well-plastered and well-whitewashed | *jāva gaṃdhavaṭṭibhūyaṃ* = "and so on, fragrant as a ball of pure scent" — canonical compression | *pāsādīyaṃ darisaṇijjaṃ abhirūvaṃ paḍirūvaṃ* = beautiful, worth beholding, surpassing in form, beyond compare (a canonical four-fold descriptive seal)

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra describes one specific mansion at the heart of the thirty-two — the central residence, the most splendid of them all. Where the previous sutra gave the general description of the thirty-two palaces, this one zooms in on the principal one. The architectural and ornamental detail is even more lavish.

It rests on hundreds of pillars — but not ordinary pillars. The columns are made of *vaidurya* — cat's-eye gem — inlaid with gold and other precious stones, every column blazing with inlaid light. Set between the columns and along raised balustrades are *shalabhanjika* figures — sculpted feminine forms posed in playful gestures, often depicted holding the branch of a tree. The *shalabhanjika* is one of the most ancient and beloved motifs in Indian sacred architecture, found on temples and palaces alike. Their presence here marks the building as architecturally classical and spiritually sanctified.

The balustrades and archways are studded with *vajra* — diamond, the hardest and most luminous of stones. Between them stand twin figures of *vidyādhara* — celestial knowledge-bearers, mythical beings who carry mystical sciences and dwell in the higher heavens. To carve such figures into the architecture was to invite the protective and elevating presence of the divine into the everyday space of habitation.

The interior is decorated with paintings of mythological creatures including the *ihamriga* — the wolf-deer, a hybrid figure popular in Indian decorative art. The roof is crowned with finials of gold and jewels, with bells and five-colored pennants, and the building emits white rays of light "all around" — that is, light reflected from its polished and jeweled surfaces in every direction. The whole building has been freshly plastered and whitewashed, and is fragrant — perhaps from incense, perhaps from sandalwood paste applied to its walls.

The closing sequence of adjectives — beautiful, worth beholding, surpassing in form, beyond compare — is a canonical four-word seal that the scripture uses to mark something as having reached the upper limit of describable splendor. After these four words, no more is said.

The Gujarati commentary, after rendering all these details, offers a quiet reflection: such a building was the dwelling of someone whose worldly fortune was complete. There was nothing missing; nothing more could be added. And the gentle implication is that this completeness itself is the seed of the renunciation to come. When a soul has experienced the highest form of worldly beauty and discovered, in the experiencing, that it does not satisfy the inner thirst — only then is the soul ready for the next step. The exquisite palace is, in the spiritual logic of the canon, also a kind of teacher. It teaches Dhanya, by giving him everything, that everything is not enough.

The detail of the bells on the rooftop deserves a brief note: in classical Indian architecture, bells on temple and palace peaks served not only as ornament but as a kind of soft music played by the wind — the structures sang as the breeze passed through them. Dhanya's central palace was therefore not only visually but audibly alive — perfumed, glowing, and singing.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

The most magnificent of the thirty-two palaces stood at the center: built on hundreds of jeweled columns, decorated with sculpted figures of women and celestial beings in playful poses, its diamond-studded balustrades shining with inlaid gold and gems, its golden finials and bells gleaming on the roof, its walls fresh-plastered and fragrant, its surfaces so polished that they emitted white rays of light in every direction — a building beautiful beyond comparison.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When you have built or received something that seems perfect — a home, a relationship, a moment — does its very perfection sometimes whisper that something more is being asked of you?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S7 — The Marriage to Thirty-Two Wives and the Thirty-Two Gifts

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं सा भद्दा सत्थवाही तं धण्णं दारयं बत्तीसाए इब्भवरकण्णगाणं एगदिवसेणं पाणिं गेण्हावेइ । बत्तीसओ दाओ । तं जहा— बत्तीसं हिरण्णकोडीओ, बत्तीसं सुवण्णकोडीणो, बत्तीसं मउडे मउडप्पवरे, बत्तीसं कुंडलजुयले कुंडलजुयलप्पवरे, बत्तीसं हारे हारप्पवरे, बत्तीसं अद्धहारे अद्धहारप्पवरे, बत्तीसं एगावलीओ एगावलिप्पवराओ, एवं मुत्तावलीओ, एवं कणगावलीओ, एवं रयणावलीओ, बत्तीसं कडगजोए कडगजोयप्पवरे, एवं तुडियजोए, बत्तीसं खोमजुयलाइं खोमजुयलप्पवराइं, एवं पडगजुयलाइं, एवं पट्टजुयलाइं एवं दुगुल्लजुयलाइं, बत्तीसं सिरीओ, बत्तीसं हिरीओ, एवं धिईओ, कित्तीओ, बुद्धीओ, लच्छीओ, बत्तीसं णंदाइं, बत्तीसं भद्दाइं, बत्तीसं तले तलप्पवरे सव्वरयणामए, बत्तीसं णगरवरभवणकेऊ, बत्तीसं झए झयप्पवरे, बत्तीसं वये वयप्पवरे दसगोसाह- स्सिएणं वएणं, बत्तीसं णाडगाइं णाडगप्पवराइं बत्तीसइबद्धेणं णाडएणं, बत्तीसं आसे आसप्पवराइं सव्वरयणामए सिरिघरपडिरूवए, बत्तीसं हत्थी हत्थिप्पवरे सव्वरयणामए सिरिघरपडिरूवए, बत्तीसं जाणाइं जाणप्पवराइं, बत्तीसं जुगाइं जुगप्पवराइं, एवं सिवियाओ, एवं संदमाणीओ, एवं गिल्लीओ थिल्लीओ, बत्तीसं वियडजाणाइं वियडजाणप्पवराइं, बत्तीसं रहे पारिजाणिए, बत्तीसं रहे संगामिए, बत्तीसं आसे आसप्पवरे, बत्तीसं हत्थी हत्थिप्पवरे, बत्तीसं गामे गामप्पवरे दसकुलसाहस्सिएणं गामेणं, बत्तीसं दासे दासप्पवरे, एवं चेव दासीओ, एवं किंकरे, एवं कंचुइज्जे, एवं वरिसधरे, एवं महत्तरए, बत्तीसं सोवण्णिए ओलंबणदीवे, बत्तीसं रुप्पामए ओलंबणदीवे, बत्तीसं सुवण्णरुप्पामए ओलंबणदीवे, बत्तीसं सोवण्णिए उक्कंचणदीवे, बत्तीसं पंचरदीवे, एवं चेव तिण्णि वि, बत्तीसं सोवण्णिय थाले, बत्तीसं रुप्पमए थाले, बत्तीसं सुवण्णरुप्पमए थाले, बत्तीसं सोवण्णियाओ पत्तीओ ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णियाइं थासयाइं ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णियाइं मल्लगाइं ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णियाओ तलियाओ ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णियाओ कावइयाओ ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णिए अवएडए ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णियाओ अवयक्काओ ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णिए पायपीढए ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णियाओ भिसियाओ ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णियाओ करोडियाओ ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णिए पल्लंके ३, बत्तीसं सोवण्णियाओ पडिसेज्जाओ ३, बत्तीसं हंसासणाइं, बत्तीसं कोंचासणाइं, एवं गरुलासणाइं, उण्णयासणाइं, पणयासणाइं, दीहासणाइं, भद्दासणाइं, पक्खासणाइं, मगरासणाइं, बत्तीसं पठमासणाइं, बत्तीसं दिसासोवत्थियासणाइं, बत्तीसं तेल्लसमुग्गे, जहा रायप्पसेणइज्जे, जाव बत्तीसं सरिसवसमुग्गे, बत्तीसं खुज्जाओ (जहा उव-वाइए) जाव बत्तीसं पारिसीओ, बत्तीसं छत्ते, बत्तीसं छत्तधारिणीओ चेडीओ, बत्तीसं चामराओ, बत्तीसं चामरधारिणीओ चेडीओ, बत्तीसं तालियंटे, बत्तीसं तालियंटधारिणीओ चेडीओ, बत्तीसं करोडियाओ, बत्तीसं करोडियाधारिणीओ चेडीओ, बत्तीसं खीरधाईओ जाव बत्तीसं अंकधाईओ, बत्तीसं अंगमद्दियाओ, बत्तीसं उम्मद्दियाओ, बत्तीसं ण्हाविवायाओ, बत्तीसं पसाहियाओ, बत्तीसं वण्णगपेसीओ, बत्तीसं चुण्णगपेसीओ, बत्तीसं कीडाकारीओ, बत्तीसं दवकारीओ, बत्तीसं उवत्थाणियाओ, बत्तीसं णाडइज्जाओ, बत्तीसं कोडुंबियणीओ, बत्तीसं महाणसिणीओ, बत्तीसं भंडारिणीओ, बत्तीसं अज्झाधारिणीओ, बत्तीसं पुष्फधारिणीओ, बत्तीसं पाणिधारिणीओ, बत्तीसं बलिकारीओ, बत्तीसं सेज्जाकारीओ, बत्तीसं अब्भिंतरियाओ पडिहारीओ, बत्तीसं बाहिरियाओ पडिहारीओ, बत्तीसं मालाकारीओ, बत्तीसं पेसणकारीओ, अण्णं वा सुबहुं हिरण्णं वा सुवण्णं वा कंसं वा दूसं वा विउलं धण कणग० जाव संतसारसावएज्जं, अलाहि जाव आसत्तमाओ कुलवंसाओ पकामं दाउं, पकामं भोत्तुं, पकामं परिभाएडं ।

तए णं से धण्णे कुमारे एगमेगाए भज्जाए एगमेगं हिरण्णकोडिं दलयइ जाव एगमेगं पेसणकारिं दलयइ, अण्णं वा सुबहुं हिरण्णं वा जाव परिभाएडं । तए णं से धण्णे कुमारे उप्पिं पासाय वरगए जाव माणुस्सए कामभोगे भुंजमाणे विहरइ । ॥3.A1.S7॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ sā bhaddā satthavāhī taṃ dhaṇṇaṃ dārayaṃ battīsāe ibbhavarakaṇṇagāṇaṃ egadivaseṇaṃ pāṇiṃ geṇhāvei | battīsao dāo | taṃ jahā— battīsaṃ hiraṇṇakoḍīo, battīsaṃ suvaṇṇakoḍīṇo, battīsaṃ mauḍe mauḍappavare, battīsaṃ kuṃḍalajuyale kuṃḍalajuyalappavare, battīsaṃ hāre hārappavare, battīsaṃ addhahāre addhahārappavare, battīsaṃ egāvalīo egāvalippavarāo, evaṃ muttāvalīo, evaṃ kaṇagāvalīo, evaṃ rayaṇāvalīo, battīsaṃ kaḍagajoe kaḍagajoyappavare, evaṃ tuḍiyajoe, battīsaṃ khomajuyalāiṃ khomajuyalappavarāiṃ, evaṃ paḍagajuyalāiṃ, evaṃ paṭṭajuyalāiṃ evaṃ dugulla-juyalāiṃ, battīsaṃ sirīo, battīsaṃ hirīo, evaṃ dhiīo, kittīo, buddhīo, lacchīo, battīsaṃ ṇaṃdāiṃ, battīsaṃ bhaddāiṃ, battīsaṃ tale talappavare savvarayaṇāmae, battīsaṃ ṇagaravarabhavaṇakeū, battīsaṃ jhae jhayappavare, battīsaṃ vaye vayappavare dasagosāhassieṇaṃ vaeṇaṃ, battīsaṃ ṇāḍagāiṃ ṇāḍagappavarāiṃ battīsaibaddheṇaṃ ṇāḍaeṇaṃ, battīsaṃ āse āsappavarāiṃ savvarayaṇāmae sirigharapaḍirūvae, battīsaṃ hatthī hatthippavare savvarayaṇāmae sirigharapaḍirūvae, battīsaṃ jāṇāiṃ jāṇappavarāiṃ, battīsaṃ jugāiṃ jugappavarāiṃ, evaṃ siviyāo, evaṃ saṃdamāṇīo, evaṃ gillīo thillīo, battīsaṃ viyaḍajāṇāiṃ viyaḍajāṇappavarāiṃ, battīsaṃ rahe pārijāṇie, battīsaṃ rahe saṃgāmie, battīsaṃ āse āsappavare, battīsaṃ hatthī hatthippavare, battīsaṃ gāme gāmappavare dasakulasāhassieṇaṃ gāmeṇaṃ, battīsaṃ dāse dāsappavare, evaṃ ceva dāsīo, evaṃ kiṃkare, evaṃ kaṃcuijje, evaṃ varisadhare, evaṃ mahattarae, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇie olaṃbaṇadīve, battīsaṃ ruppāmae olaṃbaṇadīve, battīsaṃ suvaṇṇaruppāmae olaṃbaṇadīve, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇie ukkaṃcaṇadīve, battīsaṃ paṃcaradīve, evaṃ ceva tiṇṇi vi, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiya thāle, battīsaṃ ruppamae thāle, battīsaṃ suvaṇṇaruppamae thāle, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiyāo pattīo 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiyāiṃ thāsayāiṃ 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiyāiṃ mallagāiṃ 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiyāo taliyāo 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiyāo kāvaiyāo 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇie avaeḍae 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiyāo avayakkāo 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇie pāyapīḍhae 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiyāo bhisiyāo 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiyāo karoḍiyāo 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇie pallaṃke 3, battīsaṃ sovaṇṇiyāo paḍisejjāo 3, battīsaṃ haṃsāsaṇāiṃ, battīsaṃ koṃcāsaṇāiṃ, evaṃ garulāsaṇāiṃ, uṇṇayāsaṇāiṃ, paṇayāsaṇāiṃ, dīhāsaṇāiṃ, bhaddāsaṇāiṃ, pakkhāsaṇāiṃ, magarāsaṇāiṃ, battīsaṃ paṭhamāsaṇāiṃ, battīsaṃ disāsovatthiyāsaṇāiṃ, battīsaṃ tellasamugge, jahā rāyappaseṇaijje, jāva battīsaṃ sarisavasamugge, battīsaṃ khujjāo (jahā uvavāie) jāva battīsaṃ pārisīo, battīsaṃ chatte, battīsaṃ chattadhāriṇīo ceḍīo, battīsaṃ cāmarāo, battīsaṃ cāmaradhāriṇīo ceḍīo, battīsaṃ tāliyaṃṭe, battīsaṃ tāliyaṃṭadhāriṇīo ceḍīo, battīsaṃ karoḍiyāo, battīsaṃ karoḍiyādhāriṇīo ceḍīo, battīsaṃ khīradhāīo jāva battīsaṃ aṃkadhāīo, battīsaṃ aṃgamaddiyāo, battīsaṃ ummaddiyāo, battīsaṃ ṇhāviyāo, battīsaṃ pasāhiyāo, battīsaṃ vaṇṇagapesīo, battīsaṃ cuṇṇagapesīo, battīsaṃ kīḍākārīo, battīsaṃ davakārīo, battīsaṃ uvatthāṇiyāo, battīsaṃ ṇāḍaijjāo, battīsaṃ koḍuṃbiyaṇīo, battīsaṃ mahāṇasiṇīo, battīsaṃ bhaṃḍāriṇīo, battīsaṃ ajjhādhāriṇīo, battīsaṃ pussadhāriṇīo, battīsaṃ pāṇidhāriṇīo, battīsaṃ balikārīo, battīsaṃ sejjākārīo, battīsaṃ abbhiṃtariyāo paḍihārīo, battīsaṃ bāhiriyāo paḍihārīo, battīsaṃ mālākārīo, battīsaṃ pesaṇakārīo, aṇṇaṃ vā subahuṃ hiraṇṇaṃ vā suvaṇṇaṃ vā kaṃsaṃ vā dūsaṃ vā viulaṃ dhaṇa kaṇaga° jāva saṃtasārasāvaejjaṃ, alāhi jāva āsattamāo kulavaṃsāo pakāmaṃ dāuṃ, pakāmaṃ bhottuṃ, pakāmaṃ paribhāeḍaṃ |

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe kumāre egamegāe bhajjāe egamegaṃ hiraṇṇakoḍiṃ dalayai jāva egamegaṃ pesaṇakāriṃ dalayai, aṇṇaṃ vā subahuṃ hiraṇṇaṃ vā jāva paribhāeḍuṃ | tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe kumāre uppiṃ pāsāya varagae jāva māṇussae kāmabhoge bhuṃjamāṇe viharai | || 3.A1.S7 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then the merchantess Bhadra had her son Dhanya marry, on a single day, the daughters of thirty-two leading wealthy households. The wedding-gifts were given in thirty-twos, namely: thirty-two crores of silver coins; thirty-two crores of gold coins; thirty-two finest crowns; thirty-two pairs of finest earrings; thirty-two finest necklaces; thirty-two finest half-necklaces; thirty-two finest single-strand chains; the same numbers of pearl-strands, gold-strands, and jewel-strands; thirty-two pairs of finest bracelets and the same of armlets; thirty-two pairs of fine *kshauma* (linen) cloth, and the same of fine cotton, fine silk, and fine *dukula* (silk-cotton blend) cloth; thirty-two images of the goddess Shri, of Hri, of Dhriti, of Kirti, of Buddhi, and of Lakshmi (the deities of fortune, modesty, steadfastness, fame, wisdom, and prosperity); thirty-two of the goddess Nanda; thirty-two of the goddess Bhadra; thirty-two finest seats of the floor — all made of every kind of jewel; thirty-two finest mansion-banners of the city; thirty-two finest banners; thirty-two finest cattle-herds, each herd of ten thousand cows; thirty-two finest dramas performed by troupes of thirty-two players; thirty-two finest horses, all jewel-bodied, looking like treasure-houses; thirty-two finest elephants, also jewel-bodied, looking like treasure-houses; thirty-two finest carriages; thirty-two finest yokes (a kind of vehicle); the same of palanquins, of *syandamani* (covered carriages), of *gilli* and *thilli* (kinds of vehicles); thirty-two finest open carriages; thirty-two pleasure-chariots; thirty-two war-chariots; thirty-two finest horses; thirty-two finest elephants; thirty-two finest villages, each of ten thousand families; thirty-two finest manservants; the same of maidservants; the same of attendants, chamberlains, eunuch-servants, and chief stewards; thirty-two hanging lamps of gold; thirty-two of silver; thirty-two of gold-and-silver; thirty-two raised lamps of gold; thirty-two five-branched lamps; the same in three (gold, silver, and gold-silver); thirty-two plates of gold; thirty-two of silver; thirty-two of gold-and-silver; thirty-two cups, three each in gold, silver, and gold-silver; thirty-two small dishes, three each; thirty-two bowls, three each; thirty-two trays, three each; thirty-two basins, three each; thirty-two pots, three each; thirty-two flasks, three each; thirty-two foot-stools, three each; thirty-two cushions, three each; thirty-two lidded boxes, three each; thirty-two couches, three each; thirty-two bedding-mats, three each; thirty-two swan-shaped seats, thirty-two crane-shaped seats, the same of eagle-shaped, raised, lowered, long, fine, side-, makara-, *padma*-, and *svastika*-direction-shaped seats; thirty-two oil-caskets, as described in the Rajaprashniya Sutra, down to thirty-two mustard-seed caskets; thirty-two hunchback maidservants (as described in the Aupapatika Sutra), down to thirty-two attendant maidservants of the Parisi country; thirty-two parasols, and thirty-two maidservants to hold them; thirty-two flywhisks, and thirty-two maidservants to hold them; thirty-two palm-fans, and thirty-two maidservants to hold them; thirty-two betel-boxes, and thirty-two maidservants to hold them; thirty-two milk-nurses, down to thirty-two lap-nurses; thirty-two limb-massaging maidservants; thirty-two scrubbing maidservants; thirty-two bathing maidservants; thirty-two adorning maidservants; thirty-two unguent-grinders; thirty-two powder-grinders; thirty-two play-companions; thirty-two amusement-companions; thirty-two assembly-attendants; thirty-two performers of plays; thirty-two cooks; thirty-two head-cooks; thirty-two storeroom-keepers; thirty-two custodians; thirty-two flower-bearers; thirty-two water-bearers; thirty-two offering-makers; thirty-two bed-preparers; thirty-two inner-court guardian-maids; thirty-two outer-court guardian-maids; thirty-two garland-makers; thirty-two errand-doer maidservants — together with abundant other silver, gold, brass, cloth, vast wealth and gold and so on, the very best treasure, sufficient that for seven generations of the lineage he could give freely, enjoy freely, and share freely. Then Dhanya Kumar gave to each one of his wives one crore of silver coins each, and so on down to one errand-attendant each, along with abundant other silver and gold and the rest, to share freely. Thereafter Dhanya Kumar dwelled in his fine high palace, savoring the human pleasures of the senses.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ sā bhaddā satthavāhī* = then the merchantess Bhadra | *taṃ dhaṇṇaṃ dārayaṃ* = (regarding) her son Dhanya | *battīsāe ibbha-vara-kaṇṇagāṇaṃ* = of the daughters of thirty-two leading wealthy households (*ibbha* = wealthy, prominent merchant family) | *egadivaseṇaṃ pāṇiṃ geṇhāvei* = caused him to take their hand (be married) on a single day | *battīsao dāo* = the gifts (came) in thirty-twos | *taṃ jahā* = namely | *hiraṇṇa-koḍīo* = crores of silver | *suvaṇṇa-koḍīṇo* = crores of gold | *mauḍe mauḍappavare* = finest crowns | *kuṃḍala-juyale* = pairs of earrings | *hāre / addhahāre / egāvalī / muttāvalī / kaṇagāvalī / rayaṇāvalī* = necklaces, half-necklaces, single-strand, pearl-, gold-, and jewel-strands (a graded series of jewelry) | *kaḍaga-joe / tuḍiya-joe* = pairs of bracelets / armlets | *khoma / paḍaga / paṭṭa / dugulla juyalāiṃ* = pairs of *kshauma* (linen), cotton, silk, and *dukula* (silk-cotton) cloth | *sirīo, hirīo, dhiīo, kittīo, buddhīo, lacchīo* = (images of the goddesses) Shri (fortune), Hri (modesty), Dhriti (steadfastness), Kirti (fame), Buddhi (wisdom), Lakshmi (prosperity) | *ṇaṃdāiṃ, bhaddāiṃ* = (images of the goddesses) Nanda and Bhadra | *tale talappavare* = floor-seats / dais of the finest kind | *savva-rayaṇāmae* = made entirely of jewels | *ṇagara-vara-bhavaṇa-keū* = banners (*ketu*) of the finest mansions of the city | *jhae jhayappavare* = finest banners (flags) | *vaye vayappavare* = finest cattle-herds (*vraja*) | *dasagosāhassieṇaṃ vaeṇaṃ* = each herd consisting of ten thousand cows | *ṇāḍagāiṃ* = dramas, theatrical performances | *battīsai-baddheṇaṃ ṇāḍaeṇaṃ* = with each drama performed by a troupe of thirty-two | *āse / hatthī* = horses / elephants | *savva-rayaṇāmae sirighara-paḍirūvae* = jewel-bodied, looking like treasure-houses | *jāṇāiṃ / jugāiṃ* = carriages / yokes (vehicles) | *siviyāo* = palanquins | *saṃdamāṇīo* = covered carriages | *gillī, thillī* = named types of vehicles | *viyaḍa-jāṇāiṃ* = open carriages | *rahe pārijāṇie / saṃgāmie* = pleasure-chariots / war-chariots | *gāme gāmappavare* = finest villages | *dasakulasāhassieṇaṃ gāmeṇaṃ* = each village of ten thousand families | *dāse / dāsīo / kiṃkare / kaṃcuijje / varisadhare / mahattarae* = manservants, maidservants, attendants, chamberlains, eunuch-servants, chief stewards | *sovaṇṇie / ruppāmae olaṃbaṇa-dīve* = hanging lamps of gold / silver | *ukkaṃcaṇa-dīve* = raised lamps | *paṃcara-dīve* = five-branched lamps | *thāle / pattīo / thāsayāiṃ / mallagāiṃ / taliyāo / kāvaiyāo / avaeḍae / avayakkāo / pāyapīḍhae / bhisiyāo / karoḍiyāo / pallaṃke / paḍisejjāo* = plates, cups, small dishes, bowls, trays, basins, pots, flasks, foot-stools, cushions, lidded boxes, couches, bedding (each in three metals: gold, silver, gold-silver — hence "3" in the numbering) | *haṃsa / koṃca / garula / uṇṇaya / paṇaya / dīha / bhadda / pakkha / magara / paṭha / disāsovatthiya āsaṇāiṃ* = swan-, crane-, eagle-, raised-, lowered-, long-, fine-, side-, makara-, *padma*-, and *svastika*-direction-shaped seats | *tella-samugge … sarisava-samugge* = caskets of oils to caskets of mustard-seed (a complete graduated set) | *jahā rāyappaseṇaijje* = "as in the Rajaprashniya Sutra" — canonical cross-reference | *khujjāo* = hunchback (maidservants) | *jahā uvavāie* = "as in the Aupapatika Sutra" — canonical cross-reference | *pārisīo* = maidservants of the Parisi region | *chatte / chattadhāriṇīo ceḍīo* = parasols and parasol-bearing maids | *cāmarāo / cāmaradhāriṇīo* = flywhisks and flywhisk-bearing maids | *tāliyaṃṭe / tāliyaṃṭadhāriṇīo* = palm-fans and palm-fan-bearing maids | *karoḍiyāo / karoḍiyādhāriṇīo* = betel-boxes and betel-box-bearing maids | *khīradhāīo … aṃkadhāīo* = milk-nurses, and so on, down to lap-nurses (the canonical five nurses) | *aṃga-maddiyāo* = limb-massagers | *ummaddiyāo* = scrubbers | *ṇhāviyāo* = bath-attendants | *pasāhiyāo* = adorners | *vaṇṇaga-pesīo / cuṇṇaga-pesīo* = unguent-grinders / powder-grinders | *kīḍākārīo / davakārīo* = play-companions / amusement-companions | *uvatthāṇiyāo* = assembly-attendants | *ṇāḍaijjāo* = drama-performers | *koḍuṃbiyaṇīo* = household-keepers | *mahāṇasiṇīo* = head-cooks | *bhaṃḍāriṇīo* = storeroom-keepers | *ajjhādhāriṇīo* = custodians | *pussadhāriṇīo* = flower-bearers | *pāṇidhāriṇīo* = water-bearers | *balikārīo* = offering-makers | *sejjākārīo* = bed-preparers | *abbhiṃtariyāo / bāhiriyāo paḍihārīo* = inner / outer guardian-maids | *mālākārīo* = garland-makers | *pesaṇakārīo* = errand-doers | *aṇṇaṃ vā subahuṃ hiraṇṇaṃ … saṃtasārasāvaejjaṃ* = and (besides) much other silver, gold, brass, cloth, abundant wealth — the very best treasure | *alāhi* = sufficient, enough | *āsattamāo kulavaṃsāo* = (sufficient) for the seventh generation of the lineage | *pakāmaṃ dāuṃ, bhottuṃ, paribhāeḍuṃ* = sufficient to give freely, enjoy freely, share freely | *egamegāe bhajjāe* = to each one of (his) wives | *egamegaṃ hiraṇṇa-koḍiṃ dalayai* = he gave one crore of silver each | *uppiṃ pāsāya varagae* = situated in the upper part of his fine palace | *māṇussae kāmabhoge bhuṃjamāṇe viharai* = dwelled, savoring the human pleasures of the senses

⚠️ This is one of the longest enumerative passages in the canon. The list of household items (plates, dishes, seats, lamps, caskets, etc.) follows a canonical formula attested in the Rajaprashniya Sutra and Aupapatika Sutra; the Gujarati commentary explicitly directs the reader to those texts for the full description. The repeated "3" markers in the Prakrit denote that the previous item-type was offered in three metals (gold, silver, gold-silver). Some specific item names (e.g. *gillī, thillī, kāvaiyāo, avaeḍae, avayakkāo*) are vehicle and household terms whose exact identification varies in scholarly sources; the renderings here follow the Gujarati gloss. The full set of seat-shapes (swan, crane, eagle, makara, etc.) reproduces the canonical formulaic list.

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra is the dramatic high point of Dhanya's worldly life. Bhadra arranges, on a single day, his marriage to thirty-two princesses — daughters of the most distinguished wealthy households of the city. The simultaneity is significant: this is not a series of marriages spread across years but a single great event, a one-day wedding to thirty-two brides. Such a marriage was the highest social ceremony of the time, and the gifts exchanged on it were correspondingly vast.

The list of gifts that follows is one of the most extensive enumerations in the Jain canon. It is not literary excess; it is theological architecture. Each category of gift represents one aspect of complete worldly fulfillment: wealth (crores of gold and silver coins), ornament (crowns, earrings, necklaces in graduated kinds, bracelets, armlets), clothing (four kinds of fine fabric in pairs), divine images of the goddesses of fortune and virtue (Shri, Hri, Dhriti, Kirti, Buddhi, Lakshmi, Nanda, Bhadra — each presence in the home invoking a quality of well-being), royal symbols (banners, flags, ceremonial seats), animals (cows, horses, elephants), vehicles of every kind (carriages, palanquins, covered and open and pleasure and war chariots), villages with thousands of families, every tier of human servant (manservants, maidservants, chamberlains, stewards, eunuch-attendants), light (lamps in three metals), eating-ware (plates, dishes, bowls, trays, in three metals), seats in eleven distinct shapes, caskets, parasols and flywhisks and fans (each with its bearer), the five canonical nurses, every kind of personal-care attendant (massagers, bathers, adorners, perfume-grinders), entertainers (play-companions, drama-performers), household management (cooks, storekeepers, custodians), ritual attendants (flower-bearers, water-bearers, offering-makers, bed-preparers), and guards both inner and outer. The list ends with a closing seal: and besides all this, abundant other gold, silver, brass, cloth — wealth so vast it would last for seven generations and be sufficient for free giving, free enjoyment, and free sharing.

The repetition of "thirty-two" creates a hypnotic rhythm. By the time the list ends, the reader's mind cannot hold the sum. That is precisely the point. The canon is not asking the reader to count; it is asking the reader to feel the totality. Dhanya was not merely wealthy; he stood at the absolute apex of household opulence imaginable in the canonical worldview.

The phrase "sufficient for seven generations" is significant. In Indian thought, seven generations is the standard span that the actions of the present can blessedly affect — beyond which the chain of inheritance attenuates. To say that Dhanya's wealth was sufficient for seven generations is to say that not only he but his great-great-great-great-grandchildren would never want for anything. His worldly future was secured to the absolute horizon of imagination.

The closing detail is exquisitely tender. Dhanya himself, having received all this, gave to each of his thirty-two wives one crore of silver, one crore of gold, all the way down to a personal errand-attendant — and besides this, abundant additional wealth to share freely. This gesture frames Dhanya as not just a recipient but a giver. His marriage was not an act of accumulating; it was an act of distributing. And the verb-formula at the end — "to share freely" — matches the threefold formula given to all canonical wealth: to give, to enjoy, to share. None of these ends are selfish; all three are honorable uses of fortune.

The Gujarati commentary observes that this entire passage parallels the description of Megha Kumar's marriage in the Jnata Dharma Katha — the canonical model for how a noble youth's worldly establishment is to be told. The seeker who wishes for fuller detail is invited to consult that parallel.

The sutra closes with the beautifully spare line: Dhanya now dwelled in his fine high palace, savoring the human pleasures of the senses. After two pages of overflowing detail, this single sentence is the still center. It reports, without moralizing, that Dhanya lived a complete sensory life. The judgment is left for the reader. The scripture knows what it is doing: the more carefully it has built up the picture of fullness, the more powerfully the next sutra — when Dhanya hears the Lord and turns away from it all — will land. To renounce a peasant's hut is one thing. To renounce this — to walk away from thirty-two crore-gifts and thirty-two queens and seven generations of secured fortune — is the spiritual mountain the scripture is preparing the reader to climb.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Bhadra arranged for Dhanya to marry thirty-two daughters of wealthy families on a single day, and gave him thirty-two of every imaginable gift — thirty-two crores of silver and gold, thirty-two of every kind of jewelry, thirty-two villages of ten thousand families each, thirty-two horses and elephants, thirty-two of every kind of carriage, thirty-two of every kind of servant for every conceivable household task, and so much more wealth besides that it would have been enough for seven generations to give, enjoy, and share without ever running out. Dhanya then gave each of his wives a crore of silver, a crore of gold, and a personal attendant, and lived in his palace enjoying the full pleasures of human life.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

If you were given everything — every comfort, every relationship, every pleasure, every security — secured not just for yourself but for seven generations of your descendants, what part of you, do you think, would still be unsatisfied? And what would that unsatisfied part be longing for?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S8 — The Arrival of Mahavira and the Hearing of the Dharma

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तेणं कालेणं तेणं समएणं समणे भगवं महावीरे जाव समोसढे । परिसा णिग्गया । राया जहा कोणिओ तहा जियसत्तू णिग्गओ । तए णं तस्स धण्णस्स तं चेव जहा जमाली तहा णिग्गओ । णवरं पायचारेणं जाव पज्जुवासइ । तए णं समणे भगवं महावीरे धण्णस्स कुमारस्स तीसे य महइमहालियाए इसि परिसाए जाव धम्मं परिकहेइ । परिसा पडिगया । ॥3.A1.S8॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

teṇaṃ kāleṇaṃ teṇaṃ samaeṇaṃ samaṇe bhagavaṃ mahāvīre jāva samosaḍhe | parisā ṇiggayā | rāyā jahā koṇio tahā jiyasattū ṇiggao | tae ṇaṃ tassa dhaṇṇassa taṃ ceva jahā jamālī tahā ṇiggao | ṇavaraṃ pāyacāreṇaṃ jāva pajjuvāsai | tae ṇaṃ samaṇe bhagavaṃ mahāvīre dhaṇṇassa kumārassa tīse ya mahaimahāliyāe isi parisāe jāva dhammaṃ parikahei | parisā paḍigayā | || 3.A1.S8 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

In that age, at that time, the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, the attainer of liberation, arrived (in the city of Kakandi). The assembly of citizens went forth (to hear him). Just as King Konika went forth (to greet the Lord in earlier accounts), so did King Jitashatru go forth.
