Then for Dhanya — just as Jamali had gone forth (to hear the Lord in his story), so did Dhanya go forth. The only distinction is that Dhanya went on foot — and (when he reached the Lord) he attended upon him with devotion. Then the Ascetic Lord Mahavira expounded the Dharma to Dhanya Kumar and to that great and luminous assembly of seers. The assembly returned (to its homes).

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*teṇaṃ kāleṇaṃ teṇaṃ samaeṇaṃ* = in that age, at that time | *samaṇe bhagavaṃ mahāvīre* = the Ascetic Lord Mahavira | *jāva samosaḍhe* = arrived (the canonical compression covers the standard arrival-passage) | *parisā ṇiggayā* = the assembly went forth | *rāyā jahā koṇio tahā jiyasattū ṇiggao* = "as King Konika (went forth), so King Jitashatru went forth" — a canonical reference to the standard royal-greeting passage where King Konika of Magadha went forth to receive Mahavira (told in the Aupapatika Sutra and elsewhere) | *tassa dhaṇṇassa* = of that Dhanya | *jahā jamālī tahā ṇiggao* = "as Jamali (went forth), so (Dhanya) went forth" — referring to the canonical going-forth passage in the Bhagavati Sutra concerning Mahavira's nephew Jamali | *ṇavaraṃ pāyacāreṇaṃ* = the only distinction (was that he went) on foot | *jāva pajjuvāsai* = and so on, attended upon (the Lord) with devotion | *dhaṇṇassa kumārassa* = (to) Dhanya Kumar | *tīse ya mahaimahāliyāe* = and to that very great (literally: "great-greater") | *isi parisāe* = assembly of seers (luminous gathering) | *dhammaṃ parikahei* = expounded the Dharma | *parisā paḍigayā* = the assembly returned

**5. COMMENTARY**

This is the turning point of Dhanya's life. Until this sutra, every line has built up his worldly fullness. From this sutra forward, every line will reveal his inner turning. The hinge is the arrival of one being: Mahavira.

The phrase "in that age, at that time" is the canonical formula that anchors sacred narrative in time and grants it eternal weight. Mahavira arrives in Kakandi during his roving teaching mission — the long arc of his life after enlightenment, in which he traveled from city to city giving sermons to the gathered assemblies. Wherever he went, the great divine pavilion (*samosarana*) arose around him; the assembly of human beings, gods, and animals gathered to listen.

The scripture handles the arrival in compressed canonical form. Three nested cross-references appear in a single passage. First, "the Lord arrived" — but the full description of his arrival, which would include the divine pavilion, the gathering of beings, the threefold circumambulation, and so forth, is left to the reader's familiarity with the standard arrival-passage from the Aupapatika Sutra. Second, "as King Konika went forth, so King Jitashatru went forth" — referring to the famous passage where the King of Magadha, learning of Mahavira's arrival, processed out of the city in full royal splendor, accompanied by his entourage, with garlands and elephants and chanted praises, to receive the Lord. King Jitashatru of Kakandi did the same. Third, "as Jamali went forth, so Dhanya went forth" — referring to the canonical passage about Jamali (Mahavira's nephew, son of Mahavira's sister Sudarshana) in the Bhagavati Sutra, where Jamali, on hearing the Lord, processed out of his palace with full ceremony to attend the discourse.

But then — beautifully — comes the one detail that distinguishes Dhanya from all the references: *ṇavaraṃ pāyacāreṇaṃ* — "the only difference is, he went on foot." This is a quiet but profound detail. King Jitashatru came with all royal pomp; Jamali came with palanquins and attendants in the proper canonical manner. Dhanya did not. He walked. The man who had thirty-two horses, thirty-two elephants, thirty-two of every kind of carriage, walked.

The Gujarati commentary draws specific attention to this. It is the first sign — small but unmistakable — of the shift already beginning in Dhanya's heart. He is, formally, still a householder of the highest opulence. But on this morning he wanted to come to Mahavira not as a man of the world coming with his pomp, but as a soul approaching its teacher. He left the carriages behind. He came on foot. The body was already tracking what the soul had not yet quite named.

When he arrived, he attended upon the Lord *pajjuvasai* — "with devotion, in service-posture." In the canonical idiom, this is the bodily form of a true seeker: seated near the teacher, hands folded, body still, mind alert. The phrase covers the entire posture of receptive listening.

Then Mahavira expounded the Dharma — the eternal teaching — to Dhanya and to the great assembly of seers gathered around him. The phrase *mahaimahāliyāe* — "very great, great-greater" — uses the doubled superlative deliberately. This was not an ordinary gathering; it was a vast, luminous assembly of seekers. The teaching given was therefore the full teaching, not a casual address.

The closing line — "the assembly returned" — appears almost throwaway, but it is doing significant narrative work. It is clearing the stage. The crowd has gone home. The next sutra will be a private moment: just Dhanya, the lingering teaching, and the rising of dispassion in his own heart.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Lord Mahavira arrived in the city of Kakandi; the citizens, the king, and Dhanya himself all went out to hear him. The remarkable thing about Dhanya was that, despite all his wealth and means, he chose to walk to the Lord on foot. He listened devotedly as Mahavira gave the teaching to the great assembly, and after the talk the people returned to their homes.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When you go to receive something sacred — a teaching, a meeting, a moment of truth — what does the way you arrive say about who you really are at that moment? Are you walking, or are you riding?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S9 — The Rising of Dispassion in Dhanya's Heart

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं धण्णे कुमारे समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अंतिए धम्मं सोच्चा, णिसम्म हट्ठ-तुट्ठ जाव हियए, उट्ठाए उट्ठेइ, उट्ठेत्ता समणं भगवं महावीरं तिक्खुत्तो आयाहिणं पयाहिणं करेइ, करेत्ता वंदइ णमंसइ, वंदित्ता णमंसित्ता एवं वयासी—

सद्दहामि णं भंते ! णिग्गंथं पावयणं । पत्तियामि णं भंते ! णिग्गंथं पावयणं । रोएमि णं भंते ! णिग्गंथं पावयणं । अब्भुट्ठेमि णं भंते ! णिग्गंथं पावयणं । एवमेयं भंते ! तहमेयं भंते ! अवितहमेयं भंते ! असंदिद्धमेयं भंते ! जाव से जहेयं तुब्भे वयह, जं णवरं अम्मयं भद्दं सत्थवाहिं आपुच्छामि । तए णं अहं देवाणुप्पियाणं अंतिए मुंडे भविता अगाराओ अणगारियं पव्वयामि ।

अहासुहं देवाणुप्पिया ! मा पडिबंधं करेह । ॥3.A1.S9॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ dhaṇṇe kumāre samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtie dhammaṃ soccā, ṇisamma haṭṭha-tuṭṭha jāva hiyae, uṭṭhāe uṭṭhei, uṭṭhettā samaṇaṃ bhagavaṃ mahāvīraṃ tikkhutto āyāhiṇaṃ payāhiṇaṃ karei, karettā vaṃdai ṇamaṃsai, vaṃdittā ṇamaṃsittā evaṃ vayāsī—

saddahāmi ṇaṃ bhante ! ṇiggaṃthaṃ pāvayaṇaṃ | pattiyāmi ṇaṃ bhante ! ṇiggaṃthaṃ pāvayaṇaṃ | roemi ṇaṃ bhante ! ṇiggaṃthaṃ pāvayaṇaṃ | abbhuṭṭhemi ṇaṃ bhante ! ṇiggaṃthaṃ pāvayaṇaṃ | evameyaṃ bhante ! tahameyaṃ bhante ! avitahameyaṃ bhante ! asaṃdiddhameyaṃ bhante ! jāva se jaheyaṃ tubbhe vayaha, jaṃ ṇavaraṃ ammayaṃ bhaddaṃ satthavāhiṃ āpucchāmi | tae ṇaṃ ahaṃ devāṇuppiyāṇaṃ aṃtie muṃḍe bhavittā agārāo aṇagāriyaṃ pavvayāmi |

ahāsuhaṃ devāṇuppiyā ! mā paḍibaṃdhaṃ kareha | || 3.A1.S9 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then Dhanya Kumar, having heard the Dharma from the lips of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, having taken it deeply into his mind, became delighted and joyful — and so on — in his heart. He stood up; standing, he made the threefold rightward circumambulation around the Ascetic Lord Mahavira. Having so circumambulated, he bowed and saluted; having bowed and saluted, he spoke thus: "O Lord! I have faith in this teaching of the unbound ones. O Lord! I trust in this teaching of the unbound ones. O Lord! I delight in this teaching of the unbound ones. O Lord! I am ready to act on this teaching of the unbound ones. O Lord, it is exactly so. O Lord, it is truly so. O Lord, it is without falsehood. O Lord, it is without doubt — and so on — it is exactly as you say. There is only one thing: I will first ask leave of my mother, the merchantess Bhadra. After that, in the presence of you, the Most Beloved of the gods, I shall have my head shaved and shall go forth from the household life into the homeless life." (Mahavira replied:) "As is most fitting for your happiness, Most Beloved of the gods. Make no delay (in the work of dharma)."

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ dhaṇṇe kumāre* = then Dhanya Kumar | *samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtie* = from the very lips of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira (literally "in the proximity of") | *dhammaṃ soccā* = having heard the Dharma | *ṇisamma* = having taken it in (deeply contemplated) | *haṭṭha-tuṭṭha jāva hiyae* = delighted, joyful, and so on, in his heart (the canonical compound describing inner gladness) | *uṭṭhāe uṭṭhei* = he rose with the proper posture | *tikkhutto āyāhiṇaṃ payāhiṇaṃ karei* = made the rightward circumambulation three times (the canonical gesture of reverence: walking three times clockwise around the teacher) | *vaṃdai ṇamaṃsai* = he bowed and saluted | *evaṃ vayāsī* = and spoke thus | *saddahāmi* = "I have faith in" | *pattiyāmi* = "I trust" | *roemi* = "I delight in, I am drawn to" | *abbhuṭṭhemi* = "I am ready to act on, to take up" | *ṇiggaṃthaṃ pāvayaṇaṃ* = the teaching of the *nirgranthas* — literally "the unbound ones," those who have cut all knots; the standard self-description of the Jain monastic tradition and its doctrine | *evameyaṃ … tahameyaṃ … avitahameyaṃ … asaṃdiddhameyaṃ* = "it is so / truly so / without falsehood / without doubt" — a canonical fourfold affirmation of received truth | *jāva se jaheyaṃ tubbhe vayaha* = "and so on, it is exactly as you say" | *jaṃ ṇavaraṃ* = "the one thing is" — a transitional marker introducing the only condition | *ammayaṃ bhaddaṃ satthavāhiṃ āpucchāmi* = "I shall ask leave of my mother, the merchantess Bhadra" | *muṃḍe bhavittā* = having had my head shaved | *agārāo aṇagāriyaṃ pavvayāmi* = I shall go forth from the householder's life into the homeless life (i.e., I shall take ordination as a monk) | *ahāsuhaṃ devāṇuppiyā* = "as is comfortable / fitting for your happiness, Most Beloved of the gods" — the canonical phrase of the teacher's blessing | *mā paḍibaṃdhaṃ kareha* = "do not make delay" — i.e., do not let any obstacle slow the work of dharma

**5. COMMENTARY**

This is the inner pivot of the entire chapter. The previous sutras built up Dhanya's worldly life with extraordinary care; this sutra describes the inner shift in a few lines that are utterly clear and utterly weighted. The crowd has dispersed. The sermon is over. What remains is one young man, alone with what he has just heard, and the response that rises in him.

The first verb is *soccā* — "having heard." But the next is *ṇisamma* — "having taken it in." This is the canonical pair: hearing and absorbing. Many people heard the sermon that day; only some absorbed it. Dhanya was among those who let the teaching land. The next words describe the inner state — *haṭṭha-tuṭṭha jāva hiyae* — delight, joy, and so on, in the heart. This is not the troubled response one might expect from a man whose entire worldly life has just been called into question. Dhanya's first response is *joy*. The teaching has not threatened him; it has freed something in him. The Gujarati commentary observes that this joy is itself a sign of spiritual readiness — a soul whose karma is ripening for renunciation greets the call to renounce with gladness, not grief.

He stands up. He performs the threefold rightward circumambulation — the canonical Jain gesture of devotion: walking three times clockwise around the teacher, the body itself becoming a sphere whose center is the source of teaching. He bows. He salutes. Only then, with body and posture aligned, does he speak.

The sequence of his declarations is exquisite and cumulative. He uses four verbs in sequence:

*"I have faith"* — first, the conviction that the teaching is true.
*"I trust"* — next, the willingness to rely on it as a basis for life.
*"I delight in it"* — third, the emotional turn: this is not a duty, this is what my heart has been longing for.
*"I am ready to act on it"* — finally, the commitment to live it, not just admire it.

Faith, trust, delight, action. This fourfold sequence is itself a canonical teaching about how received truth ripens in the soul. It does not stop at intellectual agreement; it does not stop at emotional appreciation; it ripens into acted-upon conviction.

Then a second fourfold formula appears: *"It is so. It is truly so. It is without falsehood. It is without doubt."* This is the canonical four-step affirmation: a teaching is first acknowledged, then deepened, then cleared of any falseness, then sealed against any doubt. By the end of these four phrases, Dhanya has placed his entire spiritual weight upon what Mahavira has just spoken.

He concludes with the only condition that remains: *"I will first ask leave of my mother, the merchantess Bhadra."* This single line is the doorway to the next sutras. Dhanya is willing — eager — to renounce immediately. But he honors his mother enough that he will not take this step without her blessing. He will leave the world; he will not leave his mother without first sitting with her, hearing her, and receiving her permission. This piety is itself part of his spiritual maturity.

The fact that Dhanya names *only* his mother — not his thirty-two wives, not his palaces, not his fortune — is striking. The wives, the wealth, the buildings: he is ready to walk away from all of them without explanation. But Bhadra is the one human being whose consent he will seek. The Gujarati commentary, which had earlier observed that the entire household ran in Bhadra's name, here closes that interpretive arc: Dhanya is the son of his mother, and his mother alone holds the relational thread he must respect before walking out of the world.

Mahavira's reply is the briefest possible. *"As is fitting for your happiness, Most Beloved of the gods. Make no delay."* The first phrase is the canonical permission given by a teacher to a student about to take up the path: do what brings true well-being. The second phrase is the gentle but firm urging: do not let the path slow. *Pratibandha* literally means "obstruction, delay" — and the warning is not that Dhanya should rush, but that he should not allow any inner or outer obstacle to keep him from what his soul has now committed to.

The arc of this sutra is the arc that the entire Anuttaropapātika scripture is, in different ways, telling thirty-three times: a soul, having heard the teaching, makes the inward turn that nothing in the world can prevent.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

After the sermon, Dhanya — alone with what he had heard — found his heart filled with joy. He stood up, walked three times around Mahavira in reverence, bowed, and said: "Lord, I have faith in your teaching, I trust it, I delight in it, I am ready to live it. It is exactly as you say — without falsehood, without doubt. There is only one thing: I want to ask my mother first. Then, with your permission, I will shave my head and leave home and become a homeless monk." Mahavira gently replied: "Do whatever brings you true happiness, dear one. But do not let anything delay the path."

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When a teaching reaches the deepest place in you, what is the four-fold ripening it asks for — believing, trusting, delighting, and acting? Where in your life have you already begun this ripening, and where, if you are honest, have you stopped at the first or second step?

---

*[End of Part A. The narrative continues in Part B with Dhanya's conversation with his mother Bhadra, her grief and resistance, his resolve, and the unfolding of his renunciation.]*


---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S10 — Dhanya Goes to His Mother

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं से धण्णे कुमारे समणेणं भगवया महावीरेणं एवं वुत्ते समाणे हट्ट-तुट्ठे समणं भगवं महावीरं तिक्खुत्तो वंदइ णमंसइ वंदित्ता णमंसित्ता, *जाव* जेणेव अम्मयाओ तेणेव उवागच्छइ, उवागच्छित्ता अम्मयाओ जएणं विजएणं वद्धावेइ जएणं विजएणं वद्धाविता एवं वयासी— एवं खलु अम्मयाओ ! मए समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अंतियं धम्मे णिसंते, से वि य मे धम्मे इच्छिए, पडिच्छिए, अभिरुइए ।

तए णं धण्णं कुमारं अम्मयाओ एवं वयासी— धण्णेसि णं तुमं जाया, कयत्थेसि णं तुमं जाया, कयपुण्णेसि णं तुमं जाया, कयलक्खणेसि णं तुमं जाया, जं णं तुमे समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अंतियं धम्मे णिसंते, से वि य धम्मे इच्छिए, पडिच्छिए, अभिरुइए ।

तए णं से धण्णे कुमारे अम्मयाओ दोच्चं पि एवं वयासी— एवं खलु अम्मयाओ ! मए समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अंतियं धम्मे णिसंते *जाव* अभिरुइए । तए णं अहं अम्मयाओ ! संसारभउव्विग्गे, भीए जम्म-जरा-मरणेणं, तं इच्छामि णं अम्मयाओ ! तुब्भेहिं अब्भणुण्णाए समाणे समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अंतियं मुंडे भविता अगाराओ अणगारियं पव्वइत्तए । ॥3.A1.S10॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe kumāre samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ evaṃ vutte samāṇe haṭṭa-tuṭṭhe samaṇaṃ bhagavaṃ mahāvīraṃ tikkhutto vaṃdai ṇamaṃsai vaṃdittā ṇamaṃsittā, *jāva* jeṇeva ammayāo teṇeva uvāgacchai, uvāgacchittā ammayāo jaeṇaṃ vijaeṇaṃ vaddhāvei jaeṇaṃ vijaeṇaṃ vaddhāvittā evaṃ vayāsī— evaṃ khalu ammayāo ! mae samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtiyaṃ dhamme ṇisaṃte, se vi ya me dhamme icchie, paḍicchie, abhirue |

tae ṇaṃ dhaṇṇaṃ kumāraṃ ammayāo evaṃ vayāsī— dhaṇṇesi ṇaṃ tumaṃ jāyā, kayatthesi ṇaṃ tumaṃ jāyā, kayapuṇṇesi ṇaṃ tumaṃ jāyā, kayalakkhaṇesi ṇaṃ tumaṃ jāyā, jaṃ ṇaṃ tume samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtiyaṃ dhamme ṇisaṃte, se vi ya dhamme icchie, paḍicchie, abhirue |

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe kumāre ammayāo doccaṃ pi evaṃ vayāsī— evaṃ khalu ammayāo ! mae samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtiyaṃ dhamme ṇisaṃte *jāva* abhirue | tae ṇaṃ ahaṃ ammayāo ! saṃsārabhauvvigge, bhīe jamma-jarā-maraṇeṇaṃ, taṃ icchāmi ṇaṃ ammayāo ! tubbhehiṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtiyaṃ muṃḍe bhavittā agārāo aṇagāriyaṃ pavvaittae | || 3.A1.S10 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then Dhanya Kumar, having been thus addressed by the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, became delighted and joyful; he bowed and saluted the Ascetic Lord Mahavira three times, and having so bowed and saluted, he came (and so on, by the canonical compression) to the place where his mother was. Having approached, he greeted his mother with the words of victory and triumph; and having so greeted her, he spoke thus: "O revered mother, truly I have heard the Dharma in the presence of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira; and that Dharma I have desired, accepted, and delighted in." Then his mother said to Dhanya Kumar: "Blessed are you, O son! Fulfilled are you, O son! Of merit accomplished are you, O son! Marked with the auspicious signs are you, O son! — that you have heard the Dharma in the presence of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, and that Dharma you have desired, accepted, and delighted in." Then Dhanya Kumar said to his mother a second time: "O revered mother, truly I have heard the Dharma in the presence of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, and so on… I have delighted in it. And now, O revered mother, I am stricken with dread of the cycle of births and deaths, and afraid of birth, old age, and dying; therefore, O revered mother, with your permission I wish to have my head shaved in the presence of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira and to go forth from the household life into the homeless life of a monk."

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ* = then, thereupon | *se dhaṇṇe kumāre* = that Dhanya Kumar | *samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ evaṃ vutte samāṇe* = having been thus addressed (i.e., blessed with permission) by the Ascetic Lord Mahavira | *haṭṭa-tuṭṭhe* = delighted and joyful | *tikkhutto vaṃdai ṇamaṃsai* = bowed and saluted three times | *jāva* = and so on (the canonical compression covering the standard taking-leave passage) | *jeṇeva ammayāo teṇeva uvāgacchai* = went to where his mother was | *jaeṇaṃ vijaeṇaṃ vaddhāvei* = greeted (her) with "Jaya, Vijaya" — the canonical formula of auspicious greeting (literally, "may you have victory, may you have triumph") | *evaṃ vayāsī* = spoke thus | *evaṃ khalu ammayāo* = "truly so, O revered mother" | *mae* = by me | *samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtiyaṃ* = in the presence (lit. proximity) of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira | *dhamme ṇisaṃte* = the Dharma has been heard | *se vi ya me dhamme* = and that Dharma | *icchie* = desired | *paḍicchie* = accepted, embraced | *abhirue* = delighted in, found pleasing | *ammayāo evaṃ vayāsī* = the mother spoke thus (to him) | *dhaṇṇesi ṇaṃ tumaṃ jāyā* = "O son, you are blessed" (literally "you have *dhanya*, you are fortunate") — note the wordplay: the boy's own name *Dhanya* means "blessed/fortunate" | *kayatthesi ṇaṃ tumaṃ jāyā* = "O son, you are *kṛtārtha* — one whose purpose has been accomplished" | *kayapuṇṇesi ṇaṃ tumaṃ jāyā* = "O son, you are one whose merit has been accomplished, full of stored merit" | *kayalakkhaṇesi ṇaṃ tumaṃ jāyā* = "O son, you are one in whom the auspicious signs have been fulfilled" | *jaṃ ṇaṃ tume* = "in that you" | *doccaṃ pi* = a second time, again | *saṃsārabhauvvigge* = stricken / agitated by the dread of the cycle of worldly existence | *bhīe* = afraid | *jamma-jarā-maraṇeṇaṃ* = of birth, old age, and death | *icchāmi ṇaṃ ammayāo* = "I wish, O revered mother" | *tubbhehiṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe* = with your permission having been granted | *muṃḍe bhavittā* = having become shaven (i.e., having my head shaved) | *agārāo aṇagāriyaṃ pavvaittae* = to go forth from the householder's life into the houseless (homeless) life

**5. COMMENTARY**

This is the threshold sutra. The teacher's blessing has been received; the resolve has been formed; only the conversation with the mother remains. With this sutra, the narrative moves from the great pavilion of the Lord into the inner room of the household — and the soul that, before the Lord, simply spoke its inmost wish, must now find words for the human being who carried him, fed him, and built her whole world around his presence.

The opening line is gentle and exact. Mahavira has just said, "As is fitting for your happiness — make no delay." Dhanya hears this not as duty but as joy. The text is careful: he is *delighted and joyful*. He is not setting out from his mother's house with the heavy step of a man going to break bad news to her; he is going with the lightness of a soul that has found its true direction. He bows to the Lord three times, performs the canonical taking-leave (covered here by the *jāva* compression), and goes straight from the assembly to his mother.

The greeting he offers is the canonical phrase *jaeṇaṃ vijaeṇaṃ vaddhāvei* — "he greeted her with the words of victory and triumph." This was the standard reverential salutation a son or junior gave to a parent or elder in the ancient Indian household: "May victory be yours, may triumph be yours." The detail is not decorative. Dhanya is signaling, before he speaks a single word about renunciation, that he has come to her with full filial reverence. He has not stormed in to announce his decision; he has come to her as a son first.

His opening is the most carefully chosen sentence he could have spoken. He does not begin with "I want to leave." He begins with what happened in his heart: "I have heard the Dharma; I have desired it, I have embraced it, I have delighted in it." The same fourfold language used in the previous sutra before Mahavira returns here, slightly compressed. He is showing his mother not his decision but the inner ground from which the decision arose.

Now an extraordinary thing happens. Before he can even ask his question, the mother responds — and her response is one of the most beautiful in the canon. *"Blessed are you, O son! Fulfilled are you, O son! You are one whose merit has been completed! You are one marked with the auspicious signs!"* These are four praises, in the same fourfold pattern Dhanya himself just used, and the very first word she speaks to him is *dhaṇṇesi* — "you are blessed" — using the very root that forms his name. She has named him *Dhanya* his whole life, and now, in the moment her son tells her he has heard the Lord's teaching with full heart, she addresses him as *blessed* in the deepest sense the name was always pointing to. The mother's first instinct, on hearing of her son's spiritual joy, is to bless him for it.

It is essential to read these four praises carefully. *Kṛtārtha* — your purpose has been accomplished. *Kṛtapuṇya* — your store of merit has come to fruition. *Kṛtalakṣaṇa* — the auspicious bodily marks you carry have proven true. The mother is acknowledging, in this moment, that everything she had ever sensed about her son's destiny is being confirmed before her eyes. Whatever path he is about to ask for, this opening blessing of hers will reverberate through the entire conversation that follows.

Yet she has only blessed him for *hearing* and *delighting in* the Dharma. She has not yet been asked the harder thing. So Dhanya speaks again — *doccaṃ pi*, "a second time." Now he names his actual fear: *saṃsārabhauvvigge*, "stricken with dread of the cycle of worldly existence"; *bhīe jamma-jarā-maraṇeṇaṃ*, "afraid of birth, old age, and death." This is the canonical triad of *saṃsāra*'s sufferings — and the language is precise. Dhanya does not say he is afraid of the world's hardships in some general way; he is afraid of the iron law that no human birth ever escapes: birth itself, the slow erosion of old age, and the certainty of dying. These are not problems to be solved within worldly life; they are the conditions of worldly life. Only the path beyond worldly life can answer them.

And so he asks: *"With your permission I wish to have my head shaved and to go forth into the homeless life of a monk."* The structure of his asking is exquisite. First he tells her his joy in the teaching. He waits for her blessing. Then, only then, does he tell her his fear and his resolve. He has not pressured her, has not surprised her, has not rushed her. He has prepared the ground.

But here the sutra ends — on the asking. The mother's blessing on the inner shift has come instantly; her response to the request itself will form the next sutra. Anyone who has ever asked a beloved one to release them into a higher calling knows the pause that now opens between mother and son.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Filled with joy after Mahavira's blessing, Dhanya bowed to the Lord three times, came home to his mother, and greeted her with full reverence. He told her, "I heard the Lord's teaching, and I loved it with all my heart." His mother's first response was a flood of blessing: "Blessed are you, my son! Fulfilled, full of merit, marked with every auspicious sign — that you have heard such Dharma and embraced it!" Then Dhanya spoke again, more carefully: "Mother, I am afraid of this whole round of birth, old age, and death. With your permission, I want to go to the Lord, have my head shaved, and become a homeless monk."

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When you must ask a loved one to release you into something higher, can you first share the joy that moved you — and only then, gently, the resolve it has produced in you?

---

---

### Dhanya's Renunciation and the Nine-Fold Abhigraha

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S12 — Dhanya Kumar's Words of Detachment

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं धण्णे कुमारे अम्मयं एवं वयासी— तहेव णं तं अम्मयाओ ! जं णं तुब्भे ममं एवं वयह— तुमं सि णं जाया ! अम्हं इट्ठे कंते *जाव* पव्वइहिसि । एवं खलु अम्मयाओ ! माणुस्सए भवे अणेगजाइ-जरा-मरण-रोग सारीरमाणस पकामदुक्ख-वेयण-वसण-सओवद्दवाभिभूए, अधुवे, अणिइए, असासए, संज्झब्भ- रागसरिसे, जलबुब्बुयसमाणे, कुसग्गजलबिंदुसण्णिभे, सुविणगदंसणोवमे, विज्जु- लयाचंचले, अणिच्चे, सडणपडणविद्धंसणधम्मे, पुव्विं वा पच्छा वा अवस्स विप्प- जहियव्वे भविस्सइ, से केस णं जाणइ अम्मयाओ ! के पुव्विं गमणयाए, के पच्छा गमणयाए ? तं इच्छामि णं अम्मयाओ ! तुब्भेहिं अब्भणुण्णाए समाणे समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अंतियं मुंडे भवित्ता अगाराओ अणगारियं पव्वइत्तए । ॥3.A1.S12॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ dhaṇṇe kumāre ammayaṃ evaṃ vayāsī— taheva ṇaṃ taṃ ammayāo ! jaṃ ṇaṃ tubbhe mamaṃ evaṃ vayaha— tumaṃ si ṇaṃ jāyā ! amhaṃ iṭṭhe kaṃte *jāva* pavvaihisi | evaṃ khalu ammayāo ! māṇussae bhave aṇegajāi-jarā-maraṇa-roga sārīramāṇasa pakāmadukkha-veyaṇa-vasaṇa-saovaddavābhibhūe, adhuve, aṇiie, asāsae, saṃjjhabbha-rāgasarise, jalabubbuyasamāṇe, kusaggajalabiṃdusaṇṇibhe, suviṇagadaṃsaṇovame, vijju-layācaṃcale, aṇicce, saḍaṇapaḍaṇaviddhaṃsaṇadhamme, puvviṃ vā pacchā vā avassa vippa-jahiyavve bhavissai, se kesa ṇaṃ jāṇai ammayāo ! ke puvviṃ gamaṇayāe, ke pacchā gamaṇayāe ? taṃ icchāmi ṇaṃ ammayāo ! tubbhehiṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtiyaṃ muṃḍe bhavittā agārāo aṇagāriyaṃ pavvaittae | || 3.A1.S12 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then Dhanya Kumar said to his mother: "It is just so, O revered mother, as you have said to me — 'O son, you are dear to us, beloved, and so on… you may take ordination only after our death.' But truly, O revered mother, this human life is overpowered by countless births, old age, death, disease, by extreme pains and sufferings of body and mind, by separations and by every kind of calamity; it is unstable, impermanent, not lasting, like the colour of the evening twilight, like a bubble on water, like a dewdrop balanced on the tip of a blade of grass, like the seeing of a dream, fickle as a flash of lightning, transient, by its very nature liable to decay, to fall, and to be destroyed; sooner or later it must without fail be left behind. Who knows, O revered mother, who will go first and who will go after? Therefore, O revered mother, with your permission I wish to have my head shaven in the presence of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira and to go forth from the household life into the homeless life of a monk."

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ* = thereupon, then | *dhaṇṇe kumāre* = Dhanya Kumar | *ammayaṃ* = to (his) mother | *evaṃ vayāsī* = spoke thus | *taheva ṇaṃ taṃ ammayāo* = "It is exactly so, O revered mother" | *jaṃ ṇaṃ tubbhe mamaṃ evaṃ vayaha* = "what you say to me" | *tumaṃ si ṇaṃ jāyā* = "you are, O son" | *amhaṃ iṭṭhe kaṃte* = "dear to us, beloved" | *jāva pavvaihisi* = "and so on… you shall take ordination (only after our death)" — canonical reference to the mother's prior plea | *māṇussae bhave* = the human existence, this human life | *aṇegajāi-jarā-maraṇa-roga* = many births, old age, death, disease | *sārīramāṇasa* = of body and mind | *pakāmadukkha-veyaṇa* = extreme pain and suffering | *vasaṇa* = misfortune, calamity | *saovaddavābhibhūe* = afflicted by all troubles | *adhuve* = unsteady | *aṇiie* = not eternal, not permanent | *asāsae* = not lasting, not enduring | *saṃjjhabbha-rāgasarise* = like the colour of evening twilight (the red glow of dusk that fades in moments) | *jalabubbuyasamāṇe* = like a bubble on water | *kusaggajalabiṃdusaṇṇibhe* = like a drop of water on the tip of a blade of *kusha* grass | *suviṇagadaṃsaṇovame* = like the seeing of a dream | *vijju-layācaṃcale* = fickle as a streak of lightning | *aṇicce* = impermanent | *saḍaṇapaḍaṇaviddhaṃsaṇadhamme* = of the very nature of decay, falling, and destruction | *puvviṃ vā pacchā vā* = sooner or later, before or after | *avassa vippajahiyavve bhavissai* = must without fail be left behind | *se kesa ṇaṃ jāṇai* = who knows? | *ke puvviṃ gamaṇayāe, ke pacchā gamaṇayāe* = who will go first, who will go after? | *icchāmi ṇaṃ* = I wish | *tubbhehiṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe* = with your permission having been given | *samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtiyaṃ* = in the presence of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira | *muṃḍe bhavittā* = becoming shaven-headed | *agārāo aṇagāriyaṃ pavvaittae* = to go forth from house-life into the houseless life

**5. COMMENTARY**

This is one of the most beloved passages in the entire Anuttarovavai — and indeed in the wider Jain canon — because it gives, in the voice of a young man on the threshold of renunciation, the clearest enumeration of the impermanence of human existence. Dhanya does not argue with his mother. He honours every word she has spoken. He acknowledges that he is dear to her, beloved, the very treasure of her heart. He does not deny her love; he simply lifts the gaze of love upward toward the truth that no love at the human level can outlast.

The series of similes given here is a famous canonical chain. Each image is chosen with extraordinary precision, and together they form a graduated meditation on transience. The colour of the evening twilight glows for a few minutes and is gone; a bubble on water swells, gleams, and bursts in an instant; a dewdrop on the sharp tip of a blade of grass trembles at the slightest breeze and falls; a dream is vivid while it lasts but vanishes the moment one wakes; a flash of lightning is dazzling and over before the eye can hold it. Each image is shorter-lived than the one before. Read together, they say: whatever measure of time you may believe a human life to be, the truth of it is briefer still.

Dhanya then names the deeper marks of human existence: it is afflicted by birth, old age, disease, and death; by sorrows of body and sorrows of mind; by separations from what is loved and meetings with what is not; by every kind of calamity. It is unstable, not eternal, not lasting. By its very inner nature it tends toward decay, toward falling, toward being destroyed. The three words *saḍaṇa* (rotting), *paḍaṇa* (falling), and *viddhaṃsaṇa* (utter destruction) form a triple law that governs every composite thing in the universe.

The closing question is the sharpest stroke: "Who knows, O mother, who will go first and who will go after?" This is not a child's threat to his parent; it is a calm, serious question. The conventional reasoning of the parent is, "Wait until we are gone, and then renounce" — as if death stood patiently in line and would surely come for the elders before the young. But death does not stand in line. Death takes whom it takes. To stake one's spiritual life on a sequence death has never agreed to follow is a foolish wager. Dhanya, having understood this, asks for the only thing left to ask: the mother's permission to enter the homeless life now, while the body still has the strength to walk the path.

This sutra is also a model of how renunciation should be communicated within a family. Dhanya speaks with full reverence, full acknowledgement, full love — and then, from within that love, he speaks the truth. He does not rebel. He does not run away in the night. He waits, he asks, he reasons. The Jain tradition has always held that renunciation taken without parental permission carries a flaw; renunciation taken with parental blessing is whole. Dhanya's words here are the very means by which that blessing is sought.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Dhanya tells his mother: "I love you, and what you say is true — I am dear to you. But this human life is short and uncertain, like a bubble on water or a flash of lightning; nobody knows who will die first. So please give me your blessing and let me become a monk now, while I still can."

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

Of all the things you are postponing in your spiritual life, which ones are you postponing under the silent assumption that death has agreed to wait for you?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S13 — Dhanya's Ordination

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं तं धण्णं कुमारं भद्दा सत्थवाही जाहे णो संचाएइ *जाव* जियसत्तुं आपुच्छइ । इच्छामि णं देवाणुप्पिया ! धण्णस्स दारयस्स णिक्खममाणस्स छत्त-मउड-चामराओ य विदिण्णाओ ।

तए णं जियसत्तू राया भद्दं सत्थवाहिं एवं वयासी— अच्छाहि णं तुमं देवाणुप्पिए ! सुणिवुत्तवीसत्था, अहण्णं सयमेव धण्णस्स दारयस्स णिक्खमणसक्कारं करिस्सामि ।

सयमेव जियसत्तू णिक्खमणं करेइ, जहा थावच्चापुत्तस्स कण्हो ।

तए णं धण्णे दारए सयमेव पंचमुट्ठियं लोयं करेइ *जाव* पव्वइए ।

तए णं धण्णे दारए अणगारे जाए ईरियासमिए *जाव* गुत्तबंभयारी । ॥3.A1.S13॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ taṃ dhaṇṇaṃ kumāraṃ bhaddā satthavāhī jāhe ṇo saṃcāei *jāva* jiyasattuṃ āpucchai | icchāmi ṇaṃ devāṇuppiyā ! dhaṇṇassa dārayassa ṇikkhamamāṇassa chatta-mauḍa-cāmarāo ya vidiṇṇāo |

tae ṇaṃ jiyasattū rāyā bhaddaṃ satthavāhiṃ evaṃ vayāsī— acchāhi ṇaṃ tumaṃ devāṇuppie ! suṇivuttavīsatthā, ahaṇṇaṃ sayameva dhaṇṇassa dārayassa ṇikkhamaṇasakkāraṃ karissāmi |

sayameva jiyasattū ṇikkhamaṇaṃ karei, jahā thāvaccāputtassa kaṇho |

tae ṇaṃ dhaṇṇe dārae sayameva paṃcamuṭṭhiyaṃ loyaṃ karei *jāva* pavvaie |

tae ṇaṃ dhaṇṇe dārae aṇagāre jāe īriyāsamie *jāva* guttabaṃbhayārī | || 3.A1.S13 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then, when the merchant-lady Bhadra was unable to dissuade Dhanya Kumar (and so on, as before), she went and asked King Jitashatru: "O Beloved of the gods! I wish that, for my son Dhanya as he goes forth, the royal parasol, the crown, and the chamaras be granted." Then King Jitashatru said to the merchant-lady Bhadra: "Stay calm, O Beloved of the gods, and be perfectly at ease — I myself will perform the ordination-honour for the boy Dhanya." King Jitashatru himself conducted the ordination-procession, just as Krishna had done for Thavaccaputra. Then the boy Dhanya, with his own hand, plucked out his hair in five fistfuls and (and so on) was ordained. Thus the boy Dhanya became a homeless monk — careful in walking, and so on, perfectly chaste and self-guarded.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ* = thereupon | *taṃ dhaṇṇaṃ kumāraṃ* = that boy Dhanya | *bhaddā satthavāhī* = the merchant-lady Bhadra (his mother) | *jāhe ṇo saṃcāei* = when she was not able (to turn him back) | *jāva* = and so on (a canonical reference to the standard parental-failure formula) | *jiyasattuṃ āpucchai* = she went to and asked King Jitashatru | *icchāmi ṇaṃ devāṇuppiyā* = "I wish, O Beloved of the gods" | *dhaṇṇassa dārayassa* = of the boy Dhanya | *ṇikkhamamāṇassa* = as he goes forth (into ordination) | *chatta-mauḍa-cāmarāo* = the (royal) parasol, the crown, and the chamaras (yak-tail fly-whisks) | *vidiṇṇāo* = be granted, be given | *jiyasattū rāyā* = King Jitashatru | *acchāhi ṇaṃ tumaṃ* = "stay (at peace), you" | *suṇivuttavīsatthā* = wholly calm, fully reassured | *ahaṇṇaṃ sayameva* = I myself | *ṇikkhamaṇasakkāraṃ karissāmi* = will perform the ordination-honour | *jahā thāvaccāputtassa kaṇho* = just as Krishna (did) for the son of Thavachcha (Thavaccaputra — a famous renunciate whose dīkṣā-mahotsava was conducted by Krishna Vasudeva, narrated in the Jnata Dharma Katha) | *paṃcamuṭṭhiyaṃ loyaṃ karei* = he plucks out (his hair) in five fistfuls (the canonical ascetic act of *pañca-muṣṭi loca*) | *pavvaie* = was ordained | *aṇagāre jāe* = became a homeless monk | *īriyāsamie* = endowed with care in walking (the first of the five *samitis*) | *jāva guttabaṃbhayārī* = and so on, up to "perfectly chaste, fully guarded in celibacy" — a canonical reference to the full set of *samitis* and *guptis* described elsewhere in the canon

**5. COMMENTARY**

When the mother Bhadra could not turn Dhanya from his resolve, she did not weep helplessly, nor did she try to bind him by emotional pressure. She rose to the height of the moment. Recognising that her son was passing from her household into the path of the Tirthankara, she went to the king himself and asked that the symbols of royal honour — the parasol, the crown, the yak-tail fly-whisks — be brought out for her son. This is the deepest reverence a mother can offer: she sends him away as a king, because she now sees what kind of king he is becoming.

King Jitashatru's response is equally noble. He does not merely grant the symbols; he says, "Be at peace, my dear lady — I myself shall conduct the ordination-procession of your son." This is unprecedented honour. The king of Kakandi himself, with his own hand, would lead Dhanya's *dīkṣā-mahotsava*. The scripture explicitly compares this to the ordination of Thavaccaputra, whose ceremony was conducted by Krishna Vasudeva himself — the parallel is precise and exalting. Just as the ruler of Dwarka had once led the merchant-son Thavaccaputra into Lord Neminatha's presence with the full pomp of a state procession, so Jitashatru would now lead Dhanya into Lord Mahavira's presence.

Then comes the moment all the wealth of the world cannot match. Dhanya himself, with his own two hands, performs *pañca-muṣṭi loca* — the plucking-out of the hair of the head in five fistfuls. This is the unmistakable mark of Jain ordination. No barber's blade is permitted; no scissors. The renouncer, by his own act, removes the hair from the body — accepting in that single gesture all the bodily pain that the ascetic life will henceforth carry without complaint. Five fistfuls — four for the four sides of the head, one for the chin-beard, in the canonical formula. With this act, Dhanya is no longer a layman in monk's dress; he is a monk.

The closing line draws the seal. He becomes *anagāra* — literally, "one with no house." He is *īrya-samita* — careful in every step he takes, lest he harm even an insect underfoot. The *jāva* clause indicates the full canonical sequence: careful in speech, careful in alms-seeking, careful in placing and lifting objects, careful in disposing of waste; restrained in mind, in speech, in body; and *gupta-brahmachārī* — perfectly chaste and inwardly guarded. With this sutra, the prince of merchants has become a soldier of the spirit.

A literary note: the sutra carefully says *bhaddā satthavāhī* — "the merchant-lady Bhadra" — without any mention of a father. The Gujarati commentary in this edition is emphatic on this point: in Dhanya's chapter, only the mother is named, and references to a father introduced from parallel passages (such as the Jamali narrative in the Bhagavati) should not be carried over here. Dhanya is, throughout this Adhyayana, a son who proceeds from his mother's blessing alone.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

When Dhanya's mother saw she could not change his mind, she went to the king himself and asked that her son be honoured with royal ceremony as he became a monk; the king said yes and led the ordination-procession personally; then Dhanya pulled out his own hair with his bare hands and became a monk who walked with perfect care and perfect self-restraint.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When someone you love walks toward something higher than the life you had planned for them, can you find the strength to send them off as royalty rather than holding them back as your own?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S14 — Dhanya Muni's Nine-Fold Abhigraha

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं से धण्णे अणगारे जं चेव दिवसे मुंडे भवित्ता अगाराओ अणगारियं पव्वइए, तं चेव दिवसं समणं भगवं महावीरं वंदइ णमंसइ वंदित्ता णमंसित्ता एवं वयासी— इच्छामि णं भंते ! तुब्भेहिं अब्भणुण्णाए समाणे जावज्जीवाए छट्ठं छट्ठेणं अणिक्खित्तेणं आयंबिलपरिग्गहिएणं तवोकम्मेणं अप्पाणं भावेमाणे विह- रित्तए । छट्टस्स वि य णं पारणयंसि कप्पि मे आयंबिलं पडिगाहित्तए णो चेव णं अणायंबिलं । तं पि य णं संसट्ठं णो चेव णं असंसट्ठं । तं पि य णं उज्झियधम्मियं णो चेव णं अणुज्झिय धम्मियं । तं पि य णं अण्णे बहवे समण माहण अतिहि- किवण वणीमगा णावकंखंति ।

अहासुहं देवाणुप्पिया ! मा पडिबंधं करेह ।

तए णं से धण्णे अणगारे समणेणं भगवया महावीरेणं अब्भणुण्णाए समाणे हट्ट-तुट्ठे जावज्जीवाए छट्ठं छट्ठेणं अणिक्खित्तेणं तवोकम्मेणं अप्पाणं भावेमाणे विहरइ । ॥3.A1.S14॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre jaṃ ceva divase muṃḍe bhavittā agārāo aṇagāriyaṃ pavvaie, taṃ ceva divasaṃ samaṇaṃ bhagavaṃ mahāvīraṃ vaṃdai ṇamaṃsai vaṃdittā ṇamaṃsittā evaṃ vayāsī— icchāmi ṇaṃ bhante ! tubbhehiṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe jāvajjīvāe chaṭṭhaṃ chaṭṭheṇaṃ aṇikkhitteṇaṃ āyaṃbilapariggahieṇaṃ tavokammeṇaṃ appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe viharittae | chaṭṭhassa vi ya ṇaṃ pāraṇayaṃsi kappi me āyaṃbilaṃ paḍigāhittae ṇo ceva ṇaṃ aṇāyaṃbilaṃ | taṃ pi ya ṇaṃ saṃsaṭṭhaṃ ṇo ceva ṇaṃ asaṃsaṭṭhaṃ | taṃ pi ya ṇaṃ ujjhiyadhammiyaṃ ṇo ceva ṇaṃ aṇujjhiya dhammiyaṃ | taṃ pi ya ṇaṃ aṇṇe bahave samaṇa māhaṇa atihi-kivaṇa vaṇīmagā ṇāvakaṃkhaṃti |

ahāsuhaṃ devāṇuppiyā ! mā paḍibaṃdhaṃ kareha |

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe haṭṭa-tuṭṭhe jāvajjīvāe chaṭṭhaṃ chaṭṭheṇaṃ aṇikkhitteṇaṃ tavokammeṇaṃ appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe viharai | || 3.A1.S14 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then on the very day on which the monk Dhanya was shaven and went forth from house-life to the homeless life, on that very day he bowed and saluted the Ascetic Lord Mahavira and, having bowed and saluted, spoke thus: "O Lord, with your permission I wish to live by this austere practice — for the whole of my life, fasting two days at a stretch without ever a break, and at every parana taking only the *ayambil* food, suffusing my soul therewith. Also, on the day of breaking the fast it shall be permitted for me to accept only *ayambil* food and not any food other than *ayambil*. And that food too only when it is *samsrishta* (already touching the giver's hand or vessel) and not *asamsrishta*. And that food too only of the *ujjhita-dharma* kind (food which the householder is otherwise about to throw away or which is fit only to be discarded), and not of the *anujjhita-dharma* kind (food the householder still wishes to keep). And that food too only when no other monk, no Brahmana, no guest, no destitute person, and no beggar desires it." (Mahavira said:) "As is your happy choice, O Beloved of the gods! Do not delay." Then the monk Dhanya, with the permission of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira granted to him, glad and joyful, dwelt for the whole of his life suffusing his soul with this austere practice — fasting two days at a stretch without ever a break.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre* = then that monk Dhanya | *jaṃ ceva divase* = on that very day | *muṃḍe bhavittā* = having become shaven | *agārāo aṇagāriyaṃ pavvaie* = went forth from house-life into the homeless life | *taṃ ceva divasaṃ* = on that very day | *vaṃdai ṇamaṃsai* = bowed and saluted | *icchāmi ṇaṃ bhante* = "O Lord, I desire" | *tubbhehiṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe* = with your permission having been given | *jāvajjīvāe* = for the whole of (my) life | *chaṭṭhaṃ chaṭṭheṇaṃ* = with continuous *chhaṭṭha* (two-day fasts, i.e., a *bela* — fasting for two consecutive days, taking food only on the third) | *aṇikkhitteṇaṃ* = without ever breaking it (literally "without ever setting it down") | *āyaṃbilapariggahieṇaṃ* = combined with *ayambil* (the austere meal-form: a single sitting, dry/spiceless food, no oil, no salt, no curd, no milk, no green vegetables, no sweet) | *tavokammeṇaṃ* = with this austere practice | *appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe* = suffusing the soul (with spiritual contemplation) | *viharittae* = to live, to abide | *chaṭṭhassa pāraṇayaṃsi* = on the day of breaking the *chhaṭṭha* fast | *kappi me āyaṃbilaṃ paḍigāhittae* = it shall be permitted for me to accept *ayambil* | *ṇo ceva ṇaṃ aṇāyaṃbilaṃ* = and not what is non-*ayambil* | *saṃsaṭṭhaṃ* = "touched" (food given by a hand or ladle already smeared with the very food being given — i.e., the giver was about to use it themselves) | *ṇo ceva ṇaṃ asaṃsaṭṭhaṃ* = and not "untouched" (i.e., not food the giver brings out fresh and clean specifically for the monk) | *ujjhiyadhammiyaṃ* = of the *ujjhita-dharma* kind, that is, food the householder considers fit to be discarded or about to be thrown away | *ṇo ceva ṇaṃ aṇujjhiya dhammiyaṃ* = and not food the householder still wishes to retain | *aṇṇe bahave* = many other (people) | *samaṇa* = (other) monks | *māhaṇa* = Brahmanas | *atihi* = guests | *kivaṇa* = the destitute, the poor | *vaṇīmagā* = beggars | *ṇāvakaṃkhaṃti* = do not desire (it) | *ahāsuhaṃ devāṇuppiyā* = "as is your happy choice, O Beloved of the gods" — Mahavira's canonical formula of permission | *mā paḍibaṃdhaṃ kareha* = "do not delay, do not put off" | *abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe* = with permission granted | *haṭṭa-tuṭṭhe* = glad and joyful | *jāvajjīvāe* = for the whole of life | *appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe viharai* = lived suffusing the soul

**5. COMMENTARY**

This is the very heart of the chapter and one of the most extreme austerity-vows in the entire Jain canon. On the very day of his ordination, before even sleeping a single night as a monk, Dhanya stood before Lord Mahavira and asked permission to bind himself for life to a discipline whose terms are almost impossible to imagine. Let us unfold them, condition by condition, so that the full architecture of his vow can be seen.

**Foundation 1 — The two-day fast, lifelong, unbroken.** The base practice is *chhaṭṭha* — fasting for two whole days, taking food only on the third. Most ascetics who undertake this do so as a periodic austerity; Dhanya undertook it lifelong, *aṇikkhitteṇaṃ* — without ever once setting it down. From the day of his ordination to the day of his death, he ate at most once every third day.

**Foundation 2 — Even on the third day, only ayambil.** *Ayambil* is the austere meal-form: a single sitting in twenty-four hours, taking only dry, tasteless, plain food — no oil, no salt, no curd, no milk, no green vegetables, no sweetness, no spice. It is the food a tongue cannot enjoy. So even on the third day when food was permitted, Dhanya did not eat a normal monk's meal; he ate only *ayambil*. There was no day in his monastic life on which he tasted a flavoured food.

Now come the seven further conditions imposed within that single *ayambil* meal — the conditions that make this vow famous as the *nava-koti* or nine-fold abhigraha:

**(3) Only ayambil food, never non-ayambil** — even within the alms-bowl, if any non-*ayambil* food was placed, it could not be accepted.

**(4) Only *samsrishta* food** — meaning the giver's hand or serving-ladle must already be smeared with that very food. *Samsrishta* literally means "already touching" — that is, the food the householder was about to eat themselves, or had already prepared for their own household, not a fresh portion brought out specially for the monk. Why this condition? Because food prepared specifically for an ascetic is *audeshika* — made-for-him — and may carry the karmic weight of having been cooked, served, or otherwise interfered with on the monk's account, leading to subtle violence or attachment in the giver. *Samsrishta* food is food that was always going to enter that household's daily flow.

**(5) Only *ujjhita-dharma* food** — food of the "to-be-discarded" nature. The Gujarati commentary cites the Tika gloss precisely: *ujjhita* means *parityāga* — that which is being given up, that which the householder considers no longer of use to themselves. Stale food. Food that would be set aside or fed to animals or thrown away. Food that no member of the household intends to eat. This is the most striking condition of all: Dhanya bound himself to take only what the world had already finished with.

**(6) Even within that, only when no other recipient wants it** — if any other monk, any Brahmana, any guest, any destitute person, or any beggar still desired that very food, Dhanya would not take it. He placed himself last in the line of all those who depend on a householder's giving. Only the food that not even a beggar would accept was food he could accept.

Read in sequence, the nine conditions are: (1) lifelong continuous *chhaṭṭha* fasting; (2) at every parana, only *ayambil*; (3) at parana, only what falls under *ayambil*-rules, never anything outside; (4) only *samsrishta* food, never *asamsrishta*; (5) only *ujjhita-dharma* food; (6) never *anujjhita-dharma* food; (7–9) and even within that, food that no monk, no Brahmana, no guest, no poor person, and no beggar wants. The structure is one of progressively narrowing acceptance — each condition strips away another category of food a monk could ordinarily take, until what remains is the food that, in a strict and beautiful sense, would otherwise be wasted.

The principle behind this vow is one of the deepest in the Jain ethic of nourishment: a monk should be a presence in the world that creates no extra production, no extra labour, no extra consumption — and ideally, a presence that converts what would be wasted into what sustains the path to liberation. Dhanya did not wish to be fed; he wished only to consume what no living being still desired. In this vow, the body's right to existence is reduced almost to nothing. The body is kept alive only by what would otherwise have rotted.

This is why this vow is among the most extreme in the entire canon. Other monks fasted long; other monks ate plain food; other monks took only what was offered without preparation. Dhanya combined every extremity at once and bound himself to it for life. The Gujarati commentary notes that the very same day he was ordained, the spirit of fierce austerity was so awakened in him that he could not wait even one day before binding himself to this vow.

Mahavira's response is, as ever, the Tirthankara's mark of approval to a soul that has truly seen: *"ahāsuhaṃ devāṇuppiyā, mā paḍibaṃdhaṃ kareha"* — "As gives you happiness, O Beloved of the gods. Do not delay." The Lord of all monks does not amplify, does not warn, does not embellish. He releases. And from that day until the end, Dhanya, glad and joyful, lived inside this nine-fold cage of fire — a cage that, paradoxically, was for him the open sky.

A practical note on the structure: the commentary observes that what matters most is not the harshness of the practice in itself but that it was undertaken with the teacher's permission. A discipline taken without the master's blessing is unsteady; a discipline taken under the Tirthankara's word is a path. Dhanya's vow is the perfect model: extreme austerity, perfectly bounded by humility, transparency, and obedience.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

On his very first day as a monk, Dhanya promised the Lord: for the rest of my life I will eat at most once every three days, and even then only the simplest, plainest food — and only food the householder was about to throw away, only food that no other monk or beggar or guest wants. The Lord blessed him and said, "Don't delay — go ahead."

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

If your body could be kept alive only by what others were about to throw away, what would your relationship to hunger, to taste, and to the silent assumption of "I deserve to eat" become?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S15 — Dhanya Muni's First Parana and Request to Beg Alms

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं से धण्णे अणगारे पढमछट्ठखमणपारणयंसि पढमाए पोरिसीए सज्झायं करेइ । बीयाए पोरिसीए झाणं झियायइ, तइयाए पोरिसीए अतुरियमचवलमसंभंते मुहपोत्तियं पडिलेहेइ, पडिलेहित्ता भायणाइं वत्थाइं पडिलेहेइ पडिलेहित्ता, भायणाइं पमज्जइ पमज्जित्ता, भायणाइं उग्गहेइ उग्गहित्ता, जेणेव समणे भगवं महावीरे तेणेव उवागच्छइ, उवागच्छित्ता समणं भगवं महावीरं वंदइ णमंसइ, वंदित्ता णमंसित्ता एवं वयासी— इच्छामि णं भंते ! तुब्भेहिं अब्भणुण्णाए छट्ठक्खमण-पारणगंसि कागंदीए णयरीए उच्च-णीय-मज्झिमाइं कुलाइं घरसमुदाणस्स भिक्खायरियाए अडित्तए ।

अहासुहं देवाणुप्पिया मा पडिबंधं करेह । ॥3.A1.S15॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre paḍhamachaṭṭhakhamaṇapāraṇayaṃsi paḍhamāe porisīe sajjhāyaṃ karei | bīyāe porisīe jhāṇaṃ jhiyāyai, taiyāe porisīe aturiyamacavalamasaṃbhaṃte muhapottiyaṃ paḍilehei, paḍilehittā bhāyaṇāiṃ vatthāiṃ paḍilehei paḍilehittā, bhāyaṇāiṃ pamajjai pamajjittā, bhāyaṇāiṃ uggahei uggahittā, jeṇeva samaṇe bhagavaṃ mahāvīre teṇeva uvāgacchai, uvāgacchittā samaṇaṃ bhagavaṃ mahāvīraṃ vaṃdai ṇamaṃsai, vaṃdittā ṇamaṃsittā evaṃ vayāsī— icchāmi ṇaṃ bhante ! tubbhehiṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe chaṭṭhakkhamaṇa-pāraṇagaṃsi kāgaṃdīe ṇayarīe ucca-ṇīya-majjhimāiṃ kulāiṃ gharasamudāṇassa bhikkhāyariyāe aḍittae |

ahāsuhaṃ devāṇuppiyā mā paḍibaṃdhaṃ kareha | || 3.A1.S15 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then the monk Dhanya, on the day of breaking his very first two-day fast, in the first quarter of the day did scripture-study; in the second quarter he sat in deep meditation; in the third quarter, without any haste, without any restlessness, without any agitation, he inspected the mouth-cloth, and having inspected it, he inspected his bowls and the cloth wrappings, and having inspected them he wiped them clean, and having wiped them he took them up; and having taken them up, he went to where the Ascetic Lord Mahavira was, and on coming there he bowed and saluted the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, and having bowed and saluted spoke thus: "O Lord, with your permission I wish, on this day of breaking my fast, to walk through the high, the low, and the middling households of the city of Kakandi, gathering alms from a community of homes." (And Mahavira said:) "As is your happy choice, O Beloved of the gods! Do not delay."

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*paḍhamachaṭṭhakhamaṇapāraṇayaṃsi* = on the day of breaking the very first two-day fast (the first *parana* after the first *chhaṭṭha*) | *paḍhamāe porisīe* = in the first *porisi* (one of the four three-hour quarters into which the daylight is divided in monastic discipline) | *sajjhāyaṃ karei* = does scripture-study, recites and reflects upon scripture | *bīyāe porisīe* = in the second *porisi* | *jhāṇaṃ jhiyāyai* = sits in deep meditation, holds *dhyana* | *taiyāe porisīe* = in the third *porisi* | *aturiyaṃ* = without haste | *acavalaṃ* = without bodily restlessness | *asaṃbhaṃte* = without mental agitation, without flutter | *muhapottiyaṃ paḍilehei* = inspects the *muh-patti* (the small mouth-cloth a Jain monk holds before the lips while speaking, to prevent harm to airborne life) | *bhāyaṇāiṃ* = the alms-bowls | *vatthāiṃ* = the cloths (cloth-coverings of the bowls) | *pamajjai* = wipes (with the soft *rajoharaṇa*-brush, to remove any small living thing) | *uggahei* = takes up, lifts up | *jeṇeva… teṇeva uvāgacchai* = goes to where the Lord is | *icchāmi ṇaṃ bhante* = "O Lord, I wish" | *abbhaṇuṇṇāe* = with permission | *chaṭṭhakkhamaṇa-pāraṇagaṃsi* = on the parana of the *chhaṭṭha* fast | *kāgaṃdīe ṇayarīe* = in the city of Kakandi | *ucca-ṇīya-majjhimāiṃ kulāiṃ* = high, low, and middling households | *gharasamudāṇassa* = of a *samudana* of houses (a community of houses, i.e., a series of homes taken without selection) | *bhikkhāyariyāe* = on the alms-rounds | *aḍittae* = to walk, to wander | *ahāsuhaṃ devāṇuppiyā* = "as is your happy choice, O Beloved of the gods" | *mā paḍibaṃdhaṃ kareha* = "do not delay"

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra is a small but vivid window into the inward shape of an ideal monastic day. Even on the day of breaking a forty-eight-hour fast — a day on which any ordinary body would be desperate for food and would think only of how soon it could eat — Dhanya does not rush. He divides the day exactly as a monk must divide it. The first quarter goes to *svadhyaya*, scripture-study. The second goes to *dhyana*, meditation. Only in the third quarter does he begin the careful preparations for the alms-walk, and even those preparations he carries out *without haste, without restlessness, and without agitation* — the threefold mark of an inwardly settled monk. Hunger does not move him. Hunger has been mastered.

The threefold formula *aturiya-macavala-masaṃbhaṃte* is one of the most beautiful tests of inner state in the canon. It says: a monk's actions should not be hurried (the body is not driven by urgency); not restless (no nervous fidgeting, no extra movement); not flustered (the mind is not jumping ahead). When even the simplest action — picking up a bowl — is performed in this triple stillness, the action itself becomes a meditation.

The careful sequence of *pratilekhana* — inspection — is also worth pausing over. The monk first inspects the mouth-cloth, then the bowls and their cloth wrappings, then he wipes the bowls clean with the soft brush, then he picks them up. Why? Because at every stage, a small living being might be harmed if not noticed. Pratilekhana is the discipline of seeing — slowly, deliberately, before any movement that might damage life. This is non-violence not merely as a vow but as a granular daily craft.

When everything is in order, Dhanya goes not to the city, but to the Lord. He does not take a single step toward food without first asking permission of his teacher. The vow of yesterday must be renewed today; yesterday's blessing does not automatically cover today's act. This is the *gurukrupa* — the absolute orientation of the disciple's every action toward the master's word.

The request itself is exact. He asks to walk through the *ucca-nica-madhyama* households — high, low, and middling. This phrase is one of the proudest features of the Jain monastic alms-discipline: the monk must not select. He must not seek out the rich; he must not avoid the poor; he must not stop only at the merchant houses. He walks the prescribed path through every kind of household, taking from each what is freely offered. The monk is, in this respect, beyond all class. The food of the poorest hut is as fitting as the food of the richest mansion, and the giving heart of any householder is honoured equally.

The phrase *gharasamudāṇa* — "a community of houses" — is canonical and important. It means the monk does not go to one favourite family; he walks through a *samudana*, a stretch of many houses one after another, accepting a small portion from each. This prevents any single household from being burdened, prevents any preferential bond from forming, and ensures that the monk's nourishment is distributed across the whole community.

Mahavira's permission, again, is the same single short release: *ahāsuhaṃ, mā paḍibaṃdhaṃ kareha* — "as gives you happiness, do not delay." Notice that Mahavira does not advise; he does not say "be careful"; he does not warn the new monk that his nine-fold vow will make alms-getting nearly impossible. He simply releases the disciple to walk. The Tirthankara has perfect trust in a vow that was perfectly seen, perfectly asked, and perfectly granted.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

On the day Dhanya could finally eat after two days of fasting, he was not in a hurry — he spent the first part of the day studying scripture, the second meditating, and only in the third did he carefully prepare to go beg for food, and even then he first asked the Lord's permission before walking out into the city.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When you are hungry, tired, or in need, can your inward life still hold its shape — or does the body's urgency push your meditation, your study, and your stillness aside?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S16 — The Alms-Walk and the Snake-Entering-Its-Hole

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं धण्णे अणगारे समणेणं भगवया महावीरेणं अब्भणुण्णाए समाणे समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अंतियाओ सहसंबवणाओ उज्जाणाओ पडिणिक्खमइ, पडिणिक्खमित्ता अतुरियमचवलमसंभंते जुगंतरपलोयणाए दिट्ठीए पुरओ रियं सोहमाणे सोहमाणे जेणेव कागंदी णगरी तेणेव उवागच्छइ, उवागच्छित्ता कागंदीए णयरीए उच्च णीय-मज्झिमाइं कुलाइं घरसमुदाणस्स भिक्खायरियं अडमाणे आयंबिलं, पडिगाहेइ णो अणायंबिलं *जाव* णावकंखंति ।

तए णं से धण्णे अणगारे ताए अब्भणुयाए पययाए पयत्ताए पग्गहियाए एसणाए एसमाणे जइ भत्तं लभइ, तो पाणं ण लभइ, अह पाणं लभइ तो भत्तं ण लभइ ।

तए णं से धण्णे अणगारे अदीणे अविमणे अकलुसे अविसादी अपरितंतजोगी जयणघडणजोगचरित्ते अहापज्जत्तं समुदाणं पडिगाहेइ । पडिगाहित्ता कागंदीओ णयरीओ पडिणिक्खमइ पडिणिक्खमित्ता जेणेव समणे भगवं महावीरे तेणेव उवागच्छइ, उवागच्छित्ता समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अदूरसामंते गमणागमणाए पडिक्कमइ एसणमणेसणं आलोएइ, आलोएत्ता भत्तपाणं पडिदंसेइ ।

तए णं से धण्णे अणगारे समणेणं भगवया महावीरेणं अब्भणुण्णाए समाणे अमुच्छिए अगिद्धे अगढिए अणज्झोववण्णे बिलमिव पण्णगभूएणं अप्पाणेणं आहारं आहारेइ आहारित्ता संजमेण तवसा अप्पाणं भावेमाणे विहरइ । ॥3.A1.S16॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ dhaṇṇe aṇagāre samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtiyāo sahasaṃbavaṇāo ujjāṇāo paḍiṇikkhamai, paḍiṇikkhamittā aturiyamacavalamasaṃbhaṃte jugaṃtarapaloyaṇāe diṭṭhīe purao riyaṃ sohamāṇe sohamāṇe jeṇeva kāgaṃdī ṇagarī teṇeva uvāgacchai, uvāgacchittā kāgaṃdīe ṇayarīe ucca ṇīya-majjhimāiṃ kulāiṃ gharasamudāṇassa bhikkhāyariyaṃ aḍamāṇe āyaṃbilaṃ, paḍigāhei ṇo aṇāyaṃbilaṃ *jāva* ṇāvakaṃkhaṃti |

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre tāe abbhaṇuyāe payayāe payattāe paggahiyāe esaṇāe esamāṇe jai bhattaṃ labhai, to pāṇaṃ ṇa labhai, aha pāṇaṃ labhai to bhattaṃ ṇa labhai |

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre adīṇe avimaṇe akaluse avisādī aparitaṃtajogī jayaṇaghaḍaṇajogacaritte ahāpajjattaṃ samudāṇaṃ paḍigāhei | paḍigāhittā kāgaṃdīo ṇayarīo paḍiṇikkhamai paḍiṇikkhamittā jeṇeva samaṇe bhagavaṃ mahāvīre teṇeva uvāgacchai, uvāgacchittā samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa adūrasāmaṃte gamaṇāgamaṇāe paḍikkamai esaṇamaṇesaṇaṃ āloei, āloettā bhattapāṇaṃ paḍidaṃsei |

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre samaṇeṇaṃ bhagavayā mahāvīreṇaṃ abbhaṇuṇṇāe samāṇe amucchie agiddhe agaḍhie aṇajjhovavaṇṇe bilamiva paṇṇagabhūeṇaṃ appāṇeṇaṃ āhāraṃ āhārei āhārittā saṃjameṇa tavasā appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe viharai | || 3.A1.S16 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then the monk Dhanya, with the permission of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira granted, set forth from the presence of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, leaving the Sahasramravan grove. Having set forth, without haste, without restlessness, without agitation, with his gaze cast forward upon the path no farther than the length of a yoke before him, he came to the city of Kakandi. Reaching it, he walked the alms-rounds through the high, the low, and the middling households of Kakandi, gathering from a community of homes; he accepted only *ayambil* food, not non-*ayambil*, and so on, only that which no other person desired. As he sought alms with that prescribed seeking — properly conducted, perfectly carried out, fully held up to the rule — sometimes if he obtained food he obtained no water, and if he obtained water he obtained no food. Yet the monk Dhanya remained un-dejected, un-disturbed in mind, un-clouded, un-saddened, untiring in his discipline, walking with conduct fully woven of careful effort; and he accepted what was sufficient as a *samudana*-collection. Having accepted it, he set forth from the city of Kakandi and came to where the Ascetic Lord Mahavira was. Coming there, he stood at a fitting distance from the Ascetic Lord Mahavira and made the *pratikramana* (formal confession) for the going and the coming, declared everything that had been correctly and incorrectly received in the alms-rounds, and showed the food and water before the Lord. Then the monk Dhanya, with the permission of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, took the food un-fascinated, un-greedy, un-clinging, un-attached — taking it into himself as a snake enters its hole — and having eaten, he dwelt suffusing his soul with restraint and austerity.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*sahasaṃbavaṇāo ujjāṇāo* = from the Sahasramravan ("Thousand-Mango") grove (the standard *udyana* outside Kakandi where Mahavira halted) | *paḍiṇikkhamai* = sets forth, departs | *aturiyaṃ acavalaṃ asaṃbhaṃte* = without haste, without restlessness, without agitation | *jugaṃtarapaloyaṇāe diṭṭhīe* = with the eye-gaze cast no farther than the distance of a yoke ahead (a yoke is roughly four cubits — about six feet — and this is the prescribed distance an alms-walking monk's gaze should reach so as not to step on any creature) | *purao riyaṃ sohamāṇe* = examining the path before him as he goes | *kāgaṃdī ṇagarī* = the city of Kakandi | *ucca ṇīya majjhimāiṃ kulāiṃ* = high, low, and middling households | *gharasamudāṇassa* = of a community of homes | *bhikkhāyariyaṃ aḍamāṇe* = walking the alms-rounds | *āyaṃbilaṃ paḍigāhei* = he accepts only *ayambil* food | *ṇo aṇāyaṃbilaṃ* = not what is non-*ayambil* | *jāva ṇāvakaṃkhaṃti* = and so on (through the rest of the nine conditions), down to "what no others desire" | *abbhaṇuyāe* = with the prescribed permission, properly authorised | *payayāe payattāe* = correctly carried out, properly performed | *paggahiyāe* = held up firmly, sustained | *esaṇāe esamāṇe* = seeking with the rule of *eshana* (the canonical rule of inspecting alms for purity at every stage) | *jai bhattaṃ labhai* = if he obtains food | *to pāṇaṃ ṇa labhai* = he does not obtain water | *aha pāṇaṃ labhai* = if he obtains water | *to bhattaṃ ṇa labhai* = he does not obtain food | *adīṇe* = un-dejected, not pitiable | *avimaṇe* = un-disturbed in mind | *akaluse* = un-clouded, without inner murkiness | *avisādī* = un-saddened, without dejection | *aparitaṃtajogī* = un-tiring in his discipline (literally "of un-fatigued *yoga*") | *jayaṇaghaḍaṇajogacaritte* = whose conduct is a constant working-together of careful effort and right exertion | *ahāpajjattaṃ samudāṇaṃ paḍigāhei* = he accepts the collection only as much as is sufficient | *adūrasāmaṃte* = at a fitting distance, neither too far nor too near | *gamaṇāgamaṇāe paḍikkamai* = makes the *pratikramana* (formal repentance) for the going and coming | *esaṇaṃ aṇesaṇaṃ āloei* = he discloses what was rightly received and what was incorrectly received (full transparency before the Lord) | *bhattapāṇaṃ paḍidaṃsei* = shows the food and the drink | *amucchie* = un-fascinated, without infatuation | *agiddhe* = un-greedy | *agaḍhie* = un-clinging | *aṇajjhovavaṇṇe* = un-attached, not absorbed | *bilamiva paṇṇagabhūeṇaṃ appāṇeṇaṃ* = with one's whole self like a snake entering its hole | *āhāraṃ āhārei* = he eats the food | *saṃjameṇa tavasā appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe viharai* = he dwells suffusing the soul with restraint and austerity

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra carries one of the most luminous and beloved similes in the entire Jain canon: a monk eating food *bilamiva paṇṇagabhūeṇaṃ appāṇeṇaṃ* — "with the self like a snake entering its hole." The image is exact and unforgettable. When a snake enters its hole, it does not pause at the entrance to taste the air, to examine the smell, to consider whether the rim is pleasant or rough; it goes straight in for one purpose only — the protection of the body. The walls of the hole touch every part of its body, but the snake has no relationship with those walls; the walls are simply the path. So too the monk eats: the food touches every part of the tongue, but the tongue is not engaged with the food — the food is simply the path along which a small measure of nourishment passes into the body so that the body may keep doing the work of liberation. Eating is reduced to a function — like breathing, like the heartbeat — without becoming an experience.

The setting of the alms-walk is described with two perfect details. First, *jugantara-paloyana* — "yoke-distance gaze." The monk's eyes do not roam; they look down upon the path immediately ahead, no farther than the length of a yoke (about six feet), so that every footstep can be placed where no creature will be crushed. The discipline of seeing-before-stepping is a continuous, moving meditation. Second, the threefold *aturiyaṃ acavalaṃ asaṃbhaṃte* — "without haste, without restlessness, without agitation" — repeated here from the previous sutra. Even as he walks among the houses of a city, the inner stillness of meditation does not break.

Then comes the consequence of his nine-fold vow. The text records, with a sober single sentence, the practical outcome of the most extreme abhigraha in the canon: *"if he obtained food, he did not obtain water; if he obtained water, he did not obtain food."* The discarded portion that no other beggar wanted, that the householder was about to throw away, that fell within the strict bounds of *ayambil* and *samsrishta* — such a portion was rare. To find both food and water that fit all conditions in the same alms-round was rarer still. So Dhanya often returned with only one of the two. He often returned with very little.

And here lies the spiritual stroke of the sutra. The text does not say he returned exhausted, hungry, or downcast. It uses five precise negations: *adīṇe* (not dejected), *avimaṇe* (not troubled in mind), *akaluse* (not clouded), *avisādī* (not saddened), *aparitaṃtajogī* (not weary in his discipline). These five together describe the inner fortress of a true ascetic. The body may have walked many streets and received almost nothing; the mind has not registered failure, because there was no expectation. He took what came; if it was sufficient as *samudana*, he stopped. If it was not, he stopped anyway. The walk itself was complete.

His return to the Lord follows the canonical four-step procedure: he stands at a respectful distance, performs *pratikramana* (formal repentance) for any inadvertent fault during the going and the coming, *aloyana* (full transparent disclosure) of what was rightly and what was wrongly received in alms, and then *pratidarshana* — showing the actual food and water in the bowl to the teacher for inspection and approval. Only after these four — *gamana-agamana-pratikramana, eshana-aneshana-aloyana, bhakta-pana-pratidarshana*, and the Lord's permission — does the monk eat. The eating itself is not the act of a hungry man taking nourishment; it is a final ritual completion of an act that began with the teacher's word and ends with the teacher's seal.

The four words for non-attachment in eating are also worth dwelling on. *Amurchhita* — un-fascinated, with no fascination for taste; *agriddha* — un-greedy, with no urge to take more; *agradhita* — un-clinging, not lingering on the experience; *anajjhopapanna* — un-absorbed, not allowing the mind to merge into the act. These four together draw the limit between an eating that nourishes the soul's path and an eating that nourishes the soul's bondage. Most beings, even pious ones, slip somewhere along this scale. Dhanya did not slip.

The Gujarati commentary makes a very fine point here. It says: the snake-and-the-hole simile is not a renunciation of *eating*; it is a renunciation of *flavour-seeking*, of *body-pampering*, of the silent attitude that "I am what I taste." The body must be sustained — Dhanya, after all, eats. But the body must be sustained for the sake of *samyama* (restraint) and *tapas* (austerity), not for the sake of itself. Just as the snake enters the hole only to keep the body safe, the monk takes food only to keep the path going.

A small but moving detail closes the sutra: *saṃjameṇa tavasā appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe viharai* — "he dwelt suffusing the soul with restraint and austerity." The verb *bhāvemāṇa* — "suffusing, soaking, saturating" — is the same verb used throughout the canon for the highest form of inward practice. The monk does not merely *do* austerity; he *becomes* austerity. The discipline is no longer something he performs upon himself; it is the colour of his being.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Dhanya walked through the city, gaze fixed on the ground in front of his feet, taking only food no one else wanted; sometimes he got food without water, sometimes water without food, but he was never sad or upset; he came back to the Lord, showed everything he had received, and ate the food the way a snake enters its hole — straight, without lingering, without enjoying it as taste, simply taking what the body needed and continuing the path.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

Could you eat the next thing you eat the way a snake enters its hole — with no attention to taste, no expectation of pleasure, only the inward thought, "this is fuel for the work I am here to do"?

---

### The Body Reduced to Bone — Dhanya's Tapas

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S17 — Dhanya Muni's Life of Tapas; the General Picture of His Emaciated Body

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं समणे भगवं महावीरे अण्णया कयाइ कागंदीओ णगरीओ सहसंबवणाओ उज्जाणाओ पडिणिक्खमइ । पडिणिक्खमित्ता बहिया जणवय विहारं विहरइ ।

तए णं से धण्णे अणगारे समणस्स भगवओ महारीरस्स तहारूवाणं थेराणं अंतिए सामाइयमाइयाइं एक्कारस अंगाइं अहिज्जइ । अहिज्जित्ता संजमेण तवसा अप्पाणं भावेमाणे विहरइ । तए णं से धण्णे अणगारे तेणं उरालेणं विउलेणं पयत्तेणं पग्गहिएणं कल्लाणेणं सिवेणं धण्णेणं मंगल्लेणंसस्सिरीएणं उदग्गेणं उत्तेणं उत्तमेणं उदारेणं महाणुभागेणं तवोकम्मेणं सुक्के लुक्खे णिम्मंसे अट्ठिचम्मच्छिरत्ताए किडिकिडियाभूए किसे धमणिसंतए जाए यावि होत्था, जीवंजीवेण गच्छइ, जीवंजीवेण चिट्ठइ, भासं भासित्ता वि गिलाइ, भासं भासमाणे गिलाइ, भासिस्सामीति गिलाइ । से जहाणामए कट्ठसगडिया इ वा, पत्तसगडिया इवा पत्त तिल भंडग सगडिया इ वा ए रंडकट्ठसगडिया इ वा इंगालसगडिया इ वा उण्हे दिण्णा सुक्का समाणी ससद्द गच्छइ, ससद्द चिट्ठइ, एवामेव धण्णे वि अणगारे ससद्दं गच्छइ, ससद्दं चिट्ठइ, उवचिए तवेणं, अवचिए मंस-सोणिएणं, हुयासणे विव भासरासि पडिच्छण्णे, तवेणं, तेएणं, तव-तेयसिरीए अईव अईव उवसोभेमाणे उवसोभेमाणे चिट्ठइ । ॥3.A1.S17॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ samaṇe bhagavaṃ mahāvīre aṇṇayā kayāi kāgaṃdīo ṇagarīo sahasaṃbavaṇāo ujjāṇāo paḍiṇikkhamai | paḍiṇikkhamittā bahiyā jaṇavaya vihāraṃ viharai |

tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa tahārūvāṇaṃ therāṇaṃ aṃtie sāmāiyamāiyāiṃ ekkārasa aṃgāiṃ ahijjai | ahijjittā saṃjameṇa tavasā appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe viharai | tae ṇaṃ se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre teṇaṃ urāleṇaṃ viuleṇaṃ payatteṇaṃ paggahieṇaṃ kallāṇeṇaṃ siveṇaṃ dhaṇṇeṇaṃ maṃgalleṇaṃ sassirīeṇaṃ udaggeṇaṃ utteṇaṃ uttameṇaṃ udāreṇaṃ mahāṇubhāgeṇaṃ tavokammeṇaṃ sukke lukkhe ṇimmaṃse aṭṭhicammacchirattāe kiḍikiḍiyābhūe kise dhamaṇisaṃtae jāe yāvi hotthā, jīvaṃjīveṇa gacchai, jīvaṃjīveṇa ciṭṭhai, bhāsaṃ bhāsittā vi gilāi, bhāsaṃ bhāsamāṇe gilāi, bhāsissāmīti gilāi | se jahāṇāmae kaṭṭhasagaḍiyā i vā, pattasagaḍiyā i vā patta tila bhaṃḍaga sagaḍiyā i vā e raṃḍakaṭṭhasagaḍiyā i vā iṃgālasagaḍiyā i vā uṇhe diṇṇā sukkā samāṇī sasadda gacchai, sasadda ciṭṭhai, evāmeva dhaṇṇe vi aṇagāre sasaddaṃ gacchai, sasaddaṃ ciṭṭhai, uvacie taveṇaṃ, avacie maṃsa-soṇieṇaṃ, huyāsaṇe viva bhāsarāsi paḍicchaṇṇe, taveṇaṃ, teeṇaṃ, tava-teyasirīe aīva aīva uvasobhemāṇe uvasobhemāṇe ciṭṭhai | || 3.A1.S17 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

After some time the Ascetic Lord Mahavira set out from the city of Kakandi, leaving the Sahasramravan grove, and travelled abroad through the outlying countries on his wandering tour. Then the monk Dhanya, in the presence of those venerable elder monks who were the appropriate teachers of the Ascetic Lord Mahavira, studied the eleven Angas beginning with the *Samayika*. Having studied them, he dwelt suffusing his soul with restraint and austerity. Then by that great, vast, properly performed, fully sustained, beneficial, peace-giving, blessed, auspicious, glorious, exalted, lofty, supreme, generous, mighty austere practice, the monk Dhanya became dry, rough, fleshless — only bone-and-skin remaining, his frame creaking and rattling, gaunt, with veins standing visible. He walked alive only by the soul-force; he stood still alive only by the soul-force; even after he had finished speaking he sank into weariness; while he was speaking he sank into weariness; even at the thought "I am about to speak" he sank into weariness. Just as a wagon piled with dry firewood, or with dry leaves, or with dry leaves and sesame and other goods, or with castor-wood, or with charcoal — when it has stood in the sun and dried thoroughly — moves with creaking sound and stands still with creaking sound, even so the monk Dhanya moved with creaking sound and stood still with creaking sound. Built up by austerity, drained of flesh and blood, he was like a fire-pit covered over with a heap of ash — by his austerity, by his inner radiance, by the splendour of austerity-glory, he shone forth ever more and more, and so he stood.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*aṇṇayā kayāi* = at some other time, after a while | *kāgaṃdīo ṇagarīo* = from the city of Kakandi | *sahasaṃbavaṇāo ujjāṇāo* = from the Sahasramravan grove | *paḍiṇikkhamai* = sets out, departs | *bahiyā jaṇavaya vihāraṃ viharai* = travels abroad in *vihāra* (the wandering tour) through the outlying countries | *tahārūvāṇaṃ therāṇaṃ aṃtie* = in the presence of the duly-qualified elder monks | *sāmāiyamāiyāiṃ ekkārasa aṃgāiṃ* = the eleven Angas beginning with the *Sāmāyika* (the first Anga, the *Acaranga Sutra*, opens with the chapter on *Sāmāyika*) | *ahijjai* = he studies | *teṇaṃ urāleṇaṃ* = by that great | *viuleṇaṃ* = vast | *payatteṇaṃ* = properly performed | *paggahieṇaṃ* = fully held up, sustained | *kallāṇeṇaṃ* = beneficial | *siveṇaṃ* = peace-giving | *dhaṇṇeṇaṃ* = blessed | *maṃgalleṇaṃ* = auspicious | *sassirīeṇaṃ* = full of inner glory | *udaggeṇaṃ* = exalted | *utteṇaṃ uttameṇaṃ* = lofty, supreme | *udāreṇaṃ* = generous, magnanimous | *mahāṇubhāgeṇaṃ* = of
