great spiritual power | *tavokammeṇaṃ* = by (this) austere practice | *sukke* = dry | *lukkhe* = rough, parched | *ṇimmaṃse* = without flesh | *aṭṭhicamma-chirattāe* = (consisting only) of bone and skin | *kiḍikiḍiyābhūe* = come to a state of creaking and rattling (the body's joints audibly creaking) | *kise* = thin, gaunt | *dhamaṇisaṃtae* = with the bellows-veins (the great veins) standing visible | *jīvaṃjīveṇa gacchai* = he moves only by the soul's life-force | *jīvaṃjīveṇa ciṭṭhai* = he stands only by the soul's life-force | *bhāsaṃ bhāsittā vi gilāi* = even after speaking, he sinks into exhaustion | *bhāsaṃ bhāsamāṇe gilāi* = while speaking, he sinks into exhaustion | *bhāsissāmīti gilāi* = even at the thought "I shall speak," he sinks into exhaustion | *kaṭṭhasagaḍiyā* = a wagon (small cart) of firewood | *pattasagaḍiyā* = a wagon of (dry) leaves | *patta-tila-bhaṃḍaga-sagaḍiyā* = a wagon of dry leaves, sesame seeds, and (small) goods | *e-raṃḍakaṭṭhasagaḍiyā* = a wagon of castor-plant wood | *iṃgālasagaḍiyā* = a wagon of charcoal | *uṇhe diṇṇā sukkā samāṇī* = (when) placed in the sun and become thoroughly dry | *sasaddaṃ gacchai* = moves with creaking sound | *sasaddaṃ ciṭṭhai* = stands with creaking sound | *uvacie taveṇaṃ* = built up by austerity | *avacie maṃsa-soṇieṇaṃ* = drained of flesh and blood | *huyāsaṇe viva* = like a fire-pit, like the sacrificial fire | *bhāsarāsi paḍicchaṇṇe* = covered over with a heap of ash | *taveṇaṃ teeṇaṃ tava-teyasirīe* = by austerity, by inner radiance, by the splendour of the glory of austerity-radiance | *aīva aīva uvasobhemāṇe* = ever more and more shining forth | *ciṭṭhai* = he stood, he abided

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra opens the most striking section of Dhanya's chapter — and indeed one of the most carefully observed depictions of a monk's body in the entire Jain canon. Mahavira moves on to other regions; the small drama of Dhanya's daily walks in Kakandi is over. Dhanya now studies the entire eleven Angas under the elder monks of Mahavira's order, mastering the full body of canonical scripture. The phrase *sāmāiyamāiyāiṃ ekkārasa aṃgāiṃ* — "the eleven Angas beginning with the *Sāmāyika*" — gives the precise scope: the entire surviving canon, beginning with the chapter on equanimity and proceeding through the whole sequence. To study all eleven Angas while simultaneously living the lifelong nine-fold abhigraha is a feat of almost incomprehensible discipline.

Then comes the description that has made this chapter beloved across centuries. The text piles eleven adjectives upon his austerity — *udāra* (great), *vipula* (vast), *prayatta* (well-performed), *prgraha* (sustained), *kalyāṇa* (beneficial), *shiva* (peace-giving), *dhanya* (blessed), *māṅgalya* (auspicious), *sa-shrī* (full of inner glory), *udagra* (exalted), *uttama* (supreme) — before naming the bodily consequence: he became *sukka* (dry), *lukkha* (parched), *nirmaṃsa* (fleshless), reduced to *aṭṭhi-camma* (bone-and-skin), so worn that his joints creaked when he moved, gaunt and hollow, the great veins standing out beneath the skin like cords drawn tight on a frame.

The threefold image of weariness in speech is one of the most poignant in the canon: *bhāsaṃ bhāsittā gilāi* — even after he has finished speaking, he sinks; *bhāsaṃ bhāsamāṇe gilāi* — while speaking, he sinks; *bhāsissāmīti gilāi* — even at the bare thought "I am about to speak," he sinks. The body's reserves are so thin that the act of forming a sentence draws on what is barely there. And yet, the *jīvanjīva* — the bare life-force of the soul — keeps him walking, keeps him standing.

The simile of the wagon is the masterstroke. A wagon piled with dry wood, dry leaves, castor-stalks, or charcoal — a wagon that has stood in the sun until every fibre has lost its moisture — does not roll smoothly; every joint of its frame creaks against every other. So too Dhanya's body. The bones, no longer cushioned by flesh, no longer lubricated by ample tissue, audibly creak as he walks. The simile is so concrete that the reader can almost hear it.

But the second simile — the fire-pit covered with ash — turns the picture inside out. To a casual observer, ash on top of a fire-pit looks merely grey, dead, finished. But anyone who has tended a fire knows that beneath the surface of ash lies a heat that can rekindle the world. Dhanya's body, to the eye, was ash; but within him burned the *teja* — the inner radiance — of austerity. The soul did not weaken as the body did; the soul, strangely, *strengthened* in inverse proportion. Hence the closing phrase, *aīva aīva uvasobhemāṇe* — "ever more and more shining forth, ever more and more shining forth" — repeated twice, as if no single statement of his glory could hold it. The Gujarati commentary notes this precisely: as the body wore away, his inner light grew like a covered flame, and on his face the radiance of austerity stood like fire visible through the thin veil of skin.

There is a deeper teaching here, and the sutra-makers do not let us miss it. The body, in the Jain ethic, is not the self. To watch the body wear away while the soul brightens is not tragedy; it is the very design of *tapas* — austerity — which means literally "that which heats" or "that which burns away." What is being burned away is not Dhanya; what is being burned away is the karma that has bound Dhanya for innumerable lives. The body is the furnace; the soul is the gold being purified. When the gold is fully bright, the furnace's wear is no loss. The wagon may creak; the fire's ash may grow heavy; but the inner flame is at last what it always was — the soul, free and luminous.

A final theological note from the *Vivechana*: the writer points out that what shines through this sutra is not the *bodily* feat (a body can be starved by many means) but the inward state — *antaḥkaraṇa* (inner instrument) so settled in restraint and austerity that the body's condition no longer disturbs it. Without that inward state, austerity becomes mere bodily torment and produces no spiritual fruit. With it, every creak of the bone is the sound of karma falling away.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Mahavira moved on to other regions; Dhanya stayed and studied all the scriptures while continuing his fierce austerities, until his body became so dry and thin that only bone and skin were left and his joints actually creaked when he walked, like an old wagon of dry wood; but inside, his soul shone brighter and brighter, like a fire hidden under a heap of ash.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

If your body grew weaker but your inner being grew brighter, would you feel that you were being lost — or that you were finally becoming visible?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S18 — Dhanya's Feet and Toes

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स पायाणं अयमेयारूवे तवरूवलावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए सुक्कछल्ली इ वा कट्ठपाउया इ वा जरग्ग ओवाहणा इ वा, एवामेव धण्णस्स अणगारस्स पाया सुक्का लुक्खा णिम्मंसा अट्ठिचम्मच्छिरत्ताए पण्णायंति, णो चेव णं मंससोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स पायंगुलियाणं अयमेयारूवे तवरूवलावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए कलसंगलिया इ वा मुग्गसंगलिया इ वा माससंगलिया इ वा, तरुणिया छिण्णा, उण्हे दिण्णा, सुक्का समाणी मिलायमाणी चिट्ठइ, एवामेव धण्णस्स पायंगुलियाओ सुक्काओ लुक्खाओ णिम्मंसाओ अट्ठिचम्मच्छिरत्ताए पण्णायंति, णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए । ॥3.A1.S18॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa pāyāṇaṃ ayameyārūve tavarūvalāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae sukkachallī i vā kaṭṭhapāuyā i vā jaragga ovāhaṇā i vā, evāmeva dhaṇṇassa aṇagārassa pāyā sukkā lukkhā ṇimmaṃsā aṭṭhicammacchirattāe paṇṇāyaṃti, ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsasoṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa pāyaṃguliyāṇaṃ ayameyārūve tavarūvalāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae kalasaṃguliyā i vā muggasaṃguliyā i vā māsasaṃguliyā i vā, taruṇiyā chiṇṇā, uṇhe diṇṇā, sukkā samāṇī milāyamāṇī ciṭṭhai, evāmeva dhaṇṇassa pāyaṃguliyāo sukkāo lukkhāo ṇimmaṃsāo aṭṭhicammacchirattāe paṇṇāyaṃti, ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe | || 3.A1.S18 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's feet had taken this form — just like the dried bark of a tree, or a wooden sandal, or an old worn-out leather sandal: in just that way the monk Dhanya's feet appeared dry, parched, fleshless, only bone-and-skin, and not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's toes had taken this form — just like the slender pods of *kala* (a kind of legume), or *mung* pods, or *urad* pods, when they have been plucked while still tender and laid out in the sun, becoming dried and shrivelled: in just that way the monk Dhanya's toes appeared dry, parched, fleshless, only bone-and-skin, and not at all flesh-and-blood.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa* = of the monk Dhanya | *pāyāṇaṃ* = of the (two) feet | *ayameyārūve* = of this kind, of this form | *tavarūvalāvaṇṇe* = the austerity-shaped beauty (the loveliness given to the body by tapas) | *hotthā* = was, had become | *sukkachallī* = dried bark of a tree | *kaṭṭhapāuyā* = a wooden sandal (pāduka) | *jaragga ovāhaṇā* = an old worn-out (leather) shoe / sandal | *evāmeva* = exactly so | *pāyā* = the feet | *sukkā lukkhā ṇimmaṃsā* = dry, parched, fleshless | *aṭṭhicamma-chirattāe paṇṇāyaṃti* = appeared as bone-and-skin only | *ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsasoṇiyattāe* = and not at all as flesh-and-blood | *pāyaṃguliyāṇaṃ* = of the toes (foot-fingers) | *kalasaṃguliyā* = pod / slender finger of *kala*-legume | *muggasaṃguliyā* = pod of *mung* (green gram) | *māsasaṃguliyā* = pod of *māṣa* (urad / black gram) | *taruṇiyā chiṇṇā* = plucked while still tender | *uṇhe diṇṇā* = laid out in the sun | *sukkā samāṇī milāyamāṇī* = becoming dry and shrivelled | *ciṭṭhai* = stands, remains

**5. COMMENTARY**

From this sutra onward, the scripture begins a careful, head-to-toe inventory of Dhanya's body, member by member. The opening phrase of each comparison is striking: *tava-rūva-lāvaṇya* — literally "the loveliness shaped by austerity." The choice of word is deliberate. *Lāvaṇya* normally means physical beauty, the kind that draws admiration in worldly life. By calling Dhanya's bony, fleshless body *tapas-shaped lāvaṇya*, the sutra-makers are making a quiet revolution: the new beauty of an austere monk is not the rounded fullness of well-fed limbs but the stripped clarity of bone made visible. The body has become a transparency through which something higher shines.

The similes for the feet are three, drawn from common village objects: dried tree-bark, a wooden sandal, an old worn leather sandal. All three are flat, hardened, surface-only — no living softness inside. Dhanya's feet had become like that.

The similes for the toes are even more vivid. The pods of *kala*, *mung*, and *urad* — three legumes everyone in his world would know — when plucked tender and left in the sun, shrivel into wrinkled little curves: dry, brittle, hollow. So too were Dhanya's toes. The repeated formula at the close of every comparison — "bone-and-skin only, and not at all flesh-and-blood" — drives home the central point with the patience of a litany. The eye that observes Dhanya is to be slowed and steadied; nothing of him as a normal body is to be missed; we are to walk over him with our gaze the way a careful pilgrim walks the perimeter of a shrine.

The Gujarati commentary notes that the text is teaching us how to read these descriptions: not as a record of suffering, but as a record of liberation made visible in flesh. Each shrivelled limb is a chapter in the falling-away of karma. The body's loss is the soul's gain.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Dhanya's feet had become as dry as old tree-bark, a wooden sandal, or a worn leather shoe — just bone and skin; his toes had shrivelled like green-gram pods left out to dry in the sun.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

What in your life is currently being asked to dry up — and could you see that drying not as a loss, but as a clearing of weight you no longer need to carry?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S19 — Dhanya's Shins, Knees, and Thighs

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स जंघाणं अयमेयारूवे तवरूवलावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए काकजंघा इ वा, कंक जंघा इ वा, ढेणियालियाजंघा इ वा ए वामेव धण्णस्स अणगारस्स जंघाओ सुक्काओ लुक्खाओ णिम्मंसाओ अट्ठिचम्मच्छिरत्ताए पण्णायंति, णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स जाणूणं अयमेयारूवे तवरूवलावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए कालिपोरे इ वा मयूरपोरे इ वा ढेणियालियापोरे इ वा ए वामेव धण्णस्स अणगारस्स जाणू सुक्का लुक्खा णिम्मंसा अट्ठिचम्मच्छिरत्ताए पण्णायंति, णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारसस ऊरुस्स अयमेयारूवे तवरूवलावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए सामकरिल्ले इ वा बोरकरिल्ले इ वा सल्लइकरिल्ले इ वा, सामलिकरिल्ले इ वा, तरुणिए उण्हे दिण्णे, सुक्के समाणे मिलायमाणे चिट्ठइ, एवामेव धण्णस्स अणगारस्स ऊरु सुक्का लुक्खा णिम्मंसा अट्ठिचम्मच्छिरत्ताए पण्णायंति, णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए । ॥3.A1.S19॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa jaṃghāṇaṃ ayameyārūve tavarūvalāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae kākajaṃghā i vā, kaṃka jaṃghā i vā, ḍheṇiyāliyājaṃghā i vā e vāmeva dhaṇṇassa aṇagārassa jaṃghāo sukkāo lukkhāo ṇimmaṃsāo aṭṭhicammacchirattāe paṇṇāyaṃti, ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa jāṇūṇaṃ ayameyārūve tavarūvalāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae kālipore i vā mayūrapore i vā ḍheṇiyāliyāpore i vā e vāmeva dhaṇṇassa aṇagārassa jāṇū sukkā lukkhā ṇimmaṃsā aṭṭhicammacchirattāe paṇṇāyaṃti, ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa ūrussa ayameyārūve tavarūvalāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae sāmakarille i vā borakarille i vā sallaikarille i vā, sāmalikarille i vā, taruṇie uṇhe diṇṇe, sukke samāṇe milāyamāṇe ciṭṭhai, evāmeva dhaṇṇassa aṇagārassa ūru sukkā lukkhā ṇimmaṃsā aṭṭhicammacchirattāe paṇṇāyaṃti, ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe | || 3.A1.S19 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's shins had taken this form — just like the leg of a crow, the leg of a heron, or the leg of a *dheniyaliya* bird: in just that way the monk Dhanya's shins appeared dry, parched, fleshless, only bone-and-skin, and not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's knees had taken this form — just like the wing-joint of a *kali*-bird, the wing-joint of a peacock, or the wing-joint of a *dheniyaliya*-bird: in just that way the monk Dhanya's knees appeared dry, parched, fleshless, only bone-and-skin, and not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's thighs had taken this form — just like the tender shoots of the *shyama*, *bora*, *shallaki*, or *shamali* tree, plucked while still soft, laid in the sun, becoming dried and shrivelled: in just that way the monk Dhanya's thighs appeared dry, parched, fleshless, only bone-and-skin, and not at all flesh-and-blood.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*jaṃghāṇaṃ* = of the (two) shins | *kākajaṃghā* = the (thin) leg of a crow | *kaṃka jaṃghā* = the leg of a *kanka*-bird (a long-legged wading bird, possibly a heron or stork) | *ḍheṇiyāliyājaṃghā* = the leg of a *dheniyaliya*-bird (a small bird whose precise modern identification ⚠️ is uncertain — the Gujarati commentary preserves only the bird-name as a known type) | *jāṇūṇaṃ* = of the (two) knees | *kālipore* = the wing-joint of a *kali*-bird (the *parva* — joint, knot — of a particular bird; the Gujarati commentary glosses *pora* as the joint at the wing or leg-bend of the bird; ⚠️ exact bird-species uncertain) | *mayūrapore* = the wing-joint / leg-joint of a peacock | *ḍheṇiyāliyāpore* = the wing-joint of a *dheniyaliya*-bird | *ūrussa* = of the (two) thighs | *sāmakarille* = the tender shoot of the *shyāma*-tree | *borakarille* = the tender shoot of the *bora* (jujube) tree | *sallaikarille* = the tender shoot of the *shallakī*-tree (frankincense-tree, *Boswellia*) | *sāmalikarille* = the tender shoot of the *shālmali*-tree (silk-cotton tree) | *taruṇie uṇhe diṇṇe* = (the shoot) plucked while tender, placed in the sun | *sukke samāṇe milāyamāṇe ciṭṭhai* = becoming dry and shrivelled, remains so

**5. COMMENTARY**

The march of the inventory continues upward — from the toes to the feet to the shins, and now to the knees and thighs. With each new member, the imagery shifts to a new register, drawing from a fresh corner of the village world. For the shins, the comparison is to the legs of birds — the crow, the *kanka*, the *dheniyaliya* — all known in the canon's world for the thin, dried-stick appearance of their lower limbs. The shin of a starving crow is famously bony; that is what Dhanya's shins had become.

For the knees, the simile shifts to the joints — *pora* — of birds. The peacock's leg-joint is a familiar image, slim and dry beneath its feathers. Dhanya's knees, no longer cushioned by the muscles that ordinarily round them out, had become like those small, hard joints.

For the thighs, the comparison turns from animals to plants: the tender new shoots of four trees — *shyama*, *bora* (jujube), *shallaki* (frankincense), and *shamali* (silk-cotton) — all of which produce delicate green shoots in their season. When such a shoot is plucked young and laid in the sun, it shrivels into a small, dry stick. So too Dhanya's thighs.

The bird-imagery and the plant-imagery together carry a quiet teaching. The body's parts are being compared not to anything precious, not to anything the world admires, but to common bones, joints, and dried shoots — the kind of objects nobody picks up, nobody saves, nobody cherishes. The text is saying, plainly: this is no longer a body to be desired. It is a body that has stepped beyond the field of desire.

The Gujarati commentary acknowledges that the precise modern identification of certain bird-names — particularly *dheniyaliya* and *kali* — has been lost, since these were local terms current in the speech of the era and may no longer correspond to known species. ⚠️ The general sense — small, thin-legged or thin-jointed birds — is what the simile preserves. The reverence of the translator is to keep the named original in the *anvayartha* and to convey the intent without forcing a false precision.

The closing phrase recurs unchanged: *bone-and-skin only, and not at all flesh-and-blood*. Through repetition, the phrase begins to sound less like description and more like a refrain of liberation — a steady drum-beat under the body's vanishing.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Dhanya's shins were as thin as a crow's leg; his knees stuck out like the joint of a peacock's leg; his thighs had shrivelled like green tree-shoots left in the sun — just bone and skin in every part.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

If, layer by layer, the world's claims on your body and life were stripped away, what would you find still standing — and would you welcome that, or fear it?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S20 — Dhanya's Waist, Belly, Ribs, Spine, and Chest

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स कडिपत्तस्स इमेयारूवे तवरूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए उट्टपादे इ वा जरग्गवपाए इ वा, महिसपाए इ वा एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स उयरभायणस्स इमेयारूवे तव रूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए सुक्क दिए(दीविए) इ वा, भज्जणय कभल्ले इ वा कट्ठकोलंबए इ वा एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स पासुलिय-कडयाणं इमेयारूवे तवरूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए थासयावली इ वा, पाणावली इ वा, मुंडावली इ वा एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स पिट्ठि-करंडयाणं इमेयारूवे तवरूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहा णामए कण्णावली इ वा गोलावली इ वा वट्टयावली इ वा एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स उरकडयस्स इमेयारूवे तवरूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहा णामए चित्तकट्टरे इ वा वीयणपत्ते इ वा तालियंटपत्ते इ वा एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए । ॥3.A1.S20॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa kaḍipattassa imeyārūve tavarūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae uṭṭapāde i vā jaraggavapāe i vā, mahisapāe i vā evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa uyarabhāyaṇassa imeyārūve tava rūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae sukka die (dīvie) i vā, bhajjaṇaya kabhalle i vā kaṭṭhakolaṃbae i vā evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa pāsuliya-kaḍayāṇaṃ imeyārūve tavarūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae thāsayāvalī i vā, pāṇāvalī i vā, muṃḍāvalī i vā evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa piṭṭhi-karaṃḍayāṇaṃ imeyārūve tavarūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahā ṇāmae kaṇṇāvalī i vā golāvalī i vā vaṭṭayāvalī i vā evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa urakaḍayassa imeyārūve tavarūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahā ṇāmae cittakaṭṭare i vā vīyaṇapatte i vā tāliyaṃṭapatte i vā evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe | || 3.A1.S20 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's waist had taken this form — just like the foot of a camel, the foot of an old worn-out ox, or the foot of a buffalo: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's belly-vessel had taken this form — just like an empty dried water-skin, or the iron pan in which gram is roasted, or a wooden trough for kneading flour: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's ribs had taken this form — just like a row of metal cups, or a row of (small) bowls, or a row of (clay) head-rings — they could be plainly counted: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's spine had taken this form — just like a string of pierced beads, or a string of small balls, or a string of round playthings: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's chest had taken this form — just like the underside of a bamboo basket, like a fan made of bamboo strips, or like a fan made of palm-leaf: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*kaḍipattassa* = of the waist / hip-region | *uṭṭapāde* = the foot of a camel | *jaraggavapāe* = the foot of an old worn-out ox (a *jaraggava* — aged bullock) | *mahisapāe* = the foot of a buffalo | *uyarabhāyaṇassa* = of the belly (literally "the belly-vessel") | *sukka die / dīvie* = a dried-up *masaka* (water-skin) | *bhajjaṇaya kabhalle* = the iron pan / earthen pot used for parching grain | *kaṭṭhakolaṃbae* = the wooden trough used for kneading flour or for receiving dough | *pāsuliya-kaḍayāṇaṃ* = of the rib-bones (the bands of ribs) | *thāsayāvalī* = a row of metal cups (the *thāsaka* — a cup-like vessel) | *pāṇāvalī* = a row of (small) drinking-vessels | *muṃḍāvalī* = a row of (clay) ring-tops or rounded clay objects (the Gujarati commentary indicates a row of small rounded ornaments) | *piṭṭhi-karaṃḍayāṇaṃ* = of the spine (literally "the back-basket / back-spool") | *kaṇṇāvalī* = a string of pierced beads | *golāvalī* = a string of small balls | *vaṭṭayāvalī* = a string of round playthings (small toys for children, the Gujarati commentary glosses as small rounded clay or wooden balls) | *urakaḍayassa* = of the chest-region | *cittakaṭṭare* = the underside / interior of a bamboo basket (the *citta-kaṭara* — a basket-frame) | *vīyaṇapatte* = a fan made of bamboo strips | *tāliyaṃṭapatte* = a fan made of palm-leaf | *evāmeva jāva ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe* = "in just that way (and so on, by the standard refrain) — not at all flesh-and-blood"

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra is the great mid-section of the body-description, gathering together five regions of the torso — the waist, the belly, the ribs, the spine, and the chest — into one continuous portrait. Each member is given its own simile-cluster, and each cluster reaches further into household and street-life imagery. The choices are deliberate.

For the waist (*kaṭi-pradesha*) — the hip and pelvic basin — the comparison is to the foot of a camel, an old ox, or a buffalo: large-boned, leathery, hardened, with no trace of softness. The waist of a well-fed body is fleshy and curved; Dhanya's waist had become a hard, bony rim, like the wide-spread pad of a beast of burden whose foot has been pounded by years on stony roads.

For the belly (*udara*), the simile-set turns to vessels: the dried-up water-skin (a soft hollow that has been emptied and shrivelled), the iron pan used for parching gram (a flat, dry, scorched surface), the wooden trough used for kneading flour (a hollow, dry container). All three share one feature: emptiness held in a thin shell. Dhanya's belly was no longer a fed thing; it was a vessel, and the vessel was empty.

For the ribs (*pārshvaka* — the side-ribs), the imagery shifts to *rows*: a row of metal cups, a row of small bowls, a row of clay head-rings. The point is countability. A normal chest has its ribs hidden beneath flesh; Dhanya's ribs stood out so clearly that they could be counted one by one, like a line of vessels arranged on a shelf. This is one of the most quoted images in the chapter, and the Gujarati commentary lingers on it: *gani shakatī hatī* — "they could be counted."

For the spine (*priṣṭha-karaṇḍaka* — the "back-spool" or "back-basket"), the imagery sharpens still further: a string of beads, a string of small balls, a string of children's round playthings. Each vertebra had become a separate, visible bead on the long line of the spine. To run a finger down such a back is to feel each knot of bone discretely, with no muscle filling the spaces between.

For the chest (*ura-kaṭa* — the chest-frame), the comparison is gentlest of all: the underside of a bamboo basket, a fan of bamboo strips, a fan of palm-leaf. All three are open, light, with thin ribs holding a flat sheet — no thickness, no flesh, no weight. This is precisely how Dhanya's chest had become: a frame of bone like a bamboo basket, with skin stretched thinly across.

The Gujarati commentary makes a beautiful theological note here. It says: when the body is no longer being nourished by the rich foods that build flesh and blood, the body shrinks; *but the soul of the ascetic does not shrink — the soul becomes more powerful*. The very disappearance of the body's support is what allows the soul's strength to step forward. This sutra is read traditionally not as a record of wasting but as a record of the soul's emergence into visibility.

A small linguistic note: in this dense passage the canonical *jāva* abbreviation appears repeatedly, instructing the reader to fill in the standard refrain "and not at all flesh-and-blood." The translation expands these for the reader's benefit, but the text itself trusts the reader's familiarity with the rhythm of the description.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Dhanya's hips were hard like a camel's foot, his belly empty like a dried water-skin, his ribs so visible you could count them like cups in a row, his backbone like a string of beads, and his chest as thin and flat as a bamboo or palm-leaf fan — every part was just bone and skin, with no flesh or blood left.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When you are most empty — physically, emotionally, materially — what is the quality that remains, and is that perhaps the most truly *yours*?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S21 — Dhanya's Arms, Hands, and Fingers

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स बाहाणं इमेयारूवे तवरूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए समिसंगलिया इ वा बाहायासंगलिया इ वा, अगत्थियसंगलिया इ वा, एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स हत्थाणं इमेयारूवे तवरूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामय सुक्कच्छगणिया इ वा, वडपत्ते इ वा, पलासपत्ते इ वा, ए वामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स हत्थंगुलियाणं इमेयारूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए कलसंगलिया इ वा, मुग्गसंगलिया इ वा, माससंगलिया इ वा, तरुणिया छिण्णा आयवे दिण्णा सुक्का समाणी मिलायमाणी चिट्ठंति एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए । ॥3.A1.S21॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa bāhāṇaṃ imeyārūve tavarūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae samisaṃguliyā i vā bāhāyāsaṃguliyā i vā, agatthiyasaṃguliyā i vā, evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa hatthāṇaṃ imeyārūve tavarūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmaya sukkacchagaṇiyā i vā, vaḍapatte i vā, palāsapatte i vā, e vāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa hatthaṃguliyāṇaṃ imeyārūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae kalasaṃguliyā i vā, muggasaṃguliyā i vā, māsasaṃguliyā i vā, taruṇiyā chiṇṇā āyave diṇṇā sukkā samāṇī milāyamāṇī ciṭṭhaṃti evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe | || 3.A1.S21 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's arms had taken this form — just like the long dried pods of the *shami*-tree, of the *bāhāyāsa*-tree (a kind of *garmāla*-tree), or of the *agastya*-tree: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's hands had taken this form — just like a dry piece of cow-dung cake, a withered banyan leaf, or a withered *palasha*-leaf: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's hand-fingers had taken this form — just like the slender pods of *kala*, *mung*, or *urad*, plucked while still tender, laid in the sun, becoming dried and shrivelled, so they remain: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*bāhāṇaṃ* = of the (two) arms (*bāhā* — Prakrit feminine form derived from Sanskrit masculine *bāhu*; the Gujarati commentary explicitly notes that the *u*-ending of Sanskrit becomes *ā*-ending in Prakrit when treated as feminine, and so this Prakrit form is grammatically pure) | *samisaṃguliyā* = the long dried pod of the *shami*-tree | *bāhāyāsaṃguliyā* = the long dried pod of the *bāhāyāsa*-tree (commentary glosses this as a kind of *garmāla*-tree — possibly the Indian laburnum, *Cassia fistula* — though ⚠️ the precise modern identification of *bāhāyāsa* is uncertain; the commentary notes that even the great Tika-author Shri Abhayadeva Suri left it as "a particular tree") | *agatthiyasaṃguliyā* = the long dried pod of the *agastya*-tree (the *agastya* — sesbania-tree, *Sesbania grandiflora* — known for its long slender pods) | *hatthāṇaṃ* = of the (two) hands | *sukkacchagaṇiyā* = a dry piece of *chhāṇa* (cow-dung cake) | *vaḍapatte* = a withered banyan leaf | *palāsapatte* = a withered *palāsha* (flame-of-the-forest) leaf | *hatthaṃguliyāṇaṃ* = of the hand-fingers | *kalasaṃguliyā* = the slender pod of *kala*-legume | *muggasaṃguliyā* = the slender pod of *mung* | *māsasaṃguliyā* = the slender pod of *māṣa* (urad) | *taruṇiyā chiṇṇā* = plucked while tender | *āyave diṇṇā* = laid in the sun | *sukkā samāṇī milāyamāṇī ciṭṭhaṃti* = becoming dry and shrivelled, they remain so

**5. COMMENTARY**

The inventory now reaches the upper limbs. The arms are compared to long dried tree-pods — the *shami*, *bahayasa*, and *agastya* — all known in the canon's world for their long, narrow, hardened pods that hang from their branches once the seeds within have ripened. The arm of a fed body is rounded by muscle; Dhanya's arms had become like those long, dry, woody pods.

The hands are compared to three things that are flat, thin, and dried: a piece of cow-dung cake (the *chhāṇa* used as fuel in village kitchens, dried to a hard light disk), a withered banyan leaf, and a withered *palāsha*-leaf. All three are surface, with no inward substance. Dhanya's palms had become like that — a thin, dry sheath stretched over the bones beneath.

The fingers receive the same comparison the toes received earlier (Sutra 18) — the slender pods of *kala*, *mung*, and *urad*, plucked young and dried. The repetition is not laziness; it is precision. Toes and fingers, after all, share the same anatomy; the same simile is therefore exact for both. The Jain canonical method does not invent a new image where the right one already exists.

The Gujarati commentary spends a small note on the word *bāhā*. In Sanskrit, *bāhu* (arm) is masculine and ends in *u*. In Prakrit, when the same word is treated grammatically as feminine, the ending shifts to *ā*. So the Prakrit form *bāhā* (the *bāhāṇaṃ* of the genitive plural here) is not a corruption but a regular grammatical adaptation. This is the kind of careful linguistic note the editor preserves so that future students do not stumble on what looks like an irregularity.

A note of reverent honesty about *bāhāyāsa*: the Gujarati commentary openly acknowledges that the precise identification of this tree is difficult — even the great Tika-author Shri Abhayadeva Suri took it simply as "a particular tree-species," and modern usage suggests it may be the *garmāla* (Indian laburnum), but this is not certain. ⚠️ The translation here preserves the named original in the *anvayartha* and gives the most likely identification while noting the uncertainty. The general sense is unchanged: a tree with long slender pods, dried hard.

The Gujarati commentary closes this section with a memorable line: *Dhanya Anagara's arms had also become like the dried branches of a tree.* The simile is not invention but observation. Anyone who has seen the dried branch of a *shami* tree, fallen and bleached by the sun for many seasons, knows the shape: long, hard, knotted at the joints, hollow within. Dhanya's arms had become exactly so.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Dhanya's arms had become like the long dried seed-pods of certain trees; his hands looked like dried cow-dung cakes or withered leaves; his fingers were as shrivelled as green-gram pods left out in the sun — only bone and skin remained.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

If your hands no longer carried the shape of someone fed and rested by the world, would you still be able to use them — and what would they be moved to do?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S22 — Dhanya's Neck, Chin, Lips, and Tongue

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स गीवाए इमेयारूव लावण्णे होत्था से जहाणामए करगगीवा इ वा, कुंडियागीवा इ वा, उच्चट्टवणए इ वा एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स हणुयाए इमेयारूवे तवरूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए लाउयफले इ वा, हकुवफले इ वा, अंबगट्ठिआ इ वा, आयवे दिण्णा सुक्का समाणी मिलायमाणी चिट्ठंति, एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स उट्ठाणं इमेयारूवे तवरूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए सुक्कजलोया इ वा, सिलेसगुलिया इ वा, अलत्तगुलिया इ वा, एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए ।

धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स जिब्भाए इमेयारूवे तवरूव लावण्णे होत्था— से जहाणामए वडपत्ते इ वा, पलासपत्ते इ वा, सागपत्ते इ वा, एवामेव *जाव* णो चेव णं मंस सोणियत्ताए । ॥3.A1.S22॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa gīvāe imeyārūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae karagagīvā i vā, kuṃḍiyāgīvā i vā, uccaṭṭavaṇae i vā evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa haṇuyāe imeyārūve tavarūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae lāuyaphale i vā, hakuvaphale i vā, aṃbagaṭṭhiā i vā, āyave diṇṇā sukkā samāṇī milāyamāṇī ciṭṭhaṃti, evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa uṭṭhāṇaṃ imeyārūve tavarūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae sukkajaloyā i vā, silesaguliyā i vā, alattaguliyā i vā, evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe |

dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa jibbhāe imeyārūve tavarūva lāvaṇṇe hotthā— se jahāṇāmae vaḍapatte i vā, palāsapatte i vā, sāgapatte i vā, evāmeva *jāva* ṇo ceva ṇaṃ maṃsa soṇiyattāe | || 3.A1.S22 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's neck had taken this form — just like the long thin neck of a *karaka*-pot (the small water-pot of a monk), the neck of a small clay-pot, or the long-spouted neck of a (water-)flask: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's chin had taken this form — just like the dried fruit of the *lauya* (gourd) plant, the dried fruit of the *hakuva*-plant, or the dried stone of a mango (*āmra*), placed in the sun and become shrivelled, so they remain: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's lips had taken this form — just like a dried-up leech, a dried lump of *shleshma-gulika* (a kind of resin-pellet, a known plant-substance), or a dried pellet of *alaktaka* (the red lac-paste): in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood. The austerity-shaped beauty of the monk Dhanya's tongue had taken this form — just like a withered banyan leaf, a withered *palāsha*-leaf, or a withered teak (*sāka*) leaf: in just that way (and so on) — not at all flesh-and-blood.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*gīvāe* = of the neck | *karagagīvā* = the long thin neck of a *karaka* (the small water-pot used in monastic life — *kamandalu*-like, with a slim spout-neck) | *kuṃḍiyāgīvā* = the neck of a small clay-pot (*kuṇḍī*) | *uccaṭṭavaṇae* = the neck of a long-spouted water-flask (the *surāhi* — a tall pot with a slender neck used to pour water) | *haṇuyāe* = of the chin / lower jaw | *lāuyaphale* = the dried fruit of the *lauya* (a gourd-creeper) | *hakuvaphale* = the dried fruit of the *hakuva*-plant (⚠️ a plant-name whose precise identification is uncertain — the Gujarati commentary treats it as a particular gourd-like creeper) | *aṃbagaṭṭhiā* = the dried stone (kernel) of a mango | *āyave diṇṇā* = placed in the sun | *sukkā samāṇī milāyamāṇī ciṭṭhaṃti* = becoming dry and shrivelled, they remain so | *uṭṭhāṇaṃ* = of the (two) lips | *sukkajaloyā* = a dried-up leech | *silesaguliyā* = a dried pellet of *shleshma-gulika* (a hardened ball of plant-resin / mucilage; the Gujarati commentary clarifies that this is a known *vanaspati*-substance — a plant-resin pellet of a particular kind) | *alattaguliyā* = a dried pellet of *alaktaka* (the red lac-resin used as a cosmetic dye for women's feet) | *jibbhāe* = of the tongue | *vaḍapatte* = a withered banyan leaf | *palāsapatte* = a withered *palāsha* (flame-of-the-forest) leaf | *sāgapatte* = a withered teak leaf

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra brings the inventory to the head and face — neck, chin, lips, tongue — leaving only the topmost members (nose, eyes, ears, skull) for the next sutra. The choice of similes shifts again, drawing this time from the world of the household and the body's own daily objects.

The neck (*grīvā*) is compared to the necks of vessels: the *karaka* (the slender water-pot), the small clay *kuṇḍī*, and the long-spouted *surāhi*. All three vessels share one shape — a long, thin, narrow neck holding the body of the pot. Dhanya's neck had become like that: a long thin column of bone bearing the head, with no flesh to round it.

The chin (*hanu*) is compared to dried fruits and stones: a dried *lauya*-gourd, a dried *hakuva*-fruit, and the dried stone of a mango. The mango-stone image is particularly precise — the inner kernel of a mango, when the flesh has all been eaten and the stone has dried in the sun, has a shape and a stripped-down hardness that closely matches a fleshless human chin. The *Gujarati commentary* notes the parallel: just as in such fruits and stones the sun has carried away every drop of moisture, leaving only the hard inward form, so Dhanya's chin retained only its bony framework.

The lips (*oṣṭha*) carry a striking set of similes. The first is *sukka-jaloyā* — a dried-up leech. A leech, alive, is plump with the blood it draws; dead and dried, it shrivels into a thin, dark, curved line. The image is exact: lips that once were full and red were now thin, dark, and shrunken. The second is *shleshma-gulika* — a small hardened pellet of plant-resin, a known herbal substance of the time. The third is *alaktaka-gulika* — a dried pellet of the red lac-paste used as cosmetic dye. All three are small, dried, hardened forms of what was once moist, red, and supple. The Gujarati commentary draws out the gentleness of the contrast: *jain hoth pahelan bimbaphalanī samān lāl varṇanā hatā te tapanā kāraṇe sukāine bilkul phikkā thaī gayā hatā* — "the lips that had once been red as the *bimba*-fruit had now, by austerity, dried out and become entirely pale."

The tongue (*jihvā*) is compared to withered leaves: a banyan leaf, a *palāsha* leaf, a teak leaf. A leaf alive on the tree is moist and supple; once dried, it becomes brittle and curled. So too the tongue, no longer plumped by saliva and the daily exercise of speech and taste, had become like a dried leaf. This image gains its full weight from the earlier mention (Sutra 17) that even speaking exhausted Dhanya — the body had so withdrawn from the act of speech that the tongue itself was now closer to dried plant-matter than to living flesh.

The Vivechana of this section adds an essential theological note: the entire body-description of Dhanya is being given so that the reader may understand that genuine austerity is for *ātma-shuddhi* — purification of the soul — not for the display of bodily endurance. The commentary makes a sharp distinction: austerity is fruitful only when it is grounded in *samyak-jñāna* (right knowledge) and *samyak-darshana* (right insight). Without those two, even the most extreme physical austerity is *bāla-tapa* — "child-austerity" — which may lead to a heavenly birth, but cannot lead the soul to liberation. Dhanya's austerity is a true austerity precisely because it is rooted in his right understanding and devotion to the Tirthankara's path; it leads him not to a merely high heaven but to *Sarvārtha-Siddha* — the highest Anuttara abode — and from there to liberation.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Dhanya's neck was as long and thin as the spout of a water-pot; his chin was as shrunken as a dried mango-stone; his lips, once red and full, had turned dark and dried like a withered leech; his tongue had become as dry as a fallen leaf — every part, only bone and skin.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

If even the act of speaking became as costly as fasting, would the words you are most tempted to speak still seem worth the price?

---


---

### Dhanya's Final Aradhana and Ascent to Sarvarthasiddha

### SUTRA 3.A1.S23 — Dhanya's Inner Radiance Through Severe Tapas

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

धण्णे णं अणगारे सुक्केणं भुक्खेणं लुक्खेणं पायजंघोरुणा, विगयतडि-करालेणं कडिकडाहेणं, पिट्ठिमवस्सिएणं उदरभायणेणं जोइज्जमाणेहिं पासुलिकडएहिं, अक्खसुत्तमाला इव गणेज्जमाणेहिं पिट्ठिकरंडगसंधीहिं, गंगातरंगभूएणं उरकडग- देसभाएणं, सुक्कसप्पसमाणाहिं बाहाहिं, सिढिल-कडाली विव लंबंतेहि य अग्गहत्थेहिं, कंपणवाइओ वेवमाणीए विव सीसघडीए, पम्माणवयणकमले, उब्भडघडमुहेव उच्छुड्डणयणकोसे जीवंजीवेणं गच्छइ, जीवंजीवेण चिट्ठइ, भासं भासित्ता गिलाइ, भासं भासमाणे गिलाइ, भासं भासिस्सामि त्ति गिलाइ । से जहाणामए इंगालसगडिया इ वा एवं जहा खंदओ तहा जाव हुयासणे इव भासरासिपलिच्छण्णे तवेणं तेएणं तवतेयसिरीए अईव अईव उवसोभेमाणे उवसोभेमाणे चिट्ठइ । ॥3.A1.S23॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

dhaṇṇe ṇaṃ aṇagāre sukkeṇaṃ bhukkheṇaṃ lukkheṇaṃ pāyajaṃghoruṇā, vigayataḍi-karāleṇaṃ kaḍikaḍāheṇaṃ, piṭṭhimavassieṇaṃ udarabhāyaṇeṇaṃ joijjamāṇehiṃ pāsulikaḍaehiṃ, akkhasuttamālā iva gaṇejjamāṇehiṃ piṭṭhikaraṃḍagasaṃdhīhiṃ, gaṃgātaraṃgabhūeṇaṃ urakaḍaga-desabhāeṇaṃ, sukkasappasamāṇāhiṃ bāhāhiṃ, siḍhila-kaḍālī viva laṃbaṃtehi ya aggahatthehiṃ, kaṃpaṇavāio vevamāṇīe viva sīsaghaḍīe, pammāṇavayaṇakamale, ubbhaḍaghaḍamuheva ucchuḍḍaṇayaṇakose jīvaṃjīveṇaṃ gacchai, jīvaṃjīveṇa ciṭṭhai, bhāsaṃ bhāsittā gilāi, bhāsaṃ bhāsamāṇe gilāi, bhāsaṃ bhāsissāmi tti gilāi | se jahānāmae iṃgālasagaḍiyā i vā evaṃ jahā khaṃdao tahā jāva huyāsaṇe iva bhāsarāsipalicchaṇṇe taveṇaṃ teeṇaṃ tavateyasirīe aīva aīva uvasobhemāṇe uvasobhemāṇe ciṭṭhai | || 3.A1.S23 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

The venerable monk Dhanya, dried up by hunger and emaciation, had legs and thighs that were like sticks; his hipbones jutted out fearfully like a broken vessel; his belly clung to his backbone; his ribs could be counted one by one; the joints of his spine could be told apart like the beads of a rosary; his chest was furrowed like the waves of the Ganges; his arms were like dried serpents; his hands hung loose like the slack reins of a horse; his head trembled as if he were stricken with palsy; his face — once a blossomed lotus — had withered; his eyes had sunk deep into their sockets like the pits of a broken pot; he moved only by the strength of his soul, he stood only by the strength of his soul; he grew weary having spoken, weary while speaking, weary at the very thought "I shall speak"; like a cart of charcoal — and as was said of Skandaka, so here also — like a fire heaped over with ash, he stood resplendent with the lustre of his austerity, with the radiance and glory of his tapas, glowing more and more with every moment.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*dhaṇṇe ṇaṃ aṇagāre* = the venerable homeless monk Dhanya | *sukkeṇaṃ* = dried up | *bhukkheṇaṃ* = by hunger | *lukkheṇaṃ* = parched, withered | *pāyajaṃghoruṇā* = with feet, shins, and thighs (reduced to sticks) | *vigayataḍi-karāleṇaṃ kaḍikaḍāheṇaṃ* = with hipbones jutting out like a fearfully cracked or broken cauldron | *piṭṭhimavassieṇaṃ udarabhāyaṇeṇaṃ* = with belly clinging to (literally "taking refuge in") the back | *joijjamāṇehiṃ pāsulikaḍaehiṃ* = with ribs visible (countable) one by one | *akkhasuttamālā iva* = like a string of rosary-beads (*akṣasūtra-mālā*) | *gaṇejjamāṇehiṃ piṭṭhikaraṃḍagasaṃdhīhiṃ* = with the joints of the spinal column countable separately | *gaṃgātaraṃgabhūeṇaṃ urakaḍaga-desabhāeṇaṃ* = with the chest area furrowed like the waves of the Ganga | *sukkasappasamāṇāhiṃ bāhāhiṃ* = with arms like dried-up serpents | *siḍhila-kaḍālī viva laṃbaṃtehi ya aggahatthehiṃ* = with hands hanging loose like the slack reins of a horse | *kaṃpaṇavāio vevamāṇīe viva sīsaghaḍīe* = with the head-pot (skull) trembling as in palsy fever | *pammāṇavayaṇakamale* = with the lotus of his face withered | *ubbhaḍaghaḍamuheva ucchuḍḍaṇayaṇakose* = with eye-cavities sunken in like the broken mouth of a cracked pot | *jīvaṃjīveṇaṃ gacchai* = he moved only by the soul-force, by the strength of life-power | *jīvaṃjīveṇa ciṭṭhai* = he stood only by the soul-force | *bhāsaṃ bhāsittā gilāi* = after speaking, he grew weary | *bhāsaṃ bhāsamāṇe gilāi* = while speaking, he grew weary | *bhāsaṃ bhāsissāmi tti gilāi* = at the thought "I shall speak," he grew weary | *se jahānāmae iṃgālasagaḍiyā i vā* = just as a charcoal-cart (creaks under its load) | *jahā khaṃdao tahā* = as it was said of Skandaka, so here too | *jāva huyāsaṇe iva bhāsarāsipalicchaṇṇe* = up to "like a fire (huyāsaṇa) covered over with a heap of ash" | *taveṇaṃ teeṇaṃ tavateyasirīe* = with the tapas, with the lustre, with the glory-of-tapas-lustre | *aīva aīva uvasobhemāṇe uvasobhemāṇe ciṭṭhai* = he stood, beautified ever more, ever more

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra is one of the great body-descriptions of the canonical literature, written with a deliberate poetic intensity that the reader is meant to feel as much as understand. The compiler is showing us, in slow and unflinching detail, what the highest ascetic discipline does to a human body and what it does *not* do to the soul that inhabits it.

Each image is exact. The legs and thighs reduced to sticks; the hipbones thrust out as if a cauldron had cracked from inside; the belly fallen so far inward that it clings to the spine; the ribs no longer covered by flesh and so countable one by one; the spinal vertebrae visible like the beads of a rosary; the chest creased into ridges that look like waves on the Ganges; the arms shrivelled like dried snakes; the hands hanging weightless like a horse's slack reins; the head trembling as in fever; the face — once a blooming lotus — now collapsed inward; the eyes drawn so deep into their sockets that they look like the broken mouths of pots. The list does not stop until the reader has felt the weight of every joint of the body abandoned to spiritual fire.

And then, in the same breath, the text turns. Despite all of this — despite the body that can no longer be described except by drought and ruin — the monk *moves* and *stands*. By what? *Jīvaṃjīveṇaṃ* — "by the strength of soul alone." The body is no longer holding him up; the soul is. He cannot speak without weariness; he cannot even *think* of speaking without weariness; yet when he does speak, the speech of the soul comes through. His body, when it walks, creaks like a cart loaded with charcoal — the simile is the same one used in the Skandaka story of the Bhagavati Sutra, and the text makes the cross-reference explicit: *jahā khaṃdao tahā* — "as it was said of Skandaka, so here also."

The closing image is the most important. The body is described as *huyāsaṇa iva bhāsarāsi-palicchaṇṇe* — "like a fire covered over with a heap of ash." From the outside, only the ash is visible: the wasted, fragile body. But within that ash the fire is burning hotter than ever — burning karmas in great quantity, burning attachments, burning the very residues of selfhood. To the eye that sees only outwardly, this is a man dying. To the eye that sees inwardly, this is the highest spiritual incandescence the human form can hold. The sutra ends with the doubled phrase *aīva aīva uvasobhemāṇe uvasobhemāṇe ciṭṭhai* — "he stood, glowing more and more, glowing more and more" — the doubling itself a literary device to convey the unending growth of inner radiance even as the outer body fades.

The Gujarati commentary draws out the doctrinal point. Bodily strength and soul-strength are inversely related in the path of severe austerity; as one falls, the other rises. The seeker who is willing to let the body wither in service of the soul gains, by that very act, an inexhaustible inner fire. Dhanya's emaciation is not the end of his power but its visible sign.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

Dhanya the monk had become so thin from his austerities that you could count every rib, every joint of his spine, and his body trembled like a very sick person — yet he still glowed from within like a bright fire hidden under a heap of ash, more and more radiant with every moment.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

When you look at someone whose body has been worn thin by what they have given — do you see only the wasting, or do you also see the fire under the ash?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S24 — King Shrenika's Question and Mahavira's Reply

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तेणं कालेणं तेणं समएणं रायगिहे नयरे, गुणसिलए चेइए, सेणिए राया । तेणं कालेणं तेणं समएणं समणे भगवं महावीरे समोसढे । परिसा णिग्गया । धम्मकहा । परिसा पडिगया । तए णं से सेणिए राया समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अंतिए धम्मं सोच्चा निसम्म समणं भगवं महावीरं वंदइ नमंसइ, वंदित्ता नमंसित्ता एवं वयासी-

इमासिं णं भंते ! इंदभूइ पामोक्खाणं चोद्दसण्हं समणसाहस्सीणं कयरे अणगारे महादुक्करकारए चेव महानिज्जरतराए चेव ?

एवं खलु सेणिया ! इमासिं इंदभूइ पामोक्खाणं चोद्दसण्हं समणसाहस्सीणं धण्णे अणगारे महादुक्करकारए चेव महानिज्जरतराए चेव । ॥3.A1.S24॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

teṇaṃ kāleṇaṃ teṇaṃ samaeṇaṃ rāyagihe nayare, guṇasilae ceie, seṇie rāyā | teṇaṃ kāleṇaṃ teṇaṃ samaeṇaṃ samaṇe bhagavaṃ mahāvīre samosaḍhe | parisā ṇiggayā | dhammakahā | parisā paḍigayā | tae ṇaṃ se seṇie rāyā samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtie dhammaṃ soccā nisamma samaṇaṃ bhagavaṃ mahāvīraṃ vaṃdai namaṃsai, vaṃdittā namaṃsittā evaṃ vayāsī-

imāsiṃ ṇaṃ bhante ! iṃdabhūi pāmokkhāṇaṃ coddasaṇhaṃ samaṇasāhassīṇaṃ kayare aṇagāre mahādukkarakārae ceva mahānijjaratarāe ceva ?

evaṃ khalu seṇiyā ! imāsiṃ iṃdabhūi pāmokkhāṇaṃ coddasaṇhaṃ samaṇasāhassīṇaṃ dhaṇṇe aṇagāre mahādukkarakārae ceva mahānijjaratarāe ceva | || 3.A1.S24 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

In that age, at that time, in the city of Rajagriha, at the Gunashilaka sacred grove, there reigned King Shrenika. In that age, at that time, the venerable Sramana Lord Mahavira arrived in holy procession. The assembly came forth, the sermon was given, the assembly returned. Then King Shrenika, having heard the dharma in the presence of the Sramana Lord Mahavira and having reflected upon it, bowed and venerated the Sramana Lord Mahavira and, having bowed and venerated, spoke thus: "Reverend Sir! Of these fourteen thousand sramanas headed by Indrabhuti, which monk performs the most difficult austerities and which monk effects the greatest karmic destruction?" — "Truly so, Shrenika! Of these fourteen thousand sramanas headed by Indrabhuti, the venerable monk Dhanya is the one who performs the most difficult austerities and who effects the greatest karmic destruction."

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*teṇaṃ kāleṇaṃ teṇaṃ samaeṇaṃ* = in that age, at that time | *rāyagihe nayare* = in the city of Rajagriha | *guṇasilae ceie* = at the Gunashilaka sacred grove | *seṇie rāyā* = King Shrenika | *samaṇe bhagavaṃ mahāvīre* = the venerable Sramana Lord Mahavira | *samosaḍhe* = arrived (in holy assembly) | *parisā ṇiggayā* = the assembly came forth | *dhammakahā* = the dharma-discourse | *parisā paḍigayā* = the assembly returned | *dhammaṃ soccā nisamma* = having heard the dharma and reflected upon it | *vaṃdai namaṃsai* = bows and venerates | *evaṃ vayāsī* = spoke thus | *imāsiṃ* = of these | *iṃdabhūi pāmokkhāṇaṃ* = headed by Indrabhuti (the chief disciple, also known as Gautama Swami) | *coddasaṇhaṃ samaṇasāhassīṇaṃ* = of the fourteen thousand sramana monks | *kayare aṇagāre* = which homeless monk | *mahādukkarakārae ceva* = is the greatest performer of difficult austerities | *mahānijjaratarāe ceva* = and the one effecting the greatest karmic destruction (*nirjarā* = the shedding of karma) | *evaṃ khalu seṇiyā* = "Truly so, Shrenika" | *dhaṇṇe aṇagāre* = the venerable monk Dhanya | *mahādukkarakārae ceva mahānijjaratarāe ceva* = is indeed the greatest in difficult austerities and the greatest in karmic destruction

**5. COMMENTARY**

The scene shifts. The text moves from the body of Dhanya to the audience hall of King Shrenika. The setting is the same Rajagriha, the same Gunashilaka grove, but now the Lord Mahavira himself has arrived. The assembly comes, the dharma is spoken, the assembly disperses — and then, in that quiet space between the public discourse and the next moment, King Shrenika asks the question that opens the central teaching of this entire chapter.

His question is precise. Among the fourteen thousand monks under Mahavira's order — fourteen thousand, headed by Indrabhuti Gautama himself — *which* monk performs the most difficult austerities, and *which* monk thereby destroys the most karma? Notice that these are two distinct questions joined into one. *Mahā-dukkara-kāraka* — "the doer of great difficulty" — is about the outward severity of practice. *Mahā-nirjara-tara* — "the greater destroyer of karma" — is about the inward effectiveness. Shrenika is asking whether one monk excels in both at the same time. He wants to know who has reached the rarest of integrations: the body that can bear the most, and the soul that thereby sheds the most.

Mahavira's answer is direct, unhesitating, and singular. *Dhaṇṇe aṇagāre* — "the venerable monk Dhanya." Of fourteen thousand, one name. The Lord does not give the answer with qualification or caveat; he simply names him.

This is a startling moment in the canon. The Tirthankara himself — the omniscient teacher whose every word is the seal of liberated knowing — picks out one monk by name and declares him supreme in austerity and karma-destruction. To be named in this way by Mahavira is to be marked, in the canonical record, as one of the highest examples in his entire order. The reader who comes upon this line is meant to feel the weight of it: among fourteen thousand serious renunciates, Dhanya stood alone at the peak.

This sutra also tells us the teaching method of the *daśā* texts. The greatness of Dhanya is not asserted by the compiler in the third person; it is set up as a question by an outside questioner — the worldly king Shrenika — and answered by Mahavira himself. The reader hears it not as a chapter introduction but as a witness in the assembly hall. The narrative form is itself a kind of trustworthiness-marker: this is what was *said*, in that age, by the Lord, in answer to the king. Nothing has been added; nothing has been editorialized.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

When King Shrenika asked Lord Mahavira "Among your fourteen thousand monks, who has the toughest practice and destroys the most karma?", Mahavira answered with a single name: Dhanya.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

If a person who could see all souls clearly were asked which one in your community of practice burns the brightest — would your name be in their reply, or would you be content that someone else's is?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S25 — Mahavira's Reasoning: Why Dhanya is Foremost

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

से केणट्ठेणं भंते ! एवं वुच्चइ- इमासिं इंदभूइ पामोक्खाणं चोद्दसण्हं समणसाहस्सीणं धण्णे अणगारे महादुक्करकारए चेव महानिज्जरतराए चेव ?

एवं खलु सेणिया ! तेणं कालेणं तेणं समएणं काकंदी णामं नयरी जाव से धण्णे अणगारे उप्पिं पासायवरगए जाव पच्चणुब्भवमाणे विहरइ ।

तए णं अहं अण्णया कयाई पुव्वाणुपुव्वीए चरमाणे गामाणुगामं दूइज्जमाणे जेणेव काकंदी नयरी जेणेव सहसंबवणे उज्जाणे तेणेव उवागए । उवागमित्ता अहापडिरूवं उग्गहं उग्गिण्हामि संजमेणं तवसा अप्पाणं भावेमाणे विहरामि । परिसा निग्गया, तहेव जाव पव्वइए जाव बिलमिव पण्णग भूएणं अप्पाणेणं आहारं आहारेइ । धण्णस्स णं अणगारस्स पादाण आढत्तं सरीरवण्णओ सव्वो उच्चारेयव्वो जाव तव तेय सिरीए अईव अईव उवसोभेमाणे उवसोभेमाणे चिट्ठइ ।

से तेणट्ठेणं सेणिया ! एवं वुच्चइ इमासिं चउद्दसण्हं समणसाहस्सीणं धण्णे अणगारे महादुक्करकारए महानिज्जरतराए चेव । ॥3.A1.S25॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

se keṇaṭṭheṇaṃ bhante ! evaṃ vuccai- imāsiṃ iṃdabhūi pāmokkhāṇaṃ coddasaṇhaṃ samaṇasāhassīṇaṃ dhaṇṇe aṇagāre mahādukkarakārae ceva mahānijjaratarāe ceva ?

evaṃ khalu seṇiyā ! teṇaṃ kāleṇaṃ teṇaṃ samaeṇaṃ kākaṃdī ṇāmaṃ nayarī jāva se dhaṇṇe aṇagāre uppiṃ pāsāyavaragae jāva paccaṇubbhavamāṇe viharai |

tae ṇaṃ ahaṃ aṇṇayā kayāī puvvāṇupuvvīe caramāṇe gāmāṇugāmaṃ dūijjamāṇe jeṇeva kākaṃdī nayarī jeṇeva sahasaṃbavaṇe ujjāṇe teṇeva uvāgae | uvāgamittā ahāpaḍirūvaṃ uggahaṃ uggiṇhāmi saṃjameṇaṃ tavasā appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe viharāmi | parisā niggayā, taheva jāva pavvaie jāva bilamiva paṇṇaga bhūeṇaṃ appāṇeṇaṃ āhāraṃ āhārei | dhaṇṇassa ṇaṃ aṇagārassa pādāṇa āḍhattaṃ sarīravaṇṇao savvo uccāreyavvo jāva tava teya sirīe aīva aīva uvasobhemāṇe uvasobhemāṇe ciṭṭhai |

se teṇaṭṭheṇaṃ seṇiyā ! evaṃ vuccai imāsiṃ caudasaṇhaṃ samaṇasāhassīṇaṃ dhaṇṇe aṇagāre mahādukkarakārae mahānijjaratarāe ceva | || 3.A1.S25 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

"For what reason, Reverend Sir, do you say that, of these fourteen thousand sramanas headed by Indrabhuti, the venerable monk Dhanya is the greatest performer of difficult austerities and the greatest destroyer of karma?" — "Truly so, Shrenika! In that age, at that time, there was a city called Kakandi — and so on, up to — the venerable Dhanya was on the upper floor of his finest mansion, immersed in human pleasures. Then I, in due wandering order, going from village to village, came to the city of Kakandi, to the Sahasramravana garden. Coming there, I took up a fitting place of stay, abiding while purifying the soul through restraint and austerity. The assembly came forth — and so on, up to — he was ordained — and so on — and he eats his food disinterestedly, like a serpent that enters its hole without tasting. The full description of Dhanya's body, beginning with his feet, is to be repeated here, up to — he stands resplendent ever more and ever more with the lustre and glory of his austerity. For this reason, Shrenika, I declare that of these fourteen thousand sramanas the venerable Dhanya is the greatest performer of difficult austerities and the greatest destroyer of karma."

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*se keṇaṭṭheṇaṃ bhante* = for what reason, Reverend Sir | *evaṃ vuccai* = is it said thus | *evaṃ khalu seṇiyā* = "Truly so, Shrenika" | *kākaṃdī ṇāmaṃ nayarī* = a city named Kakandi | *jāva* = and so on (canonical compression marker) | *uppiṃ pāsāyavaragae* = on the upper floor of the finest mansion | *paccaṇubbhavamāṇe viharai* = he dwelt enjoying (sensual pleasures) | *tae ṇaṃ ahaṃ* = then I | *aṇṇayā kayāī* = at some time | *puvvāṇupuvvīe caramāṇe* = wandering in due order | *gāmāṇugāmaṃ dūijjamāṇe* = travelling from village to village | *jeṇeva kākaṃdī nayarī* = to where the city of Kakandi was | *jeṇeva sahasaṃbavaṇe ujjāṇe* = to where the Sahasramravana garden was | *teṇeva uvāgae* = there I came | *ahāpaḍirūvaṃ uggahaṃ uggiṇhāmi* = I took a fitting and proper place of residence (for ascetics) | *saṃjameṇaṃ tavasā* = with restraint and austerity | *appāṇaṃ bhāvemāṇe* = while purifying / cultivating the soul | *parisā niggayā* = the assembly came forth | *taheva* = thus, in the same way (as before) | *pavvaie* = ordained | *bilamiva paṇṇaga bhūeṇaṃ appāṇeṇaṃ* = with a soul like a serpent entering its hole (i.e., taking food without taste-attachment, swallowing it whole as a snake swallows its prey) | *āhāraṃ āhārei* = he takes his food | *pādāṇa āḍhattaṃ sarīravaṇṇao savvo uccāreyavvo* = the entire bodily description, beginning with the feet, is to be repeated (here) | *tava teya sirīe* = with the lustre and glory of austerity | *aīva aīva uvasobhemāṇe uvasobhemāṇe ciṭṭhai* = he stands, glowing more and more | *se teṇaṭṭheṇaṃ* = for that reason

**5. COMMENTARY**

Shrenika, having heard Mahavira's verdict, asks the natural follow-up: *on what ground* do you say so? It is the question of a king who has been trained to weigh evidence — not a doubting question, but a respectful request for the reasoning behind the judgment. And Mahavira responds by giving the ground: he tells, in compressed form, the entire arc of Dhanya's life from luxury to liberation-bound austerity.

The summary moves through every stage. *In Kakandi city, Dhanya was on the upper floor of his finest mansion enjoying every human pleasure.* This is the starting point — total worldly fulfillment, every comfort attained. *Then I, in my customary wandering, came from village to village to Kakandi and to the Sahasramravana garden.* This is the trigger — the arrival of Mahavira at the edge of Dhanya's city. *The assembly came; I gave the discourse; in the same manner he was ordained.* This compresses, by the canonical *jāva*, the entire account already given in earlier sutras of part A — Dhanya's mother Bhadra Sarthavahi, his five wet-nurses, his thirty-two-bride wedding, his renunciation, the four severe vows he took on the day of his ordination.

*Bilamiva paṇṇaga bhūeṇaṃ appāṇeṇaṃ āhāraṃ āhārei* — "he eats his food with a soul that is like a serpent entering its hole." This is one of the most striking ascetic similes of the entire canon. A serpent, when it enters its hole, does not taste the path it slides over; it goes straight, neither pausing nor savoring. So a monk of the highest discipline takes food without tasting it, without discrimination of like and dislike, without lingering on the tongue or in the mind. The food is fuel, not pleasure; it is consumed for the body's bare maintenance, not for any sensory satisfaction. This is the technical mark of the highest grade of dietary self-control, and Dhanya practiced it from the day of his ordination.

The Lord then gives the second instruction: *pādāṇa āḍhattaṃ sarīravaṇṇao savvo uccāreyavvo* — "the entire body-description, beginning from the feet, is to be repeated here." This is a textual instruction to the redactor and the reciter: when this sutra is recited, the long body-description of the previous sutra (S23) is to be inserted in full, not merely referenced. Mahavira himself, before King Shrenika, recounts the wasted body of Dhanya in every detail — the legs like sticks, the spine like a rosary, the eyes sunken into their sockets, the soul-strength alone keeping him upright, the fire glowing under the ash. *That body* is the evidence. *That body* is the proof of the answer.

The closure — *se teṇaṭṭheṇaṃ* "for that reason" — completes the syllogism. The most difficult austerity has been performed; the most karma is therefore being destroyed; therefore Dhanya is the highest. The reasoning is short, but it rests on a body and a life that have just been described in painstaking detail.

What the Lord is teaching King Shrenika here, and through him every reader, is that spiritual judgments are not made in the abstract. They are made by *looking at a life* — what was renounced, how completely, with what discipline of body and mind. The verdict on Dhanya is the verdict of an omniscient eye that has watched every minute of his ascetic life and seen what burned in it.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

When the king asked "Why?", Lord Mahavira gave the full reason: he retold Dhanya's whole story — from rich young man in luxury, to ordained monk, to one who eats food without tasting it, to a body so wasted that only his soul holds it up — and then said: *that* is why he is the greatest.

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

If your life had to stand as the answer to the question "why does this person practice well?" — what evidence would your last year offer?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S26 — King Shrenika's Veneration of Dhanya

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं से सेणिए राया समणस्स भगवओ महावीरस्स अंतिए एयमट्ठं सोच्चा निसम्म हट्ठ तुट्ठे समणं भगवं महावीरं तिक्खुत्तो आयाहिणं पयाहिणं करेइ, करित्ता, वंदइ नमंसइ वंदित्ता णमंसित्ता जेणेव धण्णे अणगारे तेणेव उवागच्छइ उवागच्छित्ता धण्णं अणगारं तिक्खुत्तो आयाहिणं पायाहिणं करेइ करित्ता वंदइ नमंसइ वंदित्ता नमंसित्ता एवं वयासी-

धण्णे सि णं तुम देवाणुप्पिया ! सुपुण्णे सि णं तुम देवाणुप्पिया, सुकयत्थे सि णं तुम देवाणुप्पिया, कयलक्खणे सि णं तुम देवाणुप्पिया, सुलद्धे णं देवाणुप्पिया तव माणुस्सए जम्मजीवियफले त्ति कट्टु वंदइ नमंसइ, वंदित्ता नमंसित्ता जेणेव समणे भगवं महावीरे तेणेव उवागच्छइ उवागच्छित्ता समणं भगवं महावीरं तिक्खुत्तो आयाहिणं पयाहिणं करेइ करेत्ता वंदइ नमंसइ वंदित्ता नमंसित्ता जामेव दिसं पाउब्भूए, तामेव दिसं पडिगए । ॥3.A1.S26॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ se seṇie rāyā samaṇassa bhagavao mahāvīrassa aṃtie eyamaṭṭhaṃ soccā nisamma haṭṭha tuṭṭhe samaṇaṃ bhagavaṃ mahāvīraṃ tikkhutto āyāhiṇaṃ payāhiṇaṃ karei, karittā, vaṃdai namaṃsai vaṃdittā ṇamaṃsittā jeṇeva dhaṇṇe aṇagāre teṇeva uvāgacchai uvāgacchittā dhaṇṇaṃ aṇagāraṃ tikkhutto āyāhiṇaṃ pāyāhiṇaṃ karei karittā vaṃdai namaṃsai vaṃdittā namaṃsittā evaṃ vayāsī-

dhaṇṇe si ṇaṃ tuma devāṇuppiyā ! supuṇṇe si ṇaṃ tuma devāṇuppiyā, sukayatthe si ṇaṃ tuma devāṇuppiyā, kayalakkhaṇe si ṇaṃ tuma devāṇuppiyā, suladdhe ṇaṃ devāṇuppiyā tava māṇussae jammajīviyaphale tti kaṭṭu vaṃdai namaṃsai, vaṃdittā namaṃsittā jeṇeva samaṇe bhagavaṃ mahāvīre teṇeva uvāgacchai uvāgacchittā samaṇaṃ bhagavaṃ mahāvīraṃ tikkhutto āyāhiṇaṃ payāhiṇaṃ karei karettā vaṃdai namaṃsai vaṃdittā namaṃsittā jāmeva disaṃ pāubbhūe, tāmeva disaṃ paḍigae | || 3.A1.S26 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then King Shrenika, having heard and reflected upon this teaching from the Sramana Lord Mahavira, became delighted and overjoyed; he circumambulated the Sramana Lord Mahavira three times to the right, and bowed and venerated him; having bowed and venerated, he came to where the venerable monk Dhanya was. Coming there, he circumambulated the venerable monk Dhanya three times to the right, bowed and venerated him, and having bowed and venerated, spoke thus: "Beloved-of-the-gods! Blessed are you, full of merit are you, well-accomplished are you, marked with auspicious signs are you; well-attained, beloved-of-the-gods, is the fruit of your human birth and human life!" — and saying this he again bowed and venerated him, and having done so returned to the Sramana Lord Mahavira, circumambulated him three times to the right, bowed and venerated him, and then departed in the very direction from which he had come.

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ* = then | *eyamaṭṭhaṃ soccā nisamma* = having heard and reflected upon this teaching | *haṭṭha tuṭṭhe* = delighted and overjoyed (a doubled phrase of joy) | *tikkhutto āyāhiṇaṃ payāhiṇaṃ karei* = he circumambulates three times to the right (the auspicious direction, keeping the venerable being on one's right shoulder) | *vaṃdai namaṃsai* = he bows (with body) and venerates (with mind) | *jeṇeva ... teṇeva uvāgacchai* = goes to where (Dhanya) was | *evaṃ vayāsī* = spoke thus | *dhaṇṇe si ṇaṃ tuma* = "blessed are you" — *dhanya* here is the common adjective ("fortunate, blessed"), playing on Dhanya's own name | *devāṇuppiyā* = O beloved-of-the-gods (the standard reverent address) | *supuṇṇe si* = full of merit are you | *sukayatthe si* = well-accomplished are you (one whose purpose has been well achieved) | *kayalakkhaṇe si* = marked with auspicious signs are you | *suladdhe* = well-attained, well-grasped | *tava māṇussae jammajīviyaphale* = is the fruit of your human birth and human life | *tti kaṭṭu* = saying this | *jāmeva disaṃ pāubbhūe* = in whichever direction he had appeared (i.e., come from) | *tāmeva disaṃ paḍigae* = in that very direction he returned

**5. COMMENTARY**

This sutra is small in word-count but immense in implication. The reigning king of Magadha — the most powerful man in the eastern subcontinent — having received Mahavira's verdict, walks to the wasted body of a single monk and circles him three times in reverence. He does not send a messenger. He does not invite Dhanya to court. He himself, in person, with his own feet, walks the distance, performs the threefold circumambulation, bows, and praises.

The five-word salutation he speaks is the high formula of Jain praise. *Dhaṇṇe si* — "blessed are you" — playing on Dhanya's own name; *supuṇṇe si* — "full of merit"; *sukayatthe si* — "your purpose is well-accomplished"; *kayalakkhaṇe si* — "you bear the marks of attainment"; *suladdhe tava māṇussae jammajīviyaphale* — "the fruit of your human birth and your very being-alive has been won by you." Each clause moves up a level. The first praises Dhanya as fortunate; the second as meritorious; the third as one whose life-purpose has been achieved; the fourth as one whose body itself bears the visible marks of that achievement; the fifth as one for whom the entire purpose of being born human has now been *grasped* and *won*.

In Jain doctrine, the human birth is the rarest of all birth-states because it is the only birth from which liberation can be reached directly. To be reborn human is to receive a chance that even the gods do not have. Therefore *jamma-jīvita-phala* — "the fruit of human birth, the fruit of being alive" — is the highest possible compliment: it means that the precious opportunity of this human life has been used for what it was given. Most beings squander their human births in pursuit of comfort, status, and pleasure; this one, the king is saying, has *not*.

The detail that the king, after praising Dhanya, returns to Mahavira and bows again before leaving, is significant. The order of veneration is preserved: Tirthankara first and last, monk in the middle. The king does not let his enthusiasm for the disciple displace the reverence due to the master. This is the canonical training in graded reverence — every great soul is honored, but in the order of the spiritual hierarchy, never out of it.

The Gujarati commentary on this sutra makes the following theological point. When a Tirthankara himself praises a disciple in front of others, the disciple's spiritual standing is sealed; this is the highest possible attestation. And from such an episode, two lessons follow: first, that praising the virtuous is itself an act of merit and should be done openly, without hesitation; second, that the praised one's example becomes a source of inspiration for the rest of the community. Dhanya here is not just praised in his own person — he is offered to the reader as the visible standard.

**6. SIMPLY PUT**

After hearing Mahavira's words, King Shrenika himself walked over to Dhanya, circled him three times in reverence, and praised him in the highest words: "Blessed are you, your life has not been wasted; you have made the most of being born human."

**7. CONTEMPLATE**

If a king were to walk to your door tomorrow to honor what you have done with this life — what would they actually find that you have made of it?

---

### SUTRA 3.A1.S27 — Dhanya's Final Aradhana, Sallekhana, and Ascent to Sarvarthasiddha

**1. PRAKRIT TEXT**

तए णं तस्स धण्णस्स अणगारस्स अण्णया कयाइ पुव्वरत्तावरत्तकालसमयंसि धम्मजागरियं जागरमाणस्स इमेयारूवे अज्झत्थिए जाव समुप्पज्जित्था-

एवं खलु अहं इमेणं उरालेणं तवोकम्मेणं किसे धमणिसंतए जाए एवं जहा खंदओ तहेव चिंता । आपुच्छणं । थेरेहिं सद्धिं विउलं पव्वयं दुरुहइ । मासिया संलेहणा । णवमासा परियाओ जाव कालमासे कालं किच्चा उड्ढं चंदिम सूर-गहगण-णक्खत्त-तारारूवाणं जाव णवगेवेज्जे विमाण पत्थडे उड्ढं दूरं वीईवइत्ता सव्वट्टुसिद्धे विमाणे देवत्ताए उववण्णे ।

थेरा तहेव ओयरंति जाव इमे से आयारभंडए । ॥3.A1.S27॥

**2. ROMAN TRANSLITERATION**

tae ṇaṃ tassa dhaṇṇassa aṇagārassa aṇṇayā kayāi puvvarattāvarattakālasamayaṃsi dhammajāgariyaṃ jāgaramāṇassa imeyārūve ajjhatthie jāva samuppajjitthā-

evaṃ khalu ahaṃ imeṇaṃ urāleṇaṃ tavokammeṇaṃ kise dhamaṇisaṃtae jāe evaṃ jahā khaṃdao taheva ciṃtā | āpucchaṇaṃ | therehiṃ saddhiṃ viulaṃ pavvayaṃ duruhai | māsiyā saṃlehaṇā | ṇavamāsā pariyāo jāva kālamāse kālaṃ kiccā uḍḍhaṃ caṃdima sūra-gahagaṇa-ṇakkhatta-tārārūvāṇaṃ jāva ṇavageveijje vimāṇa patthaḍe uḍḍhaṃ dūraṃ vīīvaittā savvaṭṭhasiddhe vimāṇe devattāe uvavaṇṇe |

therā taheva oyaraṃti jāva ime se āyārabhaṃḍae | || 3.A1.S27 ||

**3. LITERAL ENGLISH TRANSLATION**

Then once, in the late hours of the night, while the venerable monk Dhanya was keeping the dharma-vigil, this kind of inward reflection arose in him: "Indeed I, by this severe austerity, have become so wasted that my veins stand out — and so on, just as Skandaka thought, so he too thought." He took leave of his teachers; with the elder monks he ascended the great Vipula mountain. There he undertook a one-month sallekhana. With nine months of total ascetic tenure — and so on, up to — at the time of his final hour he made his end, and rising upward past the moon, the sun, the planet-hosts, the constellations, and the star-forms, and so on, past the nine Graiveyaka heaven-tiers, having crossed upward and far, was reborn as a divine being in the Sarvarthasiddha heaven. The elder monks descended in the same manner — and so on, up to — saying, "These are his ascetic implements."

**4. ANVAYARTHA**

*tae ṇaṃ* = then | *aṇṇayā kayāi* = once, at some time | *puvvarattāvarattakālasamayaṃsi* = at the late-night hour, the after-midnight watch (literally "the time when the first half of the night has gone and the latter half has begun") | *dhammajāgariyaṃ jāgaramāṇassa* = while keeping the dharma-vigil (the practice of staying awake to reflect on the soul) | *imeyārūve ajjhatthie samuppajjitthā* = this kind of inward reflection arose | *evaṃ khalu ahaṃ* = "indeed I" | *imeṇaṃ urāleṇaṃ tavokammeṇaṃ* = by this severe austerity-practice | *kise dhamaṇisaṃtae jāe* = have become emaciated, with veins protruding | *jahā khaṃdao taheva ciṃtā* = the reflection is the same as Skandaka's | *āpucchaṇaṃ* = he asks permission (of his teacher Mahavira) | *therehiṃ saddhiṃ* = together with the elder monks | *viulaṃ pavvayaṃ duruhai* = he ascends the great Vipula mountain | *māsiyā saṃlehaṇā* = a one-month sallekhana (final ascetic fast) | *ṇavamāsā pariyāo* = nine months of monastic tenure | *kālamāse kālaṃ kiccā* = at the appointed time, having performed the final act of dying | *uḍḍhaṃ* = upward | *caṃdima sūra-gahagaṇa-ṇakkhatta-tārārūvāṇaṃ* = past the moon, the sun, the multitude of planets, the constellations, and the star-forms | *ṇavageveijje vimāṇa patthaḍe* = past the tiers of the nine Graiveyaka heavens | *uḍḍhaṃ dūraṃ vīīvaittā* = having crossed upward and far | *savvaṭṭhasiddhe vimāṇe* = in the Sarvarthasiddha celestial mansion (the supreme of the five Anuttara heavens) | *devattāe uvavaṇṇe* = was reborn as a divine being | *therā taheva oyaraṃti* = the elder monks descend in the same manner | *ime se āyārabhaṃḍae* = "these are his ascetic implements (robes, alms-bowl, water-pot)"

**5. COMMENTARY**

This is the climactic sutra of Adhyayana 1, the moment toward which the entire chapter has been moving. Every detail of Dhanya's earlier life — his renunciation, his fierce vow, his daily practice of one alms-piece, his fasts — has been preparing him for this single hour, and the text presents that hour with extraordinary economy.

The setting is the late-night watch — *puvva-rāttā-varatta-kāla-samaya*, the hour after midnight, when the world is most still. Dhanya is awake, keeping the *dharma-jāgariyā* — the spiritual vigil in which a monk reviews his own life, examines his own state, and contemplates the path. In that stillness, an inward reflection arises. The text does not give it to us in fresh words; it tells us, by canonical formula, that the reflection is the same as the one that arose in the great monk Skandaka — recorded in the Bhagavati Sutra, second shataka. That reflection runs roughly: "I have practiced severe austerity; my body has become wasted; my veins protrude; my spiritual zeal still burns; my teacher Mahavira is still present in this earth; I should make use of every advantage and complete my final practice now."

In Jain doctrine, this is the moment of *antima ārādhana* — the final spiritual settling. The seeker, recognizing that the body cannot serve the soul much longer, deliberately turns the body's last days into the soul's most concentrated practice. Dhanya rises at dawn, asks Mahavira's permission, and with the elder monks ascends the great Vipula mountain — a known site of ascetic dying near Rajagriha — and undertakes there the one-month *sallekhana*. The Gujarati *vivechan* gives the details that the canonical compression hides: he laid out a grass-mat on a black-stone slab, sat in lotus posture, joined his palms, faced east, performed the *Namotthuṇaṃ* salutation to all the liberated ones, then to his teacher Mahavira; he then formally renounced — for life — the four kinds of food (solid, liquid, chewed, and tasted), renounced the body itself in its final act of attachment, and took up *pādapopagamana santhāra* — the highest grade of sallekhana, the "fallen-tree" posture in which the monk lies motionless, accepting no service of any kind, until death. He practiced sallekhana for thirty days, in which the *ṣaṣṭhi-bhakta* count of meals (sixty meals over thirty days, two per day) was renounced. He then died in *uttama samādhi-maraṇa* — the supreme equipoise-death.

The result of that death is then described in the cosmic vertical. The soul, freed at the moment of dying from the wasted body, rises *upward* — the Jain cosmos is a strict vertical structure, and the meritorious soul ascends literally. It passes the realms of the moon, the sun, the planet-hosts, the constellations, and the stars (all of which are inhabited by their own classes of celestial beings); then through the *kalpa* heavens (Saudharma, Iśāna, Sanatkumāra, Māhendra, Brahma, Lāntaka, Mahāśukra, Sahasrāra, Ānata, Prāṇata, Āraṇa, Acyuta — the *jāva* compresses these); then through the nine *Graiveyaka* heavens — the "neck-tier" realms above all the *kalpa* worlds; and at last, past even the four sub-Anuttara heavens (Vijaya, Vaijayanta, Jayanta, Aparajita), it settles into *Sarvarthasiddha-vimāna* — "the celestial mansion where every desire is accomplished."

Sarvarthasiddha is the highest single realm of embodied existence in the entire Jain cosmos. It is the supreme of the five Anuttara heavens — the "heavens beyond which there is no higher" — and within them, it is the topmost. Its name is exact: *sarva-artha-siddha*, "in which all purposes are achieved." A soul reborn there is one in whom every spiritual and bodily aim is automatically fulfilled; there is no want, no striving, no incompletion. Its inhabitants live for thirty-three *sāgaropama* (a vast cosmological time-unit), and they take only one further birth — invariably as a human in the *Mahāvideha* region, where Tirthankaras are eternally present — and then attain final liberation. To be reborn in Sarvarthasiddha is therefore to be one human birth from the end of all suffering.

The closing detail — *therā taheva oyaraṃti jāva ime se āyārabhaṃḍae* — "the elder monks descend, and say 'these are his ascetic implements'" — is the last earthly note.
